ALRC Reference: ALRC DRP 2
is a draft recommendations paper?
This paper sets out the Commission's draft recommendations.
It also identifies areas where the Commission is seeking
further information.
Unlike a discussion paper or a final report, this paper
does not contain detailed summaries of the research
and submissions. It
is a working paper designed to
indicate the preliminary views of the Commission and
to form the basis for further consultations.
The draft recommendations have been developed in light
of the submissions the Commission has received on Issues
Paper 16, the
comments made at public hearings and
consultations and the Commission's own research. The
Commission's review process is described
in more detail
in chapter 1.
The Commission has not formed any final views on the
matters raised in this paper. The Commission will be
settling its final recommendations
in light of the
responses to this paper and of the submissions, evidence
and other material it has obtained in the course of
the review. The Commission's final report is due by
30 September 1996.
The Commission seeks the views of all interested persons
and organisations on the draft recommendations contained
in this paper
and on any matters that may not have
been addressed.
Submissions invited on current inquiries
Draft Recommendations
against the AFP and NCA
How to make comments and submissions
You are invited
to make comments and submissions on the draft recommendations
in this Paper.
Written comments and submissions
should be sent to
Australian Law Reform Commission
Closing date:
9 September 1996
It would be helpful if comments addressed specific
draft recommendations or paragraphs in the Paper.
Confidentiality
Unless a submission is marked confidential, it will
be made available to any person or organisation on
If you want your submission, or any part of it, to
be treated as confidential, please indicate this clearly.
A request for a submission
marked 'confidential' will
be determined in accordance with the
Freedom of Information
The Commission will include in its final report on
this project a list of submissions received in response
to this Paper. It may
also refer to those submissions
in the text of the report and other Commission publications.
It may decide to publish them. If
you do not want your
submission or any part of it to be used in any one
of these ways please indicate this clearly.

1. Introduction
AFP inquiry

NCA inquiry

These inquiries
1.1 In 1995 the
then federal Attorney-General, Michael Lavarch referred
to the Commission two related inquiries - one concerning
the complaints and disciplinary systems of the Australian
Federal Police (AFP) and the other the complaints and
disciplinary
systems of the National Crime Authority
(NCA). The Commission received the AFP reference on
29 March 1995 and the NCA reference
on 26 July 1995.
The terms of the references are set out in Appendix
The AFP inquiry
1.2 The Commission
is asked to report on the
Complaints (Australian Federal
Police) Act
1981 (the Complaints Act) and the AFP's
complaints and disciplinary system in their entirety.
The Complaints Act
provides for the complaints process
while the disciplinary process is provided for under
the AFP Act and its regulations and
the Complaints
Act. The Commission is to consider whether the legislation
meets the expectations of the public in terms of police
accountability, effectiveness and efficiency.
to the AFP inquiry
1.3 This is the
first extensive review of the legislation. It arises
because of a concern to ensure that the systems for
and discipline of the AFP are appropriate,
fair and effective for the 1990s and the future.
The NCA inquiry
.4 The terms of
the NCA inquiry are similar to those for the AFP. The
Commission is required to inquire into and report on
for the investigation and resolution of
complaints against the NCA and staff members of the
NCA or police officers seconded to
the NCA. It is asked
to consider whether the system meets the expectations
of the public in terms of accountability, effectiveness
and efficiency. In doing this the Commission is to
have regard to its reference on the AFP and to carry
out the two references
in conjunction.
1.5 There is currently
no legislatively based complaints system for the NCA,
its staff or those seconded to it. Instead there
an internal administrative arrangement for complaints
to be forwarded immediately to the Chairperson of the
NCA. The Chairperson
then directs officers of the NCA
to conduct inquiries and to compile reports. The Chairperson
considers these reports and decides
upon appropriate
1.6 The current
lack of any formal external review is widely regarded
as highly undesirable.
The major issues have been who should provide the
review and to which groups of the NCA's mixed workforce
should it apply. The Commission discusses the implications
for the efficiency and accountability of the current
mixed employment
regime in chapter 4. The Commission
suggests in that chapter that consideration should
be given to the NCA having a single employment
There have been two major attempts to introduce legislation
to introduce a formal complaints system but they have
unsuccessful.
1.7 There is also
no formal NCA disciplinary system except for those
NCA staff who are employed under the
Public Service
(Cth) and who are subject to the Australian
Public Service disciplinary procedures.
In their case the Chairperson of the NCA has all the
powers of a Permanent Secretary.
systems and disciplinary systems
1.8 This paper distinguishes
between complaints systems and disciplinary systems.
1.9 Complaints systems
concern the lodging, processing and investigation of
complaints. Complaints may be made by people outside
the particular agency or by members of it. The key
element is the investigation stage, particularly who
conducts the investigation
and according to what processes.
The final stage of the investigation process is usually
the making of a report which contains
information relevant
to deciding whether there has been a breach of the
criminal law and/or a breach of a disciplinary rule.
1.10 Disciplinary
systems involve the application of internal rules adopted
by an agency
to ensure laws, instructions and procedures are complied
to maintain proper standards of conduct
to protect the integrity and reputation of the agency.
1.11 The disciplinary
process is often invoked after a complaint has been
investigated and there is evidence to suggest that
a disciplinary rule has been breached. The head of
the agency then makes a decision about whether a person
should be charged
with a disciplinary offence on the
basis of the investigation of the complaint. However,
disciplinary action might be considered
without any
formal complaint being lodged by any person.
Expectations
for the current AFP complaints and disciplinary systems
1.12 The current
AFP complaints and disciplinary systems are based upon
two earlier Commission reports.
In the second of those reports the Commission accepted
the need for an 'independent police complaints mechanism'.
The Ombudsman was expected to play a pivotal role
in this to ensure the integrity and independence of
investigation of complaints
against the police.
The Commission was of the view that
ex post facto review after an investigation and determination
is made would be an ineffectual response... that would
police and most citizens unsatisfied, indeed
cynical about the effectiveness of the independent
scrutiny provided.
1.13 The report
envisaged that the majority of investigations would
be carried out by the proposed police Internal Investigations
Unit but that in exceptional cases the Ombudsman could
conduct his or her own investigations where of the
opinion that this
option was in the public interest.
The Commission envisaged that the Internal Investigations
Unit would have a charter of rights
and duties, would
have internal and external educative functions and
undertake research directed at the maintenance and
improvement
of discipline codes and allied police education.
1.14 The Commission
also argued that while ensuring public confidence in
the integrity of the complaints system was essential,
there was also a need for the chief officer of the
police to have responsibility for the discipline of
the force including
the capacity to deal promptly,
but justly with members of the force.
1.15 The Commission
proposed the establishment of a separate and independent
tribunal to be the medium for the determination of
the more intractable and serious of the public complaints
as well as the appeal tribunal for most internal disciplinary
The tribunal could also be requested to hold a general
inquiry in the nature of a Royal Commission to permit
complicated and
involved questions connected with the
force to be resolved.
Expectations
1.16 It would be
reasonable to assume that the current systems 15 years
after the introduction of the AFP Complaints Act would
be able to achieve to at least a minimally satisfactory
level the broad aims identified by the Commission in
those earlier
reports. That is, an AFP complaints system
where the Ombudsman provides independent and effective
scrutiny of the complaints
process, a system in respect
of which the public have confidence and a process of
determining disciplinary outcomes that is timely,
and fair and gives to the Commissioner of the AFP the
capacity to discipline his or her force.
1.17 However, the
Commission's inquiry thus far has not demonstrated
that any of these basic objectives are being met to
acceptable level. The current systems do not deliver
sufficient independent scrutiny, accountability, credibility,
effectiveness
or timeliness. They lack coherence, clarity
and vigour. When these systems have responded to perceived
problems since the introduction
of the Complaints Act
they have done so on the basis of ad hoc responses
and expediency. Specific failings of the AFP complaints
systems are discussed in chapter 2 and chapter 6 deals
with problems with the AFP disciplinary system.
Commission's
draft recommendations
1.18 The Commission's
recommendations are designed to rectify these inadequacies
and in particular to ensure that the AFP and
NCA complaints
and disciplinary processes will meet those objectives
identified almost 20 years ago. There will be a clear
chain of accountability and transparency. There will
be a measured and appropriate response to the full
range of complaints and
other forms of information
objectives can best be achieved by a significant
from the current systems that have significantly failed
to achieve the desired objectives as demonstated in
1.19 The Commission
recommends the introduction of a National Integrity
and Investigations Commission (NIIC) to manage, oversee
or directly investigate complaints against the AFP
and the NCA. It would tailor its response to the nature
of the complaint,
particularly the level of seriousness.
It would also have primary responsibility for the detection
and investigation of corruption.
It would have ultimate
and clear responsibility to ensure that the basic objectives
of complaints systems are being met. The
would be relieved of any direct responsibility in the
complaints handling process.
1.20 The Commission
also recommends significant changes to the disciplinary
process to ensure that the AFP and NCA managements
are given primary responsibility for disciplining their
organisations but subject to administrative review
of disciplinary
decisions by the Administrative Appeals
Tribunal instead of the current review in the case
of the AFP provided by the Federal
Disciplinary Police
Tribunal which would be abolished.
The Commission's
review process
In mid 1995 the Commission sent a short summary of
its preliminary views on the major issues in these
inquiries to organisations
and individuals with a special
interest or expertise in this area. The Commission
received 29 submissions in response.
The Commission appointed 19 honorary consultants with
a wide range of experience and expertise who have met
with the Commission
to discuss major issues.
In November 1995 the Commission published and distributed
an Issues Paper
Under the spotlight. Complaints against
the AFP and the NCA
. The Commission has currently received
over fifty submissions in response.
Throughout the Inquiry but particularly in February,
March and April 1996 the Commission has consulted with
a number of organisations
with a special interest in
these references including the AFP, the NCA, State
and Territory police services and Ombudsmen, the
Criminal Justice Commission (CJC), the South Australian
Police Complaints Authority and the NSW Royal Commission
into the Police. During February and March 1996 the
Commission also held public meetings in the Australian
capital cities, Norfolk
Island and Jervis Bay.
1.21 This paper
is the next step in the development of the Commission's
final recommendations.
1.22 This paper
sets out the Commission's preliminary conclusions,
its draft recommendations and the reasons for them.
indicates where the Commission is seeking additional
information or comment. Responses to this paper will
assist the Commission
in preparing its final recommendations
and report.
of this paper
Chapter 2 assesses the current AFP and NCA complaints
Chapter 3 compares investigation models and discusses
the Commission's preferred option, the establishment
of a National Integrity
and Investigations Commission
Chapter 4 analyses special issues for an NCA complaints
Chapter 5 explains the suggested role and functions
of the NIIC.
Chapter 6 discusses current disciplinary systems and
suggests an alternative approach based on increased
managerial responsibility
and then the avenue of administrative
review by the Administrative Appeals Tribunal (AAT).
Chapter 7 concerns the role of the AFP, the NCA and
the NIIC in strategies and measures to prevent complaints,
fraud and corruption.
Chapter 8 considers the scope of the complaints systems
for the AFP and the NCA including the definition of
complaint and discretions
not to investigate.
Chapter 9 examines the processing of complaints including
their receipt and recording.
Chapter 10 discusses some special issues for the AFP
in its community policing role in the Australian Capital
Territory (ACT),
Jervis Bay and the external territories.
Chapter 11 examines some personnel in the AFP and the
NCA who may have special needs in relation to complaints
systems and groups
external to these agencies who also
may have special needs.
Chapter 12 deals with some police powers and responsibilities
that are connected to complaints and disciplinary systems,
the obligation of AFP personnel to provide
identifying details to members of the public upon request.

or organisations on
the draft recommendations in this paper and on any
other relevant issues.

Introduction
2.1 This chapter
has four parts
describing the respective roles of the AFP and the
identifying the major objectives of the AFP and NCA
complaints systems
outlining the current AFP and NCA complaints systems
assessing the record of the current approaches in the
light of the identified objectives.
2.2 The conclusion
is that the current records of the complaints systems
reveal significant inadequacies. Chapter 6 assesses
the current disciplinary systems and recommends that
the NCA have its own disciplinary proceedings.
the AFP and the NCA
2.3 The AFP is a
federal law enforcement agency which
investigates breaches or potential breaches of Commonwealth
laws on a national basis
liaises and co-operates with international and overseas
law enforcement agencies
provides community policing in the ACT and Jervis Bay
Territory and the following external territories -
Norfolk Island, Cocos
(Keeling) Islands and Christmas
provides services in relation to protecting VIPs and
2.4 The AFP's primary
national role is the conduct of investigations. In
that context it has similarities with the United States
Federal Bureau of Investigations (FBI). The AFP does
not have general jurisdiction in relation to State
and Northern Territory
laws. Generally it does not
have daily contact and interaction with the broad community
in a community policing role. The only
places where
it performs that role are in those territories mentioned
2.5 The NCA is a
national law enforcement body whose main mission is
to counteract organised crime often by working in partnership
with other agencies. The NCA's working definition of
organised crime is 'a systematic conspiracy to commit
serious offences'.
Generally, the NCA investigates
relevant criminal activities and collects, analyses
and disseminates information and intelligence
to those activities. Where appropriate it establishes
and co-ordinates task forces with other law enforcement
for the investigation of those matters. It may
also make recommendations for legal and administrative
2.6 The NCA uses
multi-disciplinary teams of lawyers, police, financial
investigators, intelligence analysts and support staff
to investigate organised crime. The Act gives the NCA
coercive powers to compel people to produce documents
and to give sworn
evidence. Those powers are not available
to traditional police services. The NCA can only exercise
its coercive powers in matters
which have been formally
referred to it for investigation. These characteristics
are meant to enable the NCA to co-ordinate national
investigations against major organised crime by complementing
the efforts of other law enforcement agencies and by
co-operatively with them.
Major objectives
2.7 The Commission
considers that the main objectives for the AFP and
NCA complaints systems are to:
ensure public confidence in these agencies and their
complaints systems
achieve proper accountability of the agencies and their
be fair to complainants and to AFP and NCA officers
including taking into account the requirements of natural
justice and privacy
considerations
be credible to the agencies and their officers
be accessible to the community
promote appropriate standards of conduct, ethics and
integrity from officers and their agencies
provide the appropriate response, particularly the
appropriate level of investigation, to the full range
of complaints, from minor
to serious, including those
involving corruption
provide feedback to the agency about individual officers,
agency practices, procedures and operations
take into account appropriately other agency priorities
such as law enforcement operations
be timely in process and outcome
use resources efficiently.
2.8 The Commission
does not propose to try to rate these objectives in
importance as a fully effective and integrated system
should endeavour to meet each of them. However, it
is clear that ensuring credibility with the public
and the agencies themselves
is a very significant objective.
The general public should be able to see how complaints
are dealt with and whether the outcomes
satisfy general
community standards and expectations. A failure to
ensure public confidence may lead to public mistrust
the police. This mistrust can taint the whole administration
of justice. It may deter people from reporting crimes,
from seeking
the assistance of the police or assisting
them, from giving evidence or appearing as witnesses.
Furthermore, this public mistrust
can spill over and
affect public perceptions of other aspects of administration
and government, sometimes leading to widespread
and distortion and providing sustenance to all-enveloping
conspiracy theories.
Articulating
these objectives
2.9 The Commission
considers that the major objectives of the complaints
systems should be included in the new legislation.
The objectives are likely to remain the same for the
foreseeable future and should therefore be enshrined
in legislation. This
will assist in the understanding
of the fundamental objectives of the legislation and
will be useful to those who use it. It
will also serve
an educative function. The objectives should also be
incorporated in AFP and NCA codes of conduct, management
goals and practices.
Draft recommendation
Articulating these objectives
Draft recommendation 2.1.
The objectives listed above
should be included in an objects clause in the new
legislation. They should also be incorporated
and NCA codes of conduct and corporate goals and practice.
The current
AFP complaints system
is a complaint?
2.10 There is no
definition of complaint in the Complaints Act but a
complaint must relate to 'action taken by an AFP appointee'.
That expression is defined as:
action that the appointee takes or purports to take:
(a) because of his or her being an AFP appointee; or
(b) in the exercise of powers, or the performance of
functions, given to him or her as an AFP appointee
by this Act or by another
2.11 The term 'AFP
appointee' includes members of the AFP ( that is, the
Commissioner, Deputy Commissioners, senior executive
commissioned police officers and commissioned and non-commissioned
police officers), staff members and special members
by the Commissioner.
Each of these is subject to the Complaints Act.
and allegations
2.12 Complaints
- complaints and allegations. Complaints by members
of the public against the AFP or its personnel are
made under the Complaints Act. Allegations are provided
for under subsidiary
AFP legislation
and deal with internal complaints, that is, by one
AFP appointee in respect of another and other complaints
concerning non-operational
police matters. This distinction
between complaints and allegations is further discussed
in chapter 8.
of complaints and allegations
2.13 During 1994-95
the AFP's Internal Investigations Division (IID) recorded
900 new complaints made by 596 complainants. Of
1101 complaints finalised by the Ombudsman, 70 or 6.4%
were substantiated and 41.1% were unsubstantiated.
Of the remainder
42.9% were reconciled, withdrawn or
otherwise did not require a full investigation.
2.14 There were
also 109 allegations made which represented a slight
decrease in allegations from the previous year.
2.15 Complaints
may be lodged with the Ombudsman or the AFP each of
whom is to record them and to notify the other of each
Conciliation
of some complaints
2.16 The Act provides
for a special procedure for the conciliation of minor
complaints.
The Commissioner may also make a decision that conciliation
should be attempted for those complaints which are
not dealt with
under the minor complaints procedure
but which are received either by the IID or by the
2.17 On 3 April
1995, a more detailed arrangement for the conciliation
of complaints was introduced in the ACT as a pilot
resolution scheme. It is based on an agreement
between the AFP and the Commonwealth Ombudsman and
allows sergeants and work-level
3's and above to resolve
non-serious complaints and non-serious allegations
internally through conciliation.These workplace resolutions
are reviewed by IID and the Ombudsman. The results
of workplace resolution may include counselling, apologies
and explanations.
investigations
2.18 The IID is
created by the Complaints Act.
Its functions are set out in the Act:
to investigate complaints concerning action taken by
AFP appointees that are referred to it by an AFP appointee
who has received
a complaint or by the Ombudsman;
to investigate special matters referred to it by the
Commissioner.
2.19 The IID has
power under the Complaints Act to
investigate complaints
further investigate complaints if this is requested
by the Ombudsman
compel answers and the provision of information.
2.20 The investigative
powers of the IID are limited to investigation of AFP
members only.
The IID investigates almost all complaints against
the police which are made under the Complaints Act
other than minor complaints,
complaints which the Ombudsman
decides to investigate herself, or the small number
which are referred to a special investigator
s 46. At the conclusion of its investigation the IID
must provide a report to the Ombudsman.
In addition to investigating complaints the IID investigates
most allegations (other than minor allegations) which
are made pursuant
to General Order 6.
2.21 Due to lack
of staffing, remote locations and cost effectiveness,
the IID refers approximately 80 complaints each year
to 'authorised officers'
who conduct an investigation under the supervision
of the officer-in-charge of IID.
2.22 The IID is
directly responsible to a Deputy Commissioner. Members
are appointed to the IID by the Deputy Commissioner
a period of two years.
Presently there are 19 members and 3 staff members
in the IID.
When transferring an officer to the IID the Commissioner
is required to have regard to the nature of the functions
and the need to ensure that there is a member
in the IID with experience in criminal investigations
and a member with experience
in general police duties.
Ombudsman's role in investigations
2.23 The primary
role undertaken by the Ombudsman under the Complaints
Act has been to review complaint investigations by
IID. In 1994-5, 276 complaints were investigated
by the IID, 8 by the Ombudsman and 2 by special investigation.
2.24 According
to the Ombudsman when she receives a complaint or is
notified by the AFP of a complaint, she writes to the
complainant
to explain her role. She then monitors
the progress of the investigation, following up with
the IID when there is undue delay.
Her office discusses
with the Director of IID any particular issues that
require attention. On receipt of the investigation
report the Ombudsman examines the evidence and identifies
any further action that is necessary. The Ombudsman
may request interim reports of the police investigation
may provide progress reports about complaints to complainants
may determine that a complaint should not be investigated
may conduct his/her own investigations following an
IID investigation.
In limited circumstances, when conducting her own investigation
the Ombudsman
has power to obtain information and documents and any
person who is summonsed to provide information does
not retain the right
has the power to enter premises of the AFP or a government
department or prescribed authority.
The Ombudsman has the power to conduct four types of
independent investigations.
Complaints about 'practices and procedures' referring
to complaints that are about administrative matters,
and are very similar
to the administrative investigations
conducted under the
Ombudsman Act
Special investigations, conducted after agreement between
the Ombudsman and the Commissioner in cases where it
is not appropriate
for the IID to investigate. These
are cases usually involving complaints about IID personnel.
There is also an agreement between the Ombudsman and
the Commissioner that the Ombudsman will conduct a
special investigation
into any complaint relating to
a death or serious injury of an Aborigine or Torres
Strait Islander in AFP custody.
Further investigations where the Ombudsman can conduct
investigations following IID investigations. The Ombudsman
has only occasionally
exercised this power.
'Own motion' investigations where the Ombudsman has
the power to investigate actions of AFP appointees
on her own initiative.
role in investigations
2.25 Internal Security
and Audit (ISA)
was established by the AFP Commissioner in April 1990.
It has no legislative basis and was an internal response
by the AFP to
the type of problems that the Fitzgerald
exposed in Queensland. Its objectives are to:
change the culture of the AFP so as to engender an
ethos of personal and organisational integrity
establish and maintain preventive policies, practices
and procedures
maintain a pro-active capacity to investigate corruption,
including any link between corrupt conduct and organised
2.26 ISA has 50
personnel, comprising 28 members and 22 staff members.
It has three branches: investigations, audit and internal
Investigations.
ISA is intended to investigate serious
criminal and corrupt conduct within the AFP and in
particular any links between AFP personnel
and organised
crime. If ISA is to investigate a complaint under the
Complaints Act then it must be authorised to do so.
ISA's Internal Audit Branch (IAB) functions
are to reform policy and practices in the AFP. It conducts
regular audits of different
areas of the AFP, particularly
high risk areas such as drug holding, cash holdings
and financial accounts.
Internal security.
This focuses on security policy
and training, vetting of staff and ethics education.
It also includes the pro-active gathering
of information
and intelligence on the identity, capabilities, intentions
and methods of organisations and individuals who pose,
or may pose, a threat to the security of the AFP. It
also implements protective security policies, procedures
and provides
security support.
2.27 In 1994-95,
ISA investigated 87 references and completed 60 with
the following outcomes
47 allegations substantiated
59 allegations not substantiated
39 criminal charges recommended (comprising a few cases
that result in multiple charges)
5 disciplinary charges recommended
4 recommendations that fixed term appointments not
be renewed or be terminated
7 other administrative actions
2 references resulted in laying of serious criminal
charges against two AFP investigators.
The current
NCA complaints process
2.28 The NCA has
a mixed workforce consisting of
the Chairperson and members
persons employed under the
Public Service Act 1922
consultants
seconded staff from federal, State or Territory law
enforcement and other agencies
members of Commonwealth, State or joint Commonwealth
and State Task Forces arranged by the NCA.
2.29 Since the
establishment of the NCA in 1984 none of its mixed
workforce has been subject to a uniform, legislatively
NCA complaints system. There is no provision
in the NCA Act for a process of making and investigating
complaints against the
NCA or members of its staff.
The current administrative procedure is that all complaints
are forwarded to the Chairperson who
then directs officers
to undertake inquiries on his or her behalf. Where
allegations against the NCA or its staff have been
deemed to be serious enough to warrant it, either the
NCA or the Minister has appointed an independent inquirer
to investigate
2.30 Current accountability
for the general activities of the NCA resides in two
bodies: the Inter-Governmental Committee (IGC)
the Parliamentary Joint Committee on the NCA (PJC).
2.31 The IGC is
established under the NCA Act and consists of Commonwealth,
State and Territory Ministers responsible for administering
the NCA Act in their jurisdictions. It is chaired by
the Commonwealth Minister.
2.32 The PJC is
made up of five Members of the House of Representatives
and five Senators. It monitors and reviews the performance
by the NCA of its functions and reports to both Houses
of Parliament.
2.33 The IGC and
the PJC do not investigate or resolve individual complaints
against the NCA. Neither body would be appropriate
or has the structure and resources to perform that
of complaints
2.34 The NCA received
17 complaints in 1994-95, 18 the year before and 15
in 1992-93. Some were not investigated because there
were no objective facts or circumstances in support
of them, a few related to recruitment matters, others
related to the alleged
unauthorised use or release
of information or to allegations that investigations
or investigative techniques were illegal or
Inadequacies
of the AFP complaints system
2.35 The AFP complaints
system has a number of deficiencies.
of clear responsibility for integrity of the system
2.36 The major
problem is that there is no one entity who accepts
responsibility for the integrity of the system or who
be identified as satisfying that responsibility.
The Ombudsman has provided only a limited form of scrutiny
that has rarely extended
beyond a review of IID investigations
after they have commenced or after they are concluded.
The Ombudsman has therefore been
unable in any unequivocal
way to vouch for the integrity and independence of
the process. There are many weaknesses in a reliance
on the Ombudsman's current limited role as a guarantee
of independence. They concern the credibility and effectiveness
2.37 A complaints
system must be perceived by complainants, potential
complainants and the general public as credible. They
must have confidence that complaints will be properly
and impartially handled and investigated. However there
is increasing evidence
that many complainants and the
general public feel unease about the police investigating
other police. The Ombudsman has said
that feedback
from complainants and community groups and evidence
to the Senate Committee examining amendments to the
Act in 1994 indicate that the public is
not confident about the AFP's internal investigations.
2.38 A consistent
theme in many of the Commission's consultations and
the submissions it has received is that the police,
particularly
in respect to serious complaints, should
not be conducting these investigations. This theme
is echoed in the NSW Royal Commission
into the Police's
Interim Report and the submissions made to it. It is
a concern heightened by the focus on police corruption
and misconduct not only in NSW but in other Australian
2.39 It is clear
that there have been many cases under the current AFP
complaints system where primary investigation by an
agency and not by the IID would have been
much more preferable if not demanded by the circumstances.
These include cases of significant
public interest
or media attention, where there appear to be particularly
vulnerable complainants or where there are suspicions
of extensive police involvement in misconduct and allegations
of cover-up. The Ombudsman has identified cases where
much greater
input from her office would have been
preferable.
2.40 One graphic
illustration is provided by the response to the 169
complaints against the AFP arising from the Australian
International Defence Exhibition (AIDEX) demonstrations.
This was a highly publicised incident resulting in
a broad range of
complaints including serious police
brutality, assault or excessive force. Primary investigation
of the complaints was conducted
by the IID. The Ombudsman's
office did not conduct one primary investigation.
2.41 The Commission
is unable to comment on the substance of these 169
complaints. That is not the point here. What is at
is the very significant damage that can be done
to the credibility of the complaints system when there
is no adequate response
that will satisfy the expectations
of complainants or the community.
2.42 The Ombudsman
only occasionally re-investigates IID investigations
and she does so in cases where she is particularly
dissatisfied
with the IID investigation. She has given
the Commission the following examples where she has
re-investigated: misconduct involved
in search warrants;
a complaint in relation to property; inadequate investigations
of a motor vehicle accident; improperly accessing
releasing information; and drinking on duty at a night
club. However, she acknowledges that re-investigation
effective because of the time that has usually
elapsed before it occurs.
The trail of evidence is lost or tainted.
2.43 The Ombudsman
has advised the Commission that the failure of her
office to carry out her own investigations or to effectively
supervise the IID in these types of matters is due
to a lack of resources and the restrictions imposed
upon her role by the
Complaints Act. The issue of resources
is further considered in chapter 5. The Ombudsman argues
that the following interpretation
of the Complaints
Act has restricted her role:
The Ombudsman is unable to make the initial assessment
The Ombudsman has no power to supervise IID investigations
because apart from the power to obtain progress reports
the Ombudsman
has no statutory power to influence the
IID investigation in any way.
The Act gives the Officer-in-Charge of the IID the
power to conduct the investigation as she or he thinks
The power for the Ombudsman to conduct special investigations
where it is not 'appropriate' for investigation by
the IID is a
power that must be interpreted in the
context of the philosophy and mandate provided by ALRC
Report 9 to the effect that it would
a rare case where
the IID would not be conducting the investigation.
Moreover the Ombudsman's power to conduct special
investigations in appropriate cases requires agreement
by the AFP Commissioner
or failing that a report to
the Minister.
2.44 The Commission
considers that these views are unnecessarily restrictive.
As far as the Commission has been able to ascertain
the full scope of the Ombudsman's investigatory powers
has not been subject to expert legal opinion, particularly
Attorney-General's Department. The Ombudsman
has not taken the opportunity to test the extent of
those powers when it has been
clear for some considerable
time that the system has not achieved its objectives.
2.45 The legislation
should be considered in the clear context that the
Ombudsman is to provide effective external review of
the AFP complaints system. For example, it is the Ombudsman
who may determine that a complaint should not be investigated
and it is the Ombudsman who can make reports and recommendations
after investigations are carried out.
It is also the Ombudsman who has the reporting role
to the complainant.
The key role of the Ombudsman as the external agency
is manifest.
2.46 The Commission
is not convinced that the Act prevents the Ombudsman
from taking a more active role. For example, the AFP
is under a duty to notify the Ombudsman of every complaint
received by it.
It should be possible for the Ombudsman to obtain
a reasonably early view about which complaints require
greater involvement
or supervision. Under s 46 there
is power for the Ombudsman to request that her office
or another person undertake an investigation
an IID investigation would be inappropriate. It would
be open to the Ombudsman to use this power to investigate
or to get others to investigate a far greater
number and range of matters than has been investigated
by that means. Also under
s 23(1) (a) the Ombudsman
is to investigate a complaint that is in substance
power could be construed quite widely to allow much
more involvement from the Ombudsman.
2.47 In addition
s 35 enables the Ombudsman when the IID is investigating
not only to request progress reports but also to discuss
the complaint with the complainant and to request the
Commissioner for access to any documents and to interview
a person other
than the complainant in relation to
the complaint. Also since 1995 the Ombudsman has had
an 'own motion' power which has been
used three times.
2.48 The Commission
considers that the Ombudsman could have tried to use
his or her powers more robustly than the record of
activity since the introduction of the Act indicates.
distinction between complaints and allegations
2.49 The distinction
between complaints and allegations is artificial and
creates difficulties. Allegations are currently not
subject to review by the Ombudsman or by any other
external agency but yet it is internal complaints that
are most likely to
allege or suggest corruption.
2.50 Under the
current system the IID has a central role in the investigation
of complaints. The Ombudsman has expressed doubts
the current capacity of the IID to perform this role
to a consistently high standard.
She has advised that in the past year she has reinvestigated
three complaints following investigation by the IID.
that in all three cases her office was able
to identify and gather significant evidence not identified
by the IID. In each case
the investigation by the IID
was found to be inadequate. She claims that the record
of IID investigations is uneven, ranging
poor to very good. The Ombudsman has also expressed
concern about inadequate training of IID investigators,
that there is no requirement that IID recruits
have experience in investigations and that there is
no formal training program
for those who may need it.
2.51 It is disquieting
to receive such comments from the Ombudsman who provides
oversight of the IID and should therefore be well
to make informed and detailed comments on the record
and competence of the IID. The reinvestigation of three
cases appears
to be to be a fairly small sample to
sample is a cause for concern in
itself. However, that
limited sample suggests that there are significant
problems with IID investigations. The Commission is
concerned that currently the Ombudsman appears to have
been unable to provide any comprehensive or effective
quality control
of the performance of the IID.
2.52 The Commission
is also concerned about the apparent lack of established
guidelines and standards agreed upon by the AFP and
the Ombudsman about the staffing, training of the IID
and the conduct of investigations. There is an administrative
arrangement
that the Ombudsman and the IID meet regularly
to discuss cases and broader issues but there does
not appear to be any established
case management process
nor an established manual of procedure and practice,
nor any formal record of meetings. Matters appear
be dealt with largely on a case by case, ad hoc basis.
This is a significant problem when the experience and
skills of staff
may vary and where consistency and
timeliness are key objectives.
2.53 ISA was introduced
not by legislation but by internal arrangement by the
AFP in 1990. The Commission acknowledges that its
was a desirable step and a recognition by the AFP that
it must respond vigorously to corruption and potential
corruption.
However, the Commission is concerned that
ISA has not received any legislative recognition but
yet operates as the AFP's major
anti-corruption force
with significant powers and responsibilities. It uses
the full range of investigative tools including listening
devices, telephone intercepts and surveillance. However,
there is no external monitoring of its activities in
the same fashion
as the IID is oversighted by the Ombudsman.
2.54 Another problem
is that there appears to be no clear delineation between
the respective roles of the IID and ISA in investigating
complaints. While in general terms ISA has as its stated
role the investigation of serious criminal and corrupt
activity, as
far as the Commission has been able to
ascertain, serious criminal conduct is not necessarily
investigated by ISA. For example,
a complaint arising
from a murder or rape may be investigated by a team
of investigators with specific skills in those areas
managed or oversighted by members of IID or ISA. IID
is likely to be involved in those investigations unless
there are additional
elements involved such as an allegation
of corruption, an abuse of office, the involvement
of non-AFP personnel or the need for
surveillance or
other sophisticated means of investigation.
2.55 Currently
the decision as to whether IID or ISA will investigate
a particular matter is a 'judgment call' that is determined
generally by conferences between ISA and the IID and
the relevant Deputy Commissioner on an ad hoc basis.
These decisions can
have considerable ramifications.
ISA 's investigations are very thorough and often require
considerable resources and time. There
are 26 ISA investigators
who between them investigate about 80 matters per year.
Of this range of about 80 matters only about
cent will result in serious criminal charges.
2.56 The Commission
believes that this current ad hoc arrangement is inadequate
and should be replaced by an appropriate system
risk assessment and the application of criteria to
determine whether a matter is likely to concern serious
or systemic corruption
or might be best dealt with
at least initially by some form of preliminary inquiry
before launching a full scale investigation.
the Commission acknowledges that there are difficulties
in determining how a particular matter will develop
it is essential
that a form of risk management is adopted
that is based upon experience and precedents and an
established database of information.
2.57 The Commission
is also concerned that having two separate investigative
units is an inefficient use of resources that leads
to unnecessary arguments about demarcation and the
likelihood of duplication. There is a risk that it
can also lead to internal
jealousies and empire building
with the danger of both bodies seeking to justify their
positions, funding and staffing rather
than acting
on the realities of the task. The general public may
also have difficulties in understanding the difference
the bodies and the reasoning behind decisions
2.58 The Ombudsman
has criticised the current response to complaints as
being too slow. She says that the average time to finalise
complaints against the AFP is almost six months. This
includes cases handled quickly by conciliation and
those not investigated.
She adds that this is by far
the longest average of all agencies investigated by
the Commonwealth Ombudsman - for most of them
less than two months to finalise complaints. Therefore,
there are a large number of investigated cases involving
AFP that take more than six months to finalise.
The Ombudsman has advised that in respect of complaints
closed in 1994-5, 16.8%
took 6 to 12 months to finalise,
12.7% took one to two years and 3% took more than two
2.59 The AFP acknowledges
that delays do occur from time to time but states that
the majority of complaints handled by the AFP
are completed
and reports forwarded to the Ombudsman within a reasonable
time. The AFP claims that lengthy delays sometimes
occur when awaiting reviews by the Ombudsman. These
delays can occur, for example, if the Ombudsman requires
the AFP to take
additional action. The AFP advises
that for the reporting year 1994-95 the average time
taken by the IID to complete investigations
For the same period the average time taken by the
Ombudsman to review and finalise work on complaints
was 114.2 days.
According to the AFP another factor that contributes
to delay is where there is a protracted court or tribunal
2.60 The Commission
considers that the current system is clearly too slow.
Survey evidence
and the Commission's consultations and submissions
show that delay is one of the most consistent criticisms
made of complaints
systems by complainants and by those
who are the subject of complaints.
of accessibility
2.61 To achieve
an effective complaints system members of the public
and officers of the agencies should have ready access
finding out about how they can complain and have
the confidence to use the procedures available. The
research available suggests
that there is a lack of
awareness and knowledge about complaints systems particularly
by people from a non-English speaking background,
and Torres Strait Islanders and youth. The Commission
would expect that ACT residents may have a much greater
of the AFP complaints mechanism compared
to other communities. The Commission's consultations
in Norfolk Island suggest that
there is little awareness
in that community about the role of the Commonwealth
2.62 The Ombudsman
and the AFP have taken measures to improve the awareness
and accessibility of the system. The Ombudsman has
advised that limited resources have restricted her
capacity to implement programs to promote awareness.
of common or comprehensive database
2.63 There is not
after 14 years a common complaints database used by
both the AFP and the Ombudsman. Thus, for example,
employ different methods in assessing the number
of complaints from any one incident. This means that
at the most basic level
there is no agreement on the
total number of complaints. Nor do the Ombudsman and
the AFP have any common information technology
would enable them to record and pass on information
to each other virtually simultaneously.
2.64 Nor is there
any comprehensive system available to the AFP or the
Ombudsman for classifying complaints as to their seriousness
or their nature. For example, the AFP data on the classification
of complaints contained in its Annual Reports have
the classifications
'assault' and 'use excessive force'
with no explanation as to the difference between these
two categories. The Ombudsman has
provided the Commission
with statistics on the number of matters conciliated
but has not provided data on other types of complaints
in terms of seriousness or other characteristics. The
Ombudsman has advised the Commission that her office
has in the last
18 months developed a system that can
provide some classification of complaints, for example,
using categories such as assault,
excessive force,
racism, rudeness and sexual harassment.
defects in the NCA complaints process
2.65 The current
ad hoc arrangement for complaints against the NCA is
grossly deficient in that it lacks any publicly known
or recognised process and any consistent external scrutiny.
The system does little if anything to create public
confidence in
the accountability or integrity of the
NCA. The current system is particularly inadequate
for the majority of citizens who do
not have the resources
and time to mount expensive court challenges to the
exercise of NCA powers.
2.66 In its 1991
Who is to Guard the Guards. An evaluation of
the National Crime Authority
the Parliamentary Joint
Committee on the NCA (PJC ) considered that the complaints
system needed to be improved and canvassed
such as the Commonwealth Ombudsman, the Inspector-General
of Intelligence and Security or a new agency.
The then Chairperson of the NCA, Justice Phillips
told the PJC that he favoured a system of inquiry outside
the NCA for handling
serious complaints.
A further report by the PJC in October 1994 again
drew the Parliament's attention to the lack of a fully
adequate avenue available
for investigations of complaints
brought against the NCA.
It commented that the continuing lack of a complaints
mechanism was unacceptable. The PJC itself had received
complaints from
individuals about the NCA but the PJC
commented that it lacked the time and the resources
to investigate the more complex complaints.

for both complainants and the NCA.
