3. Investigative models
Introduction
1. An internal investigation model
2. An external investigation model
3. Internal investigation with external oversight
4. Combination of internal and external investigations
Queensland CJC model
of the ICAC model
role for the Commonwealth Ombudsman
NSW Police Integrity Commission
Commission's favoured option
Introduction
3.1 Chapter 2 exposed
the major defects in the operation of the current AFP
and NCA complaints systems. This chapter discusses
investigative models. It reveals some basic flaws in
the current AFP complaints model which relies on internal
investigation
with external oversight. It suggests
that a new body with responsibility for investigating
or controlling the investigation of
both AFP and NCA
complaints and corruption is the most suitable investigative
model. The fundamental issue is to determine the
appropriate
combination of internal and external investigation.
The major options for investigating complaints are
as follows.
1. An internal investigation model
3.2 This model would
mean that the particular law enforcement agency would
have sole responsibility for investigating complaints
against itself or its staff.
3.3 This is an outdated
concept in Australia and increasingly so in countries
with comparable legal systems to Australia such
New Zealand, England, Northern Ireland, Wales, Canada
and the United States.
The concept lacks credibility with the public and
a number of inquiries have demonstrated the likelihood
of ineffectiveness of
internal investigations where
there is insufficient external scrutiny.
There is very little support for a return to this
format in Australia. This is borne out in the Commission's
consultations and
the submissions it has received.
The Commission considers that there is no further need
to consider it in this inquiry.
2. An external investigation model
3.4 Under this option
an external agency would carry out all the main functions
of the complaints system. This would mean that
external agency would have its own investigation unit
which would conduct all investigations or the vast
majority of them.
The AFP or the NCA would have no
role or be limited to passing on information to the
external agency.
3.5 This model has
considerable superficial attraction as it is the model
most likely to engender the greatest initial public
confidence. It gives as much power and responsibility
as possible to an external agency. It therefore deals
with concerns about
the lack of effectiveness or objectivity
of internal investigations.
3.6 The Commission
does not support this option because it regards it
as neither desirable in principle nor practical. Before
discussing those arguments it should be noted that
a completely external system is probably illusory.
There is always likely
to be a need for some degree
of liaison and co-operation with the police and for
some police filtering of complaints for the
agency to be effective.
The Commission is unaware of any police complaints
system that could be characterised as completely external.
3.7 The most important
reason for ensuring that a law enforcement agency continues
to have some role in the investigation and
of complaints is that the agency must be given managerial
responsibility to deal with complaints that are generated
by its own officers. This is clearly so for complaints
that can be categorised as customer service or internal
management complaints.
The law enforcement agency must
have the primary role for resolving these types of
complaints because they are by their very
nature matters
that concern managerial responsibility.
3.8 A purely external
investigation will weaken the responsibility of the
agency to maintain discipline within the service. The
task should be to engage the agency at all levels in
the need for active responsibility for effective discipline
and a changed
culture which values complaints as a
source of information, that is as a management tool
for identifying where changes in policy
and practices
may be necessary. Faced with a completely external
complaints and discipline authority it is likely that
enforcement agencies, particularly their middle
managers, will simply absolve themselves of responsibility
for making the system
It is also possible that they may actively work against
it, for example by being unco-operative or withholding
information. The
law enforcement agency may not become
engaged or committed to the process. A completely external
body might also reduce the chances
of internal institutional
reforms designed to reduce complaints in the first
Moreover a completely external unit would become in
effect an alternative police force and may itself develop
some undesirable
aspects of culture such as defensiveness
and insularity.
3.9 There are also
the following practical difficulties in trying to use
this model.
3.10 It would be
difficult to ensure that there are sufficiently experienced
and trained civilian staff to investigate all complaints
that warrant investigation. While the Commission does
not subscribe to the view that only experienced and
trained police officers
or ex-police officers can conduct
high quality investigations into complaints against
the police it may be difficult logistically
costly to have sufficient civilian staff with the requisite
qualities to investigate all complaints that require
investigation.
These logistical and resource difficulties
are especially significant in relation to the AFP and
the NCA because both are national
bodies with a presence
in the States and Territories. The geographical spread
of the jurisdiction means that it would be costly
have a completely external investigation unit with
sufficient resources and expertise to service the whole
of Australia.
3.11 Another practical
problem is that a completely external process will
create delays and duplication because it is likely
that the police will still have to conduct at least
some preliminary inquiries to establish the nature
of the complaint and
to process it. This is especially
so for the lower end of complaints concerning, for
example, rudeness or incivility. To remove
these types
of complaints from the police would mean that it would
be much more difficult and time consuming to use informal
resolution and mediation. The external body would have
to contact the relevant law enforcement officers in
each of these matters,
ascertain what happened and
then order or conduct the informal resolution. It would
be adding another layer to the resolution
matters where informality and speed are essential goals.
3.12 The Commission
is also of the view that the AFP and the NCA should
continue to have a role in investigating the medium
range of complaints and in some circumstances serious
complaints. However, their role in these investigations
should either be
managed or supervised by one external
body with a wide range of powers and ultimate responsibility
for the working of the whole
system. This would enable
the AFP and the NCA to have a significant role in the
investigations aspect of the complaints system
the parameters of that role generally and in the particular
case would be determined by the external agency. This
achieve the objectives of credibility and effectiveness.
It establishes a system whereby each complaint receives
the most appropriate
response that is tailored to its
particular circumstances. Moreover, the AFP and the
NCA would continue to have a primary role
in the disciplining
of their personnel. In fact under the Commission's
proposals in relation to the AFP and NCA disciplinary
systems, these agencies' responsibilities would be
3. Internal investigation with external oversight
3.13 This model
broadly describes the current AFP complaints system
and those of the Australian States and the Northern
The Queensland CJC has a different approach
which is discussed at paragraphs 3.27-33. However,
for the Commonwealth, Victoria,
Western Australia,
the Northern Territory and Tasmania review of complaints
against the police is part of the responsibility
an Ombudsman's Office or similar body which has wider
responsibilities to review government activity. South
Australia has
a Police Complaints Authority who can
be described as a specialist Police Ombudsman. Chapter
6 of the issues paper describes
these Australian systems.
3.14 In these jurisdictions
other than Queensland the majority of investigations
are carried out by the internal investigation
of the respective police services. In general terms
the external agency has a power to conduct its own
independent investigations
the complaint concerns a police officer senior to all
members of the internal unit
the complaint concerns a member of the unit
the complaint relates to a matter the external agency
is already investigating
the external agency is of the view that it is in the
public interest that the complaint be investigated
by the agency.
3.15 These external
agencies may also monitor and review initial investigations
by the police, directing them to conduct further
investigations.
However a common observation by external agencies in
Australia is that they lack the resources to conduct
investigations
themselves into as many of the complaints
as they would like. In particular, they are unable
to initiate and conduct investigations
in cases where
the complaints involve serious allegations, where there
are particularly vulnerable complainants, for example,
youth or people from a non-English speaking background
or where matters of significant public interest are
3.16 In the Australian
to discipline rests in the first instance with the
Commissioner of the particular police service and on
appeal by the particular police officer complained
against to an independent
disciplinary tribunal or
board. The external agency may make recommendations
criminal or disciplinary charges should be laid but
it is limited to recommendatory powers only.
3.17 This basic
model is used widely overseas in comparable countries
to Australia including New Zealand, England, Northern
Ireland, Republic of Ireland, Scotland, Wales and a
number of jurisdictions in Canada and the United States.
Chapter 7 of the
issues paper describes some of these
overseas systems.
3.18 The specific
defects in the AFP complaints version of this model
have been dealt with in detail in chapter 2. The Commission
is of the view that, while this model was innovative
in the 1970's, it is now ill equipped to cope with
the increasing demands
for greater accountability of
the police and for an effective response to police
corruption.
3.19 The model
relies on the internal investigation unit investigating
the bulk of complaints and for the external overseer
to investigate in exceptional cases. However, the number
of cases that are actually investigated by the external
agency appear
on the whole to be inadequate for the
effective and credible working of the system. Many
of these external oversight agencies
do not or cannot
fund their operations so that they are able to conduct
their own investigations in anything but a few cases
and their monitoring of internal investigations tends
to be patchy and often confined to a review of the
papers. Moreover,
because the agency has the task of
reviewing or monitoring the whole range of complaints,
scarce resources are often allocated
to the minimal
task of maintaining the system without any strategic
targeting of serious or 'high risk' cases.
3.20 The effectiveness
of a system that mainly relies on police conducting
and controlling investigations is also open to serious
question. Major inquiries such as Royal Commissions
in Australia that have closely examined these issues
and used significant
powers to do so indicate that
there are very significant problems in relying on police
internal investigation units and police
to ensure accountability and consistently appropriate
standards of conduct.
3.21 The performance
of internal investigations has received considerable
criticism in Australia.
Criticisms usually concern inadequacy, bias or excessive
delays. The prevailing police culture means that there
are strong risks
that they will not be able to conduct
thorough and fair investigations.
A number of inquiries in Australia have discussed
corrupt or inefficient police internal investigation
units. The Fitzgerald
report of 1989 in Queensland
commented that the Queensland Internal Investigations
Unit had been 'woefully ineffective' and recommended
its abolition.
The NSW Royal Commission into Police Interim Report
also found deficiencies in a number of significant
NSW police internal investigations.
There was also a police culture where reporting misconduct
was discouraged and where those who did were ostracised
and persecuted.
3.22 These types
of criticisms are not limited to Australia. The Fitzgerald
inquiry reviewed overseas experiences and made the
following comment on the problem of relying on police
internal investigations:
To a large extent, attempts all over the world to combat
police misconduct locally [ie internally] have revealed
similar and recurrent
problems: police culture, lack
of effective control of internal investigative procedures,
lack of investigative resources, organizations
procedures which inhibit honest police and lack of
public confidence in the Police Force's ability to
investigate complaints
against its members.
Lord Scarman in his report on the Brixton disorders
in 1981 wrote:
My own view is that if public confidence in the complaints
procedure is to be achieved any solution falling short
of a system
of independent investigation available
for all complainants (other than the frivolous) which
are not withdrawn is unlikely to
be successful.
One London study on the basis of observing operational
police work concluded that police officers will normally
tell lies to prevent
another officer from being disciplined
or prosecuted.
A commentary on the American experience made similar
points. According to this commentary American police
officers will rarely
complain or give evidence about
another's brutality or corruption. If an officer does
come forward with a complaint of brutality
or corruption
by a fellow officer, he or she is shunned and the entire
police force closes ranks in silence and denial.
3.23 The pervasive
influence of a defensive or antagonistic police culture
has greatly contributed to doubts about the effectiveness
of complaints systems that rely on police control of
complaints investigations. Police culture describes
the operation of informal
rules that prescribe or modify
police behaviour and that will affect police attitudes
to defining and dealing with misconduct.
Police cultures
are often regarded as defensive and antagonistic towards
complainants and their complaints. Police will protect
each other and reinforce the informal rules in the
face of perceived external and internal challenges
including those arising
from complaints. Codes of silence
are recognised as difficulties in law enforcement cultures
around the world. These 'negative'
police cultures
can permeate the entire organisation or can prevent
relevant information from being received by senior
management.
There are clear examples of the gross failures
in accountability that can develop. The NSW Royal Commission
into Police Interim
Report commented that the NSW police
senior management appeared to be unaware of the extent
of the problem of serious misconduct
in NSW or if aware,
have failed to adequately respond to it.
3.24 Another significant
limitation is that this investigative model does not
provide any effective mechanisms to prevent or detect
corruption. Nor could it. Complaints will not often
provide information about corruption and corruption
prevention and detection
is about pro-active measures
and not waiting for complaints to expose misconduct.
The work of the NSW Royal Commission into the
demonstrates that extensive and sophisticated surveillance
may be needed to detect and gather sufficient evidence
police corruption.
3.25 In response
to these types of problems the overall trend in countries
comparable to Australia is to systems of increasing
external review. In Queensland the level of external
input has gone beyond the model of internal investigations
with external
oversight to a combination of internal
and external investigations. The introduction of the
Police Integrity Commission (PIC)
in NSW will mean
that NSW would also go beyond that model. The approaches
in Queensland and NSW are discussed below.
4. Combination of internal and external investigations
3.26 Queensland
and NSW have developed models which combine internal
and external investigations.
Queensland CJC model
3.27 Review of
complaints against the Queensland Police is conducted
by the Criminal Justice Commission (CJC) which was
established
in 1990 following upon the recommendations
of the Fitzgerald Inquiry. The CJC is not a body solely
concerned with police complaints,
but instead has various
functions and responsibilities in criminal justice.
There are five main divisions: the Official Misconduct
Division; the Witness Protection Division; the Research
and Co-ordination Division; the Intelligence Division
and the Corruption
Prevention Division. The CJC's functions
investigating official conduct in units of public administration
and alleged or suspected misconduct by members of the
Police Service (QPS)
monitoring, reviewing, co-ordinating and initiating
reform of the administration of criminal justice
overseeing criminal intelligence matters and managing
criminal intelligence
providing witness protection
in certain situations, investigating organised or major
overseeing and reporting of reform of the QPS
providing policy directives and recommendations with
respect to law enforcement priorities, education and
3.28 The Official
Misconduct Division (OMD) is the investigative unit
within the CJC and it investigates cases of alleged
suspected misconduct by members of the QPS. It also
reviews minor complaints that are investigated and
dealt with internally
by the QPS. The QPS must report
all complaints of misconduct to the CJC.
3.29 Complaints
against members of the QPS are divided into three categories:
A breach of discipline
is a breach of any provision
Police Service Administration Act 1990
or directions of the Police Commissioner. These can
commonly be described as a violation or dereliction
Misconduct matters
are more serious and are defined
as disgraceful, improper or other conduct unbecoming
an officer; or conduct that does not meet
the standard
of conduct reasonably expected by the community of
a police officer.
Official misconduct
is the most serious category and
involves conduct that constitutes or could constitute,
a criminal offence or disciplinary breach
dismissal. The conduct must involve the officer acting
in his or her capacity as a police officer in a manner
is not honest or impartial, involves a breach
of trust or a misuse of information.
3.30 The QPS is
responsible for dealing with complaints involving breaches
of discipline. Most of these are handled within the
region where the officer who is subject of the complaint
is located. The QPS will notify the CJC of all complaints
it considers
involve breaches of discipline. The CJC
assesses all complaints to determine whether they involve
breaches of discipline or misconduct.
If a complaint
is assessed as a breach of discipline it is referred
to the QPS for action and the CJC has no further interest
3.31 If the complaint
is assessed as a misconduct matter, the CJC is responsible
for its investigation. The OMD can refer cases
misconduct back to the QPS for investigation on behalf
of the CJC. When the investigation has been concluded,
CJC reviews the investigation report, examines
the recommendations and determines if the complaint
has been substantiated. In
certain cases, the CJC may
request that the QPS investigate a complaint alleging
serious misconduct or conduct part of the investigation.
This is particularly likely to happen where the complaint
relates to events outside Brisbane and CJC investigators
be in the area for some time. The investigation
is always overviewed by a CJC police officer of the
rank of inspector or above.
On the basis of the report
provided by the QPS in such cases the CJC decides if
further action and investigation are necessary.
3.32 If a complaint
is assessed as alleging serious misconduct, official
misconduct or is criminal in nature, the CJC retains
carriage of the investigation. Such cases are usually
referred to multi-disciplinary teams for investigation.
The teams comprise
seconded police, civilian investigators
(former police) and civilian lawyers. On occasions
the CJC will have the QPS conduct
a preliminary investigation
into a complaint that has been assessed as possibly
constituting criminal or official misconduct.
in such instances the CJC retains responsibility for
the investigation. The OMD's complaints section closely
these matters. The police officers follow
detailed guidelines laid down by the CJC. The officers
are told who the CJC wants interviewed
and what the
report is to include. When the report is received by
the CJC the complaint goes through the normal assessment
process. The officer, or officers, who are the subject
of the complaint are interviewed by CJC personnel.
Allegations which involve
major or organised crime
where the CJC has jurisdiction, or serious complaints
against police of a more complex nature are also
to a multi-disciplinary team for investigation.
3.33 The Commission
would not recommend the introduction of the CJC model
in its entirety at the federal level. The adoption
of a model essentially similar to the CJC would require
a major restructuring of the federal criminal justice
system. It would
involve combining many of the essential
elements and agencies responsible for the administration
of criminal justice. There appears
to be little reason
at present to justify such a radical restructuring.
The CJC model was a response to findings of systemic
failings in the Queensland justice system which were
not limited to the police force. The same findings
have not been made
in respect of the federal system
of criminal justice. There are also concerns about
the breadth of the Queensland CJC's involvement
the investigation of organised crime and the fact that
the Misconduct Tribunals are linked even administratively
investigative division of the CJC.
of the ICAC model
3.34 The NSW ICAC's
charter is to expose and minimise corruption in the
public sector including the NSW Police Service. Its
three main functions are:
exposing and deterring corrupt conduct by investigating
and reporting on allegations of corruption
corruption prevention
education about the detrimental effects of corruption
and ways to prevent it.
3.35 ICAC can investigate
on its own initiative or on a complaint by any person.
One of its central features is its holding of
hearings. It can require witnesses to make self incriminating
statements. A public authority or public official may
be required to produce a statement of information and
any person may be required to produce specific documents
or things. It
can search public premises and may obtain
warrants to search other places. It can issue summonses
to attend hearings and then
provide reports to Parliament.
3.36 The NSW Royal
Commission examined whether the ICAC should have a
dedicated police corruption division within it. It
this option for a number of reasons including:
there is a public perception that the ICAC has failed
to tackle police corruption
a real difficulty exists in structuring a division
of the ICAC which could be kept separate and independent
from the rest of the
organisation
the other functions of ICAC might mean that competing
demands for resources could result in a lack of proper
resourcing and attention
for the police corruption
3.37 The NSW ICAC
has a broad charter to deal with corruption in the
public sector. In that sense it extends far beyond
with police corruption or complaints. The question
of whether an ICAC with such a broad ranging responsibility
is necessary at
the federal level is beyond the Commission's
terms of reference for this inquiry. However, as noted
above, in the context of
the NSW ICAC's role in dealing
with police corruption the NSW Royal Commission into
the Police did not favour a dedicated Police
division within the NSW ICAC. The effect of this recommendation
is that the ICAC would play no active role in investigating
corruption within the NSW Police Service.
role for the Commonwealth Ombudsman
3.38 The Commonwealth
Ombudsman has submitted that the most effective response
in terms of outcome and costs would be to provide
office with
the general power to determine who should investigate
a matter, for example, the power to investigate herself
when it is in the
public interest
the resources to conduct between 40 to 50 independent
investigations of police complaints per year
enhanced powers and resources to supervise effectively
IID investigations (eg the power to attend interviews,
to veto or approve
an investigating officer and to
give directions about the investigation such as interviewing
in respect of alleged criminal or corrupt activities
the capacity to conduct joint investigations with ISA
or with other law enforcement
agencies under the Ombudsman's
supervision
and the power to determine for any complaint that no
further action is warranted (a current power).
3.39 The Commission
is of the view that one new agency would be preferable
to the Ombudsman undertaking an enhanced role as she
has suggested.
3.40 Currently
the Ombudsman in assessing her priorities has only
been able to assign very limited resources to conducting
own investigations or providing concerted management
or oversight of IID investigations. The very broad
nature of the Ombudsman's
is more difficult for that office to provide a single
focus on police complaints particularly those
are serious. However, it is the proper investigation
and response to these complaints that are crucial to
the effectiveness
and credibility of the system. These
are the complaints that are most likely to raise significant
public interest and to be the
subject of media publicity.
It is these complaints that are most likely to involve
suggestions of cover-up or misconduct by
one officer. The Commission considers that there is
always the difficulty with a generalist Ombudsman's
office that
resources will be spread too thinly to
the detriment of appropriate funding and attention
to very vital and in some respects
more exacting areas
such as police complaints. In addition, there is always
the prospect that the broad jurisdiction of an Ombudsman's
office will be further expanded into new areas thus
compounding the risk of insufficient responses.
3.41 Investigations
into the police as opposed to other public officers
are often more complex and difficult as the NSW Royal
Commission into the Police has indicated. In the Commission's
view such complaints in the broad context of complaints
public agencies and their officers must receive
priority and an uninterrupted focus.
3.42 The Commission
considers that the external agency must have available
to it and the mandate and confidence to use the full
range of coercive powers that may be required, especially
for some very serious complaints such as those involving
corruption.
What will be necessary are the powers available
to a royal commission such as demonstrated with the
NSW Royal Commission into
the NSW Police. To have the
Ombudsman regularly using that range of powers would
be moving the role of the Ombudsman into unfamiliar
areas and beyond the generally accepted view of an
Ombudsman's office that it is concerned with defective
administration.
3.43 The Commission
is also concerned that the Ombudsman's office is unlikely
to have ordinarily available to her the expertise
consistently investigate serious police complaints
and corruption that in many cases will be complex and
difficult to investigate.
Some of these complaints
will concern experienced law enforcement officers determined
to avoid detection or prosecution. Some
will require a range of surveillance and intelligence
techniques and forensic skills that may be difficult
generalist Ombudsman's office to acquire or maintain
or to be fully conversant with.
3.44 A final difficulty
with relying on the Ombudsman's office is that it does
not have as a normal part of its work the preparation
of briefs for use in criminal proceedings. In the Commission's
view the preparation of briefs by the external body
is an essential
element. Criminal matters must be detected
and acted upon with the greatest speed possible and
with early consideration of the
available evidence
and what needs to be done to prepare the prosecution
3.45 The Commission
has considered the option of having the Ombudsman restricted
to investigating or overseeing non-serious complaints
but has rejected it for the reasons set out below in
its discussion of the NSW Royal Commission into the
Police Interim Report
and its proposed Police Integrity
Commission.
Police Integrity Commission
3.46 The NSW Royal
Commission's Interim Report found that a new complaints
and corruption investigation system was necessary in
NSW and should be determined according to the following
circumstances:
within the NSW Police Service there is a pattern of
corruption which must be urgently addressed, so that
public confidence is
the handling of complaints and the detection and investigation
of serious corruption demand different approaches
complaints alone do not provide an adequate measure
of the existence or extent of police corruption
corruption is insidiously difficult to detect and amounts
to serious criminal conduct which requires the application
of sophisticated
and determined investigative techniques;
the Police Service is a disciplined service which must
accept ownership of and responsibility for its problems.
3.47 The draft
exposure NSW Police Corruption Commission Bill provides
that the Police Corruption Commission (now known as
Police Integrity Commission as provided by a recent
new draft Bill) would have as a principal objective
the detection and investigation
of police corruption
and other serious police misconduct. The Bill does
not define what serious police misconduct is. The PIC
would be able to investigate itself or manage or supervise
the investigation conducted by other agencies. The
PIC and the NSW
Ombudsman can agree that Category 1
complaints, that is those which are likely to be considered
as involving allegations of serious
misconduct, should
be referred to the PIC.
The Ombudsman is to forward copies of all Category
1 complaints to the PIC. The PIC can investigate the
complaint itself or send
it to the Ombudsman. The general
scheme is that Category 1 complaints will be investigated
or managed by the PIC while other
categories will be
dealt with by the police with oversight or audit by
the Ombudsman. The majority of complaints will still
be dealt with by the NSW Ombudsman.
3.48 A key function
of the PIC would be to assemble admissible evidence
when investigations reveal criminal conduct and to
such evidence to the NSW Director of Public
Prosecutions. It would have the resources to be able
to conduct its own investigations
with a full range
of coercive powers similar to those of a Royal Commission
or the ICAC.
3.49 The model
proposed by the NSW Royal Commission and provided for
in the draft exposure Bill places the PIC at the apex
the complaints system but still envisages the Ombudsman
providing oversight and auditing of less serious complaints.
significantly different approach to the CJC
which is a single external agency with sole responsibility
for determining the investigation
of complaints.
3.50 The Commission
prefers that a single agency should have full responsibility
for external review. The advantages of a single
are that it
encourages a co-ordinated and systematic approach to
criminal justice issues that by their nature involve
many issues and systemic
ensures that cases do not fall through the cracks existing
where there is more than one agency involved
avoids duplication of resources and effort
centralise records and databases
heightens public awareness on the problems of corruption
and official misconduct because it is clearly the peak
makes it clear who has ultimate responsibility for
ensuring accountability and transparency.
3.51 Some possible
disadvantages are that the model could
lose focus on dealing with serious complaints and corruption
because it is also handling less serious complaints,
particularly
if there is a large volume of complaints
and a variety of other tasks
be unable to provide the necessary delineation between
on the one hand its pro-active, generally secretive
response to corruption
with, on the other hand, its
largely reactive, consumer focused response to complaints
lessen the responsibility and capacity of the law enforcement
agency to deal with its own problems, particularly
through prevention
and effective management.
3.52 The Commission
considers that one of the reasons for the serious problems
in the NSW police complaints and disciplinary systems
has been that there have been too many agencies with
some role to play. The NSW police complaints system
has input from the
NSW police as follows: the Office
of Professional Responsibility with its constituents
of the Professional Integrity Branch and
of Internal Affairs; the Corruption Prevention Unit;
the Comprehensive Audit Section and the Operational
Audit Section. The NSW Ombudsman has an oversight
role in relation to police complaints and the ICAC
could investigate police
corruption. Other bodies with
a role to play include the Ministry for Police, the
NSW Crime Commission and the Police Board of
NSW disciplinary structure can involve the
Commissioner of Police
Police Tribunal
Police Board
Government and Related Employees Appeal Tribunal
Minister for Police
3.53 In the Commission's
view a multiplicity of agencies increases the potential
for fragmented responsibility, for duplication
and resources and for individual cases not receiving
appropriate levels of attention.
3.54 The NSW Royal
Commission, while acknowledging that a single agency
would have the attraction of simplifying and integrating
the process, did not favour a single agency because
the different approaches needed for the complaints
system as opposed to corruption investigation
the need for a specific focus on corruption with an
aggressive and sophisticated investigative capacity,
the resources needed for effective monitoring of the
complaints system.
3.55 The Commission
does not consider that these concerns outweigh the
benefits of single external body in the context of
AFP and the NCA. This does not mean that the recommendations
of the NSW Royal Commission were not the most appropriate
As the Royal Commission noted the selection
of the appropriate model depends heavily on factors
specific to the particular jurisdiction
at the particular
3.56 In relation
to complaints against the AFP and the NCA the suggested
difficulties of a single external agency can be overcome
with proper planning and commitment to the objectives.
3.57 The Commission
acknowledges that complaints and corruption require
different approaches but this can be accommodated within
a single agency. The Commission proposes that the National
Integrity and Investigations Commission (NIIC) as discussed
5 should have two separate divisions, one
dealing with complaints and one with corruption. The
Chairperson of the NIIC would
have responsibility for
overseeing both divisions' work and any co-ordination
between them including the movement of matters
one division to another if there was a change in the
nature and requirements of the particular matter. However,
majority of cases matters would be dealt with
by the one division.
3.58 The NSW Royal
Commission was concerned that the sheer number of police
complaints in NSW would swamp a single agency and
the focus on serious misconduct and corruption. In
its Annual Report 1994-95 the NSW Police Service stated
that during
that financial year it received 5 537 written
complaints containing a total number of 10 172 complaints.
The AFP in the same
period reported 900 complaints
made by 596 complainants. The potential problem of
swamping does not appear to be so significant
federal level. Moreover with the introduction of a
national system of streamlined informal resolution
for customer service
and internal management complaints
the input required from the external agency will be
considerably less for those types of complaints.
should constitute about 70% of all complaints.
3.59 The Commission
proposes that the AFP and the NCA would still play
a major role in investigating complaints but that role
would be at the formal request of the external agency
particularly whenever there was a doubt or conflict
of an investigation. The experience
of the CJC does not suggest that discipline in the
Queensland Police Service has been detrimentally
by the powers of the CJC in respect of investigating
complaints against the police. It is important here
mind that complaints and discipline are
related but nevertheless two different processes. The
Commission is advocating a reduced
role for the AFP
and the NCA in the control of the investigation of
complaints but an increased role for these agencies
imposing discipline.
3.60 The Commission
is of the view that the AFP and the NCA can discharge
their disciplinary functions responsibly and effectively
with a new disciplinary process as proposed in chapter
6. As discussed in that chapter it would appear that
the current AFP
disciplinary process has a number of
major faults but there is no suggestion that AFP senior
management has a record of leniency
with officers when
imposing penalties for breaches of discipline. On the
contrary, the Commission considers that the AFP senior
management has demonstrated a commitment to impose
appropriate penalties that will act as a deterrent
to police misconduct.
The major problems of the current
disciplinary system relate to its characterisation
as quasi-criminal with a review and appeal
that has full adversarial hearings with their attendant
procedures and technicalities. In effect the current
has hamstrung the AFP in its efforts to achieve
effective discipline.
3.61 The Commission
does not consider that the introduction of the NIIC
will weaken the responsibility or commitment of the
NCA or the AFP to take a pro-active stand against misconduct
and corruption. The AFP and the NCA will still have
primary responsibility
for their own internal audit
and security, vetting and training of personnel and
the conduct of integrity testing and assessment.
will work in co-operation with the NIIC in detecting
and investigating corruption and misconduct but there
question that final control and decision
making rests with the NIIC once it is involved in an
investigation.
3.62 The NIIC will
be able to make recommendations about AFP and NCA policies
and procedures in relation to corruption and other
misconduct. The NIIC will also have a research and
policy division providing information and data about
the effectiveness of
the systems. These measures should
assist the AFP and the NCA in their capacity to be
pro-active and take effective preventive
The Commission's
favoured option
3.63 The Commission
favours the introduction of a new single external agency
to deal with serious complaints and corruption but
with the capacity and responsibility to manage or oversight
the complete AFP and NCA complaints process. An integrated
with one body assuming primary responsibility
is necessary.
3.64 The current
AFP and NCA systems have proved to be so inadequate
that merely fine tuning or restructuring the current
is unlikely to be sufficient particularly
given the need to ensure public credibility and credibility
with the agencies concerned.
3.65 If defective
systems are allowed to continue there is always the
danger that misconduct will be encouraged and will
flourish. Maintaining and improving the
integrity of law enforcement agencies is an area where
relying on a static response or
a sense of complacency
is fraught with risks. Both Australian and overseas
developments reinforce that view.
3.66 The Commission
has not received advice from any organisation or an
individual in an informed position to comment that
AFP or the NCA currently has problems with corruption
and misconduct of the same significance as some other
Australian law enforcement
bodies. In relation to the
AFP, the Commonwealth Ombudsman has submitted that
there is no evidence of the type of wide ranging
that led to the establishment of the CJC in Queensland.
The AFP has submitted that corruption within it is
not systemic
or widespread. It does concede that recent
allegations of corruption are of concern. The NCA has
advised that the opportunity
for corruption or serious
misconduct within its activities are limited.
3.67 The Commission
does not consider that these comments should be the
basis for any complacency. Nor were they intended to
be by the agencies concerned. Both the AFP and the
NCA are closely involved in the detection and investigation
of serious drug
and fraud matters. These are areas
where there is significant potential for serious corruption.
That potential is likely to increase
rather than decrease
because there is a growing acknowledgment, including
from the AFP and the NCA, that global crime is presenting
new and demanding challenges that are having an impact
on Australia especially in these areas. Wishful thinking
or platitudes
will not make the problems or potential
problems go away. Opportunities for corruption and
other forms of misconduct will not
totally disappear
not even with significant improvements to police integrity
and culture.
[T]he potential for corruption is an intrinsic and
indivisible feature of modern policing, closely linked
to, and deriving from,
the essential role of the police
in society.
3.68 Indeed there
is evidence that corruption and its forms can adapt
to and indeed co-opt intended anti-corruption strategies.
3.69 What is needed
is to sustain vigilance and attention to the issue
and to adapt policies and strategies and structures
accordingly.
Corruption and other serious misconduct have to be
met by a co-ordinated and systematic response that
monitors and improves law
enforcement cultures, practices
and procedures but also has in place an effective,
credible and responsive system for investigating
and corruption. The Commission believes that the issues
are of such importance that the best possible response
demanded. The Commission considers that the NIIC
as discussed in detail in chapter 5 constitutes the
optimal response in terms
of effectiveness, credibility
and cost efficiency.
3.70 The Commission
proposes that the NIIC should have a dedicated anti-corruption
division which is devoted to detecting and
investigating
corruption. That is, it must have a substantial pro-active
stance. It should have the power to audit or review
any AFP or NCA office and to gain full access to information.
The anti-corruption division should be established
by the NIIC
Act and its charter should be to detect
and investigate corruption. It should act to discover
corruption, potential corruption
or likely avenues
for the development of corruption. This work would
be done in conjunction with the AFP particularly with
IID and its Audit and Security Branch and also with
the NCA. Risk management strategies could be developed
to identify, change
or monitor high risk corruption
areas, personnel and processes.
3.71 The NIIC would
have access to the complete flow of information about
complaints to enable it to provide comprehensive responses
to individual complaints but also to clearly identify
trends and issues of policy and procedure. It would
streamline administrative
arrangements. It would clearly
be at the apex of the complaints systems for the AFP
and the NCA. The system would be holistic
the fragmentation and divided responsibility under
the current system.

4. Special issues for the NCA
Introduction
need for a formal complaints system including external

and members

Introduction
4.1 This chapter
discusses some special issues that arise when considering
complaints against the NCA. Those issues relate to
its mixed and changing workforce and its joint operations
with other law enforcement agencies. The chapter also
discusses which
agency should provide external review
of complaints against the NCA and, in particular, considers
the arguments for the Inspector-General
of Intelligence
and Security, the Commonwealth Ombudsman or the Commission's
proposed National Integrity and Investigations Commission
(NIIC). The NCA's disciplinary process is discussed
in chapter 6 where a separate disciplinary code for
the NCA is recommended.
for a formal complaints system including external review
4.2 The Commission
is strongly of the view that there must be a formal
complaints system for the NCA that includes effective
external review. This view has been overwhelmingly
endorsed during the Commission's consultations and
in submissions received.
The NCA's submission has said
that such a system is necessary to ensure public confidence
and accountability.
Draft recommendation
The need for a formal complaints system including external
Draft recommendation 4.1.
The NCA should have a formal
complaints system established under legislation and
providing effective external participation and
be subject to the NCA complaints system?
4.3 The NCA has
a mixed workforce consisting of a number of categories
of personnel. Each category is defined under the Act
to mean a member of staff
primarily to ensure that the secrecy provisions for
the unauthorised disclosure of information apply in
every case.
Chairperson
and members
4.4 The NCA is constituted
by the Chairperson and members who are statutory office
holders. Members and the Chairperson hold office
a maximum of four years.
4.5 Under the NCA
Act, staff of the Authority fall within one of the
following categories.
(a) Those employed under the Public Service Act
A majority of NCA staff are appointed and employed
Public Service Act 1922 (
and the NCA Chairperson has all the powers of a Departmental
Secretary under that Act.
(b) Consultants
The Chairperson may employ consultants according to
terms and conditions as determined from time to time.
The argument for making consultants subject to an
NCA complaints regime is that if they are not included
there may be no alternative
method of complaint and
discipline in relation to them. In some cases where
the person is a member of a professional organisation
there may be a system of complaint and discipline but
this system may be removed from the control and responsibility
(c) Seconded personnel
Some personnel may be seconded to the NCA from federal,
State or Territory police services and other agencies
such as the Australian
Securities Commission, the Australian
Taxation Office, AUSTRAC, the Australian Customs Service
and State revenue agencies for
varying periods of time.
All police seconded to the NCA have the status of 'members
of staff of the Authority' under the Act. The NCA has
entered into
memoranda of understanding with the AFP,
the Northern Territory and all States except South
The Memoranda cover staffing and the continuation
of the Commissioner's and members' legislative and
operational responsibilities,
including discipline.
A common period of secondment is two years.
All State Police seconded to the NCA are subject to
the discipline and internal investigation procedures
of their home forces.
The Queensland, WA, NSW, Victorian
and Tasmanian Police Commissioners have agreed that
complaints against members seconded to
the NCA will
be forwarded to the Chairperson in the first instance.
Where the matter warrants formal investigation, the
Chairperson
will forward the details of the complaint
to the relevant police service. This means that the
NCA Chairperson is responsible
for making an initial
decision about whether a complaint warrants further
investigation.
Northern Territory (NT) seconded police are subject
to the internal investigation procedures of the NCA
or the State Police in
the jurisdiction where the complaint
was made. The results of the investigation are then
forwarded to the NT Police for any disciplinary
other action. The NT Commissioner has agreed that complaints
against members of NT police seconded to the NCA in
to their duties with the NCA are forwarded
to the Chairperson of the NCA in the first instance.
A copy of the initial complaint
is also forwarded to
the Commander Internal Audit and Review Division in
the NT Police. Complaints are investigated by either
officers of the NCA or by police officers from the
investigation has
been finalised, details of the outcome
and a copy of the investigation file are forwarded
to the Commander Internal Audit and
Review Division.
(d) Legal counsel
Some legal practitioners assisting the NCA may be appointed
as consultants.
It is possible that during litigation, counsel may
be the subject of complaint. If the complaint concerns
an aspect of professional
practice then the relevant
Bar Association and/or Law Society may become involved
and investigation of the complaint and disciplinary
action may ensue.
(e) Task forces
Officers of the AFP, State and Territory police services
and other agencies may be members of an NCA task force
under the NCA
and are consequently defined as members of staff of
Task forces are often established to investigate allegations
of significant and wide-scale organised criminal activity
that crosses
State and Territory boundaries and involves
a variety of criminal activity such as drugs, money
laundering, extortion and violence.
The NCA has advised
that it keeps a list of the membership of a task force
for each operation but there is no consolidated register
of members of all task forces. The total number changes
frequently as members are added or removed and as operations
or end. The total might be in the range of
No formal arrangements have been made with these agencies
who are task force
members. It has been the NCA's practice
to request the home forces of police against whom complaints
have been made to conduct
investigations.
Task force members remain under the control and direction
of their home agency and may not be subject to the
day to day control
of the NCA. Some task forces may
last for long periods of time, for example, for three
years. Some members of a task force may
have only intermittent
roles (for example, passing on information) and no
routine contact with the operation. For some individual
operations there may be federal, State or Territory
police or other professionals who are involved but
who are not officially
members of the task force.
of NCA staff
4.6 NCA staff are
currently in categories (a), (b), (c) and (e) as described
above. As at 30 June 1995 there was a total of 399
staff including the Chairperson and members. This total
included 112 seconded police with 50 of those AFP members.
uniform system of investigations desirable
4.7 Under the current
fragmented approach complainants may well be confused
the NCA is 'passing the buck' if the NCA refers matters
to the home agency. There is also potential duplication
of investigation
by federal and State or Territory
bodies. For example, one complaint may concern officers
from a large number of agencies scattered
across Australia.
Under the current approach it is possible that for
such a complaint each agency will conduct its own separate
investigation including interviewing the same witnesses
and gathering the same evidence with attendant extensive
accommodation costs.
4.8 The Commission
is of the view that the Chairperson, NCA members and
all staff members should be subject to the NCA complaints
system. This single system would provide the clearest
line of procedure, accountability and consistency.
Seconded police, consultants,
legal practitioners and
task force members would only be subject to the NCA
complaints procedure in relation to their work for
the NCA. For example, Task Force members from a State
or Territory agency would only be subject to the NCA
complaints procedure
in their capacity as a member
of that Task Force. The legislation could specify the
range of conduct covered by providing that
that may be subject to the operation of the NCA complaints
provisions is that action in performance or purported
performance of the person's powers or functions under
the NCA Act or action arising directly or indirectly
in the course of
the person's employment or engagement
under the NCA Act. 'Employment or engagement' would
be defined to cover all possible categories
'Action' would include a failure to act. The relevant
State and Territory home agencies could introduce complementary
legislation to make it clear that their officers who
are staff members of the NCA by virtue of the NCA Act
are, while performing
their NCA functions, covered
by the NCA complaints legislation.
4.9 If a uniform
system were implemented all personnel including seconded
staff, consultants, legal counsel and task force members
should be advised as soon as practicable that they
are subject to the NCA complaints system. This should
be done where possible
before people become staff under
the NCA Act. Their obligations should be made clear
to them before they join. Where there is
of engagement then a term of the contract should be
that the person acknowledges that he or she is subject
NCA complaints procedure. In other cases this
could be readily achieved by a simple information sheet
designed for each category
Draft recommendation
A uniform system of investigations desirable
Draft recommendation 4.2.
There should be one uniform
NCA complaints system applying to NCA members and all
its staff that is, to (a) members and the Chairperson,
(b) staff employed under the Public Service Act, (b)
seconded staff, (c) consultants, (d) legal practitioners,
(e) members
of task forces.
The external
4.10 The issues
paper identified the Commonwealth Ombudsman or the
Inspector-General of Intelligence and Security or as
two of the main choices for providing external
overview. The current role of the Ombudsman in complaints
against the AFP is discussed
in chapter 2.
4.11 Under the
Inspector-General of Intelligence and Security Act
(Cth) the Inspector-General investigates complaints
against Australia's five intelligence and security
agencies with broadly
similar powers of investigation
and recommendation as the Commonwealth Ombudsman.
4.12 The Commission
considers that neither the Ombudsman nor the Inspector-General
is the most appropriate agency to deal with
against the NCA. Instead the Commission prefers that
the Commission's proposed National Integrity and Investigations
Commission (NIIC) should have this responsibility to
deal with both complaints against the AFP and the NCA.
The general reasons
for this preference are discussed
in chapter 3. The broad issues raised and the objectives
for the AFP complaints system and
the NCA complaints
system are essentially the same. The AFP and the NCA
have differences in composition, focus and powers but
there are also some broad similarities. Both are national
law enforcement agencies with a primary function being
the investigation
of organised crime. However there
are also specific reasons why the NIIC should be the
external agency to investigate and review
against the NCA.
4.13 The NCA is
a body with significant coercive powers and a broad
charter of responsibility. Any agency that provides
review of complaints against it must have
the status, investigative powers and capacity to be
able to provide effective review.
Community confidence
is most likely to be increased by the introduction
of a specialist and focussed external agency that can
provide searching and thorough scrutiny. The Commission
considers that it is the NIIC that is most likely to
have those characteristics.
Moreover, if it is accepted
that the AFP requires the introduction of the NIIC
it means that there are efficiency and cost effectiveness
factors that support the NIIC also providing external
review of the NCA.
4.14 The Ombudsman
may experience difficulties in devoting sufficient
resources to complaints against the NCA. The Ombudsman's
record in relation to the AFP suggests that this would
be a significant concern. Moreover, as explained in
chapter 3 the Commission
considers that the Ombudsman
with her traditional role of reviewing defective administration
is not the most appropriate agency
to be investigating
complaints against the NCA where some complaints may
concern actions taken by police officers or the improper
use of coercive powers.
4.15 The limitations
of the Inspector-General's office in this context are
that it is a small agency with currently little resourcing
for the conduct of intensive investigations. It would
require additional resources to undertake supervision
of complaints against
the NCA. Moreover, its current
role has been limited to complaints against the intelligence
organisations. While it is true that
the NCA has an
intelligence gathering role it also has similarities
with the role of the AFP. The NCA is not a specialist
intelligence
agency in the nature of ASIO or ASIS.
It is primarily concerned with organised crime and
law enforcement and, for example, has
personnel who
are police or others who are experienced and trained
in aspects of law enforcement, not espionage or national
security. It could be broadly said that the NCA is
an organisation falling somewhere between an intelligence
agency and a police
4.16 The Commission
considers that the NIIC would be able to build up reasonably
quickly a high profile in the community for independent
monitoring and review of federal law enforcement activity.
In contrast the Inspector-General of Intelligence and
Security may
be less widely known and is more likely
to be regarded as specialising in security and espionage
and not in investigating a wider
range of complaints.
The NIIC with its separate divisions relating to complaints
and corruption would be able to offer greater
accessibility
to the general community for the full range of complaints
than the Inspector-General.
4.17 The Commission
also considers that the NIIC with its specific focus
on complaints and corruption in relation to law enforcement
and with its public status as a standing royal commission
is less likely than either the Ombudsman or the Inspector-General
to have difficulties in handling attempts at abuse
of its process. It may also be better placed than the
Ombudsman or the Inspector-General
to respond to any
attempt by organised crime to use threats, intimidation
or inducements for corruption.
Draft recommendation
The external agency
Draft recommendation 4.3.
The National Integrity and
Investigations Commission should provide external scrutiny
and participation in the system of complaints
the NCA and its staff.
the IGC be part of the NCA complaints system?
4.18 The Inter-Governmental
Committee (IGC) comprises Commonwealth, State and Territory
Ministers responsible for administering
the NCA Act
in their jurisdictions. The Commission considers that
it would be inappropriate for the IGC to be part of
complaints system. Those Ministers are accountable
to their respective Parliaments.
the PJC be part of the NCA complaints system?
4.19 The Parliamentary
Joint Committee on the NCA (PJC) is made up of five
Members of the House of Representatives and five Senators.
Its main role is to monitor and review the performance
by the Authority of its functions and to report to
both Houses of Parliament.
It is possible that in fulfilling
this role that the PJC or its members could be the
subject of a complaint. The possibility
being the subject of complaints may be increased if
the PJC were to monitor and review the NIIC as recommended
the Commission in chapter 5. The Commission does
not propose that the PJC should be part of the NCA
complaints system. It should
not be regarded as part
of the NCA. The question of whether any complaints
handling process is necessary for the PJC is a matter
for Parliament.
Draft recommendation
Should the IGC and the PJC be part of the NCA complaints
Draft recommendation 4.4.
The IGC and the PJC should
not be part of the NCA complaints system.
The secrecy
4.20 The secrecy
provision of the NCA Act makes it an offence to directly
or indirectly disclose information relating to the
performance of duties. This provision might be construed
to apply to cases where an NCA officer wishes to make
a complaint about
the activities of the NCA or a member
of its staff. This would seem to be an unwarranted
extension of the basic policy behind
the secrecy provision.
To prevent complaints by the use of the secrecy provision
might actually allow wrong-doers to escape investigation
and penalty.
4.21 The Commission
considers that an exception should be made to the NCA
Act secrecy provision so that it does not apply to
a person making a complaint to the NCA or to the NIIC
or any person designated to be able to receive complaints
the appointed investigator of the complaint so that
the investigator has full access to relevant information.
Draft recommendation
Exceptions to the secrecy provision
Draft recommendation 4.5.
There should be an exception
to the NCA Act secrecy provision so that it does not
a person making a complaint to the NCA or to the NIIC
or to any person designated to be able to receive complaints
the appointed investigator of a complaint so that the
investigator has full access to relevant information.
internal unit unnecessary
4.22 Unlike the
AFP the NCA does not have an internal investigations
division. The Commission does not consider that it
establish one. Given the small number and the
nature of the complaints a separate unit would not
be cost effective. What will
be necessary is for some
NCA officers to be designated as 'Complaints officers'
whose primary task in relation to complaints
be to liaise with the NIIC and to arrange 'in-house'
resolution of minor complaints.
Draft recommendation
Dedicated internal unit unnecessary
Draft recommendation 4.6.
The NCA should not establish
an internal investigations unit or division but may
need to designate some officers as those who
accept and record complaints, to arrange informal resolution
of minor complaints and to liaise with the NIIC.
s 47. The NCA has advised the Commission that 285 out
of 399 NCA staff are public servants.
s 49 and 58.
According to the NCA the memorandum with South Australia
is in draft form and is likely to be formally entered

number of AFP seconded officers was provided to the
Commission by the NCA.

with complaints concerning the NCA and ch 6 discusses
the proposed disciplinary
regime for the NCA.

Introduction
5.1 This chapter
examines the role, functions and resources of the National
Integrity and Investigations Commission (NIIC), the
proposed new body to investigate or manage/supervise
the investigation of complaints against the AFP and
the NCA. The chapter
concludes by considering a number
of possible initiatives that would complement the NIIC's
role and jurisdiction. One initiative
would involve
a national resourcing arrangement for federal, State
and Territory investigations involving law enforcement
including accreditation for investigators
and arrangements for the sharing of information and
facilities. Another possible initiative
would be for
the NIIC to have jurisdiction about complaints and
corruption in respect of other federal agencies which
law enforcement functions. This would be a very
significant extension of its role.
functions and structure
5.2 Two key functions
of the NIIC would be to investigate serious complaints
against the AFP and the NCA and to detect and investigate
corruption within those two agencies. It would be able
to conduct or order investigations in response to a
complaint, report
or other form of information or on
its own initiative.
5.3 It would receive
information about all complaints made against the AFP
and the NCA and would then make decisions about the
most appropriate means of investigation and the most
appropriate persons or agencies to conduct those investigations.
have wide investigative and review functions
in respect of complaints and information about corruption.
The NIIC would have the
ultimate power in determining
who should investigate and would in every case have
available to it the power of control and direction
of the investigation.
5.4 The NIIC would
also produce and analyse data about complaints and
corruption and provide reports about trends.
divisions: complaints and corruption
5.5 There would
be two main divisions within the NIIC
the Office of the Commissioner for Complaints and
the Office for Anti-Corruption.
5.6 This organisational
distinction between complaints and corruption is necessary
because the two require different approaches.
5.7 The handling
of complaints is likely to
be reactive to individual complainants
be concerned with day to day policing
have a significant focus on accessibility and service
provision to complainants.
5.8 Anti-corruption
is likely to
be pro-active to be effective
rely on diverse intelligence information and not merely
a formal complaint
be secretive
require surveillance and sophisticated methods of investigation,
often conducted on a covert basis.
5.9 The Commission
envisages that these two divisions would be physically
separated within the NIIC but share infrastructure
and an information system. Both divisions would have
access to the NIIC database subject to any special
security requirements,
particularly those relating
to the Office for Anti-Corruption.
Legislation
5.10 The NIIC would
be established by its own Act and that Act would provide
that the NIIC has its own corporate personality.
Commission considers that the complaints provisions
for both the AFP and the NCA should be included in
this Act. This would
mean that all the main elements
of the complaints systems would be included in the
one piece of legislation making it easier
to locate the relevant law and to understand how the
systems work. The Act should be drafted in plain English
should ensure that it is easy for a reader to identify
and follow the complaints process.
5.11 The NIIC would
have a full-time Chairperson, a full-time Commissioner
for Complaints and at least one part-time Commissioner.
The legislation should allow the Chairperson to determine
the exact number of part-time Commissioners according
to the demands
of work and the available skills and
interests of the Commissioners.
5.12 The Chairperson
should be a retired or seconded superior court judge
or a person qualified for appointment as a judge who
has special relevant experience. The Commission considers
that the position of Chairperson requires a person
with judicial
or extensive legal experience and qualifications
because of the coercive powers available to the NIIC
and the great responsibility
placed on it to be effective
and to act according to the law. The Chairperson should
be able to delegate to the full-time Commissioner
a part-time Commissioner any of his or her functions
5.13 The Commissioner
for Complaints should desirably be a person with extensive
experience in human rights or broad based experience
in community affairs such as community legal centres
or consumer advocacy. The part-time Commissioner should
desirably have
extensive experience in police or other
investigative work.
5.14 The NIIC would
be based in Canberra since the bulk of its work would
relate to local AFP community policing. However, the
NIIC should have arrangements with the AFP and the
NCA to use their regional facilities where appropriate
and consideration
should be given to establishing arrangements
for the use of facilities and resources with other
Commonwealth, State and Territory
law enforcement and
review agencies.
Draft recommendations
Role, functions and structure
Draft recommendation 5.1.
A National Integrity and
Investigations Commission (NIIC) should be established
to investigate or manage/supervise the investigation
of complaints against the AFP and the NCA.
Draft recommendation 5.2.
The new legislation to establish
the NIIC should include the complaints provisions for
both the AFP and the NCA. The Act should
the National Integrity and Investigations Commission
(Australian Federal Police and National Crime Authority
Complaints)
Draft recommendation 5.3.
The Act should be drafted
in plain English and should ensure that it is easy
for a reader to identify and follow the complaints
Draft recommendation 5.4.
The NIIC should have two
main divisions
the Office of the Commissioner for Complaints and
the Office for Anti-Corruption.
Draft recommendation 5.5.
The NIIC would have a full-time
Chairperson, a full-time Commissioner for Complaints
and at least one part-time Commissioner.
5.15 The Commission
envisages a staff of about 30 people. There would be
a modest Secretariat and a core of people with extensive
investigative and legal experience in policing or other
investigative work. The current estimate of the Commission
would have the capacity to conduct their own investigations
or manage or supervise
other agencies' investigations.
All staff would be engaged on fixed term contracts
with staggered exits to promote a clear focus
organisation's key objectives, to avoid 'bureaucratisation'
and to foster staff independence from the agencies
are investigated. The NIIC would be able to second
personnel and to engage consultants. All staff would
be subject to initial
integrity tests and then subject
to subsequent checks.
5.16 The NIIC would
be federally funded. The Commission's preliminary estimate
is that funding would be in the order of three
a half to four million dollars annually. This amount
includes any additional resources for the AAT if it
were to provide
review of disciplinary decisions instead
of the Federal Police Disciplinary Tribunal (FPDT).
The Commission considers that that amount would be
in the order of the current expenditure in relation
to the FPDT.
5.17 The Commission
considers that a substantial part of the NIIC's funding
could be obtained from off-sets from the current system,
by greater efficiencies and appropriate contributions
by the NCA and the States and Territories in relation
to the operations
of the NCA. Off-sets would be available
by removing the Ombudsman's supervisory role in this
area, some reduction in the AFP's
role and the abolition
of the FPDT. Efficiencies would result from the consolidation
of the complaints and investigative functions
AFP's IID and ISA, the more effective use of informal
resolution and a more streamlined system of review
of disciplinary
decisions. The NCA and the States and
Territories should make contributions to funding according
to the expected role of the
NIIC in relation to the
5.18 The Commission
in attempting to estimate funding and off-sets has
sought information from the Ombudsman and the AFP about
their current levels of staffing and resources for
dealing with complaints against the AFP. The Commission
considers that the
information so far provided and
as described immediately below is not sufficient to
provide a completely accurate account of
current spending.
from the Ombudsman's current role
5.19 The Ombudsman
receives a single line appropriation from the Commonwealth
Government that does not break down the amounts
be spent on specific functions. Her total budget for
1994-95 was approximately $7.6 million.
The Ombudsman has advised the Commission that she
is unable to break down the expenditure against parts
of her jurisdiction.
The best estimate that the Ombudsman
could provide in relation to the AFP was as follows:
5.20 The Ombudsman
also advised that the reduction in 1994/95 reflects
the more streamlined approach to AFP complaints not
investigation including the use of informal
resolution.
5.21 The Ombudsman
has about seven staff who work regularly in the area
although no person works exclusively on these matters.
The Ombudsman believes that working across more than
one area encourages multi-skilling. One section of
those who work on AFP
complaints specialises in complaints
originating in the ACT and the other on national policing
and Customs and Defence.
5.22 The Ombudsman
has also advised that her office has recently been
able to conduct more of her own primary investigations
because she has been able to draw from a major projects
fund granted to her office. However, she says that
in the past there
have not been the resources available
to conduct her own investigations. For example, to
review investigations carried out by
the IID in relation
to the AIDEX demonstration she had to request a special
grant for a two year period of $175 000.
5.23 The Commission
considers that, as a preliminary estimate which is
based on the information currently provided, 95% of
Ombudsman's average expenditure on the AFP complaints
system over the last three years, that is, approximately
$590 000 could
be made available to the NIIC.
5.24 The Commission

approximately $380 000 annually.

AFP complaints

for the rest of the AFP complaints function. The Commission

from the ACT contribution of $380 000 could be available
for the NIIC.
This is a matter that would require further

5.27 The Commission
considers that significant off-sets could be found
from the AFP. This assessment is based upon a number
of considerations. The main consideration is the extent
to which investigations conducted by the NIIC staff
in cases of very
serious misconduct and corruption
would relieve the AFP of a significant part of its
current investigative role. The management
or oversight
by the NIIC of the AFP's investigations into complaints
or allegations of corruption would also reduce the
on AFP resources. The adoption of an effective
workplace resolution scheme across Australia would
also significantly reduce IID
expenditure because for
those matters the IID should have no role. The NIIC
would provide any auditing or review of that process.
There are other areas of the AFP system that could
contribute to the NIIC's funding.
5.28 The proposed
amalgamation of ISA and the IID would result in a saving
of funds by reducing administrative costs. The Commission
notes that there are now currently about 50 personnel
in ISA of whom 24 are involved in investigative roles
and 22 personnel
in the IID.
The Commission is of the view that
prima facie
appears to be a larger number of staff than should
be necessary to perform the allotted tasks.
5.29 Another factor
which should reduce IID and ISA costs and use of resources
would be the effective implementation of a comprehensive
anti-fraud and anti-corruption plan as recommended
in chapter 7. In addition, an effective pro-active
plan with integrity testing,
risk assessment and regular
review should reduce the scope for fraud and corruption.
An effective and comprehensive risk management
for IID investigations should result in a more cost
efficient use of investigative resources with strategic
of high risk cases.
5.30 The Commission's
draft recommendations for reform to the disciplinary
process should result in savings to the AFP. Fewer
matters should proceed to fully contested hearings
and those that do should be determined more quickly.

availability of funds from the AFP, a figure which

of the current system were to be audited,

5.33 The NCA would

is that this should be in the order of $150 000 per
year. That amount
takes into account a likely rise
in the number of complaints concerning the NCA if the
