the likelihood that some of the complaints involving
the NCA would be complex and require extensive input.
It is an amount
that represents what the Commission
believes the NCA ought to be spending to operate a
fully effective complaints and disciplinary
together with proper anti-fraud and anti-corruption
5.34 The Commission
also considers that the States and the Territories
could make some contribution to funding in relation
complaints concerning NCA activities. The making
of these contributions would be appropriate and fair
given that under the Commission's
draft recommendations
the NIIC would be conducting or managing investigations
concerning State and Territory officers in their
related activities and the NCA would have its own disciplinary
code that could apply to these officers in the context
their work for the NCA. In that sense the proposed
new system would provide a service for the State and
Territory agencies.
5.35 The Commission
considers that the amount the State and Territories
would contribute would not be excessive. There are
number of possible methods for determining appropriate
contributions. Some major alternatives are as follows.
Each jurisdiction could contribute the same amount
on the basis that each benefits from NCA activities
against organised crime
in the sense that organised
crime is a national problem and that in the longer
term each jurisdiction will have cause to be involved
in NCA operations.
Each jurisdiction could contribute on a direct user
pays basis. That is, if the NIIC was investigating
a complaint in relation
to a State or Territory officer,
the relevant home agency would pay for that investigation
or a proportion of it.
A formula that differentiates according to the particular
could be introduced.
One possibility is that the States
and Territories contribute in proportion to their respective
populations as at July 1 each
year. This is the formula
agreed upon in relation to the establishment of the
Standing Committee on Organised Crime and Criminal
Intelligence (SCOCCI).
An alternative formula that might more accurately
reflect use of resources would be to base the contribution
upon relative police
strengths. For example, as at
July 1 of each previous year the combined strengths
of all police forces excluding the AFP could
be determined
and each jurisdiction would contribute in proportion
to its own police force strength as a fraction of the
A combination of these options.
5.36 The Commission's
initial estimate for the combined total of State and
Territory contribution would be about $150 000 per
likely workload
5.37 It is important
to try to assess the likely workload of the NIIC in
estimating its staffing and resources. There are a
number of reasons to suggest that the workload of the
NIIC will not be as heavy as a cursory consideration
might suggest.
5.38 The latest
AFP statistics for 1994-95 indicate that there were
900 complaints made by 596 complainants and 109 allegations.
There were only 596 complainants but 900 complaints
because many complaints and allegations arise out of
the one incident.
The task of investigating and resolving
these related complaints and allegations is thus reduced.
Many in practical terms will
involve the one investigation
and the consideration of the same evidence.
5.39 The level
of complaints about the NCA is about 18 per year, although
this may well increase with the introduction of a formal
system. Nevertheless the workload of the NIIC in connection
with the NCA is unlikely to be significant in terms
of raw numbers
of complaints.
5.40 Only about
half the number of complaints lodged will be investigated
at all. Some will be formally withdrawn and in relation
to others there will be a decision not to investigate
because the complaint lacks substance or credibility,
is trivial or is
too old. Moreover a significant number
of complaints will be dealt with by informal resolution
without the need for investigation.
The Commission
would expect that with the introduction of a national
system for the informal resolution of customer service
or internal management types of complaints the number
of investigated complaints should be reduced with resulting
benefits to
the efficiency of the complaints system.
The Commission anticipates that about 70% of all complaints
and allegations should be
capable of being resolved
informally. In relation to these informally resolved
matters the NIIC will essentially take a monitoring
and auditing role. It will be sent a record of the
matter and will check to see whether the complainant
was satisfied with
the process. Its only other general
function in relation to these matters will be to conduct
random audits to ensure quality
5.41 At the more
serious end of the spectrum where the NIIC involvement
will be at its most significant the available information
suggests the following range of investigations - IID
investigated 276 matters in 1994-5 and ISAD was given
87 references. The
total of investigated matters at
its highest was thus about 370. The Ombudsman's estimate
for a effective complaints system is
that 40 to 50
complaints each year should be investigated externally
and not by the AFP. In approximately 320 cases the
would not be providing the primary input into
the investigation. It would either be managing or oversighting.
In many cases this
would be oversighting which would
mean that the bulk of the 'legwork' and interviewing
would be carried out by the AFP or the
NCA possibly
in co-operation with other agencies. These investigations
would be carried out according to detailed guidelines
determined by the NIIC and the AFP or the NCA. The
NIIC would not be duplicating the investigative facilities
and resources
of the AFP or the NCA. It would not require
its own surveillance equipment. It would direct the
use of AFP and NCA resources
and equipment.
Commission's
assessment of NIIC's funding
5.42 The Commission
considers that a more detailed analysis of the expenditure
of the current system and the proposed NIIC would
necessary to assess more accurately relative costs.
5.43 However, a
summary of the off-sets and efficiencies that the Commission
considers from this initial examination are reasonable
is as follows
States and Territories (excluding the ACT)
5.44 There are
also other cost factors that are more difficult to
quantify but nonetheless necessary to consider. They
to effectiveness and credibility.
5.45 An effective
complaints and anti-corruption system may be much more
cost efficient in the mid to long term than current
approaches because it should reduce the incidence of
corruption and misconduct. Dealing with these problems
after they occur,
particularly when royal commissions
or special inquiries become necessary, rather than
having systems that can prevent, deter
or identify
them early is a false economy at best.
5.46 A credible
complaints, corruption and disciplinary process may
also be cost efficient at the macro level. Effective
enforcement and crime prevention rely heavily on
the co-operation and assistance of the public. Having
a credible complaints
and disciplinary system is an
essential part of ensuring that confidence. An absence
of this co-operation and assistance means
resources must be pumped into investigating crimes
committed and dealing with the consequences of crime.
5.47 The Commission
believes that it is possible that the system that it
proposes could be cost neutral if a thorough analysis
of the operation of the expenditure of the current
system is undertaken and appropriate off-sets and savings
are made. However,
if it were to be found that some
additional funding was necessary either on a regular
basis or in response to special circumstances
as a very extensive investigation involving a high
level of investigative resources, the Commission is
still of the view
that its draft recommendations for
a new system are clearly justified. The defects of
the current systems
and the undoubted importance of the integrity and
accountability of the AFP and the NCA
demonstrate the need for these suggested changes.
The Commission invites comments on staffing and funding
issues in relation to the NIIC.
Investigative
and inquisitorial powers
5.48 The NIIC should
have conferred on it the full range of investigative
and inquisitorial powers of a royal commission. This
should include powers to
enter and search public premises and seize material
summon witnesses to give evidence
pursue contempt of its procedures
obtain and execute listening device warrants
intercept and record telephone conversations and electronic
communications
hold public and private hearings
gain access to taxation records.
5.49 The NIIC would
conduct, manage or supervise three major types of investigations
a full investigation conducted by itself and where
necessary using its range of coercive powers
joint investigations with the AFP or NCA (and where
appropriate and practical including members of other
Commonwealth or State
agencies, particularly law enforcement
agencies) - these investigations would normally be
under the direction and control of
the NIIC and where
necessary the NIIC could use its coercive powers
AFP or NCA conducting internal investigations but those
investigations being managed or supervised
by the NIIC with no use of the NIIC's coercive powers.
5.50 The NIIC would
have the power to make inquiries to determine whether
to undertake an investigation. The NIIC would be able
to change the type of investigation being carried out
where it considered that it was necessary. Thus, for
example, if the
AFP or the NCA were conducting an investigation
the NIIC could at any stage alter that to a joint investigation
or a full NIIC
investigation. This might occur where
the NIIC considered that it was necessary to have its
coercive powers available.
5.51 The NIIC Act
should provide that the NIIC has the power of control
and direction of any investigation. It would have a
discretion in the particular case as to the appropriate
level of its involvement. In some cases it might manage
the investigation,
that is, provide detailed guidance
in the planning and execution of detection or investigation,
including selecting the personnel
involved, the method
of detection or investigation, the witnesses to be
interviewed and the evidence to be gathered. In other
cases it might provide a lower level of guidance, relying
on a system of guidelines prepared by it and progress
reports and
final reports furnished to it. In most
cases the Commission envisages that the level of management
or oversight would be achieved
by agreement with the
AFP or the NCA and that no formal use of the NIIC's
directive powers would be necessary.
5.52 Where the
NIIC finds a person guilty of contempt it would be
able to apply to the Federal Court for that Court to
into the matter and the Court, if satisfied
that the person is guilty of contempt, would punish
the person in the same manner
and to the same extent
if the person was found guilty of that contempt in
relation to proceedings before the Federal Court.
Audit powers
5.53 The NIIC would
have the power to undertake inquiries, examine or audit
any aspect of the AFP or the NCA for the purpose of
discovering whether there is misconduct or circumstances
that may lead to misconduct. This would include examining
and other data.
to recommend action after investigations
5.54 The NIIC would
have the power to make recommendations, give opinions
and make assessments to the AFP or the NCA on the basis
of its investigations or those of agencies which it
has managed or directed. This would include recommendations
for the prosecution
of a person for a criminal offence
or the taking of disciplinary action. The NIIC would
also have the power to go directly to
the DPP with
a brief for a criminal prosecution.
in relation to complainant's remedies
5.55 The Commission
considers that generally it should be a matter for
the complainant to pursue a specific remedy by means
other than the disciplinary proceeding. The main rationale
for investigations into complaints and disciplinary
proceedings is
to ensure the suitability of officers
and to maintain appropriate levels of conduct within
the NCA and the AFP. It is not to
provide redress to
complainants. However, it is clear that there will
be cases where the complainant is unable or unwilling
to pursue an action where nevertheless some form of
redress is appropriate. To take account of this the
Chairperson of the NIIC
should be able to recommend
to the Commissioner of the AFP, the Chairperson of
the NCA or the Attorney-General, where he or she
of the opinion that action should be taken in relation
to a complaint to rectify, mitigate or alter the effects
or omission by the officers of the AFP or
the NCA. This would be a similar power to that of the
Ombudsman under the
Ombudsman Act 1976
and (3), This action may include:
an apology or explanation
financial compensation or restitution of property
a change in decision or course of action.
5.56 Where the
NIIC makes such a recommendation and the AFP Commissioner
or the NCA Chairperson disagrees in the case of the
AFP or the NCA respectively the NIIC should be able
to make a report to the Attorney-General on the matter.
power to examine policies and procedures and make recommendations
5.57 Under the
Complaints Act the Disciplinary Tribunal may inquire
into a matter relating to the AFP that is referred
by the Attorney-General.
This power has not been used. The Commission is of
the view that the NIIC should have a similar power
as the new peak body for
investigating complaints against
the AFP and the NCA. The NIIC legislation should also
specify that the NIIC may make recommendations
Commissioner of the AFP, the Chairperson of the NCA
and the Attorney-General on principles and practice
for these organisations,
including recommendations
AFP or NCA misconduct.
5.58 The NIIC would
not be bound by the rules of evidence and could inform
itself on any matter as it sees fit.

The NIIC should have conferred
on it the full range of investigative and inquisitorial
powers of a royal commission.
Draft recommendation 5.7.
The NIIC Act should provide
that the NIIC has the power of control and direction
of any investigation about the AFP or the NCA
in relation
to a complaint or corruption.
Draft recommendation 5.8.
The NIIC would have the power
to make recommendations, give opinions and make assessments
to the AFP or the NCA on the basis
of its investigations
or those of agencies which it has managed or directed.
Draft recommendation 5.9.
The NIIC may inquire into
a matter relating to the AFP or the NCA that is referred
to it by the Attorney-General.
Accountability
Accountability
to the Government and Parliament
5.59 There is clearly
a need for a body with the NIIC's coercive powers and
with its anti-corruption and complaints functions
have a clear line of accountability to the federal
Government and Parliament. Therefore, in respect of
its overall activities,
the NIIC should be accountable
to the federal Attorney-General who would have portfolio
responsibility for NIIC thus complementing
the Attorney's
portfolio responsibility for the AFP and NCA. The NIIC
should be subject to oversight by the Parliamentary
Committee on the NCA regarding its activities
in relation to the AFP and the NCA.
5.60 The NIIC would
also be required to provide Annual Reports to Parliament
on its operations and effectiveness. It would also
have the power to make a special report to Parliament
at any time.
5.61 Additional
accountability is necessary in relation to complaints
against the NCA as they touch upon members of State
Territory personnel who are members of staff of
the NCA under the NCA Act. In such cases the NIIC should
be required to report
regularly to the Australasian
Council of Police Ministers. In relation to NCA complaints
as they touch generally upon the members
of State or
Territory public services and police forces the NIIC
should also be required to report regularly to the
5.62 The NSW Royal
Commission into the Police has recommended that there
should be an Inspector of the PIC to be a watchdog
to audit functions of the PIC, to deal with complaints
of abuse of power and other forms of misconduct on
the part of its employees
and to report to Parliament
on matters affecting the PIC and its operational effectiveness
5.63 The Commission
does not consider that an Inspector is necessary in
the context of the NIIC. Having an Inspector above
NIIC would be adding an additional layer of review
with its attendant costs and delays and where the ultimate
source of accountability
should be and must be the
Government and Parliament. The NIIC will be required
to report to those institutions on its effectiveness
and on the probity and legitimacy of its conduct. There
would also be review available under the
Administrative
Decisions (Judicial Review) Act 1977
(Cth) of appropriate
NIIC decisions and judicial scrutiny of its coercive
on AFP and NCA officers to report misconduct and evidence
of misconduct
5.64 Every AFP
appointee other than the AFP Commissioner and every
NCA staff member other than the NCA Chairperson would
obliged to report a 'reportable matter' as soon
as practicable to the Commissioner of the AFP or the
Chairperson of the NCA respectively
and to the NIIC.
A 'reportable matter' would mean any information derived
from any source (including a complaint, report, audit,
intelligence source, informant or record) concerning
the commission of, or a reasonable suspicion of, the
commission of a criminal offence by an AFP or NCA officer
a breach of, or the reasonable suspicion of, the breach
of a disciplinary rule by an AFP or NCA officer
conduct, or the reasonable suspicion of conduct, by
an AFP officer or NCA officer that is otherwise contrary
5.65 A wilful or
intentional failure to report such information would
constitute a criminal offence. A negligent or careless
failure would constitute a breach of discipline.
5.66 The Commissioner
of the AFP and the Chairperson of the NCA would be
under the same obligation but must report the matter
only to the NIIC.
5.67 The only exceptions
to these reporting obligations would be those cases
where the officer knew that the matter had already
been reported or it was a matter to be dealt with under
Category C (Customer Service) or D (Internal Management)
where there
would be other reporting obligations to
the designated supervisor.
against the NIIC

be a clear procedure to deal with complaints made against
the NIIC or its officers. The NIIC Act should provide

PJC any complaint of misconduct against the NIIC or
its officers. The Act
should also provide that the
require a report from the Chairperson of the NIIC on
choose not to investigate the matter

appoint its own investigators

to investigate
take any other action it deems appropriate.

Investigative
Amalgamation
of IID and ISA
5.69 In chapter
2 the deficencies of the current demarcation between

Draft recommendation
Draft recommendation 5.13
. The IID and ISA should be
amalgamated to form a new IID.
of complaints
5.70 Classifying
complaints is an essential element in developing appropriate
responses to the full range of complaints. Classifications
should not be used to try to provide rigid definitions
nor in practice could they be. Their main purpose is
to provide effective
streaming of matters so that resources,
particularly investigative resources, are allocated
efficiently and, for example, target
serious matters
for greater attention.
5.71 The characteristics
or criteria for determining which is the appropriate
category for a particular complaint would not be
in the legislation. The legislation would refer to
the NIIC issuing guidelines about categories and refer
to the four
main categories. The NIIC would have the
power to determine the appropriate category for any
particular complaint. Both the AFP
and the NCA would
be under a statutory obligation to give full access
to the NIIC to all its records relevant to the complaint
and the NIIC would have power to interview all personnel
involved. The NIIC would also have power to reclassify
the complaint
to another category or redirect as to
the manner of handling.
5.72 The NIIC would
consult with the AFP and the NCA about the content
of the guidelines. There would be four main categories:
Category A - serious criminality, corruption or significant
public interest
Category B - serious misconduct
Category C - customer service matters
Category D - internal management matters.
- Serious criminality, corruption or significant public
This would include matters
involving serious criminality such as those matters
that would constitute an indictable offence, for example,
murder, the infliction
of grievous bodily harm or serious
injury (eg involving fractured or broken bones or otherwise
requiring hospitalisation), serious
fraud or theft,
blackmail, bribery, dangerous conduct with a firearm,
perjury and falsifying evidence
where the public interest may require an investigation
completely external to the subject agency or with limited
input from the
subject agency.
- Serious misconduct.
These are matters where a less
serious criminal action or significant disciplinary
action might be warranted - for example, assault,
theft, significant dereliction of duty, misdirected
of a warrant.
- Customer service complaints (minor).
This category
constitutes the majority of complaints and includes
minor breaches of police rules and procedures, rudeness,
incivility, technical assault, a perception of threat
or menace, and misunderstandings as to facts and law.
- Internal management matters (minor).
These are matters
where there is no allegation or implication of misconduct
but managerial action is called for.
This would include minor incidents of loss or damage
to police property and absence from duty. Under the
Commission's draft recommendations
for an increasing
managerial role these internal management matters should
be dealt with by management including by performance
appraisal rather than the misconduct process. Few matters
of this nature should come to the direct attention
of the NIIC. However
if they do the NIIC should be
able to refer them back to the respective management
for appropriate action and be able to review
any decision
taken. Procedures for dealing with Category C, B and
A complaints are discussed below in that order which
the increasing level of seriousness.
5.77 The NIIC promulgated
guidelines would prescribe strict time limits to be
complied with and reporting processes to the NIIC
be followed. The Commission considers that a complaint
should be recorded immediately it is received and the
complainant
should be contacted within 24 hours of
the receipt of the complaint. If informal resolution
is appropriate it should be completed
within 14 days.
Mediation should be completed and reported on within
30 days. Preliminary enquiries and a report should
be available within 30
days and full investigations
must be completed and reported on within 90 days.
5.78 A failure
to comply with a time limit should be reported to the
NIIC within 24 hours of that failure unless the NIIC
granted an extension of time. The NIIC would provide
comment in its reports to Parliament on failures to
meet time limits.
with Category C complaints (minor)
5.79 These matters
should be suitable for informal resolution by conciliation
or mediation.
The current guidelines of the AFP pilot workplace resolution
scheme in the ACT are a starting point for considering
the content
of the guidelines for dealing with Category
C complaints. The ACT pilot workplace resolution guidelines
provide that workplace
resolution should be used for:
complaints relating to acts of incivility such as demeanour,
discourtesy, rudeness or abruptness
complaints relating to misunderstandings of facts or
of law that may be resolved by explanation
complaints based on a misunderstanding of police practices
or procedures that may be resolved by explanation
a complaint made by a person who has an apparent mental
dysfunction or is otherwise disturbed or obsessive
and, the complaint
has either been made before or appears,
by its nature, to be without substance and consistent
with the complainant's apparent
state of mind
a complaint alleging a less than serious neglect of
duty, including loss or misuse of property or equipment,
failure to respond
promptly or to provide adequate
service, and other like matters
less than serious traffic complaints
with the approval of the IID, complaints concerning
the use of minor force associated with an arrest or
other lawful police conduct
that may include mere jostling,
pushing and shoving in the execution of duty - without
any intended features such as intimidation
or attempts
to obtain a confession - but excludes intentional assaults.
5.80 A major goal
of these Category C guidelines would be to ensure that
minor complaints are immediately acted upon by supervisors
and put on a fast track to a mediated outcome.
5.81 Minor complaints
would not await registration with the NIIC or confirmation
by the NIIC of a classification as a Category
C complaint
before informal resolution is commenced.
5.82 The NIIC would
receive a record of all Category C complaints including
a description of the nature of the complaint and the
results of the informal resolution. It would also have
power to review the process and outcome but would rarely
conduct any
of its own enquiries or investigations
into individual complaints in this Category except
for routinely contacting complainants
to see if they
were satisfied with the outcomes. The NIIC would conduct
random audits to test the effectiveness and probity
of the system.
with Category B complaints (serious misconduct)
5.83 Where a complaint
received directly by the AFP or NCA appears under the
guidelines to be a Category B complaint, the AFP
NCA would take no action in relation to the complaint
until the complaint has been registered with the NIIC
and the NIIC
has given directions as to its handling.
This would be subject to any requirements on the AFP
and the NCA to preserve evidence.
Where a delay in
handling a Category B complaint until registration
and consideration by NIIC might prejudice the outcome
the complaint, the AFP or NCA should be able to
seek directions from the NIIC regarding immediate interim
B complaints involving the AFP

management or supervision of the

particularly law enforcement agencies
- these investigations would be subject to the appropriate

and where necessary the NIIC could use its coercive
investigating the matter directly itself (with its

if the complaint
concerned the Commissioner of the AFP or a Deputy Commissioner
referring a complaint that concerns the AFP generally
to the Commissioner of the AFP for consideration and
5.85 The majority
of B complaints are likely to be dealt with by the
AFP conducting an internal investigation but under
management or supervision of the NIIC. The IID

The Commission proposes
that the investigation function
of the AFP's Internal Security and Audit (ISA) would

extensions to the NIIC's work and jurisdiction
approach to investigations
5.113 It is increasingly
common in Australian jurisdictions for one police service
to request the assistance of other police services
in the conduct of a major investigations concerning
serious police misconduct within that service. For
example, the AFP is
assisting the Western Australian
Police in the investigation of WA police internal investigations
into the Argyle diamond case.
This external input into
investigations can be very valuable because it gives
greater credibility to the independence of the
investigation.
Every service in some circumstances might want to consider
or use this option. At the moment these arrangements
are entered into on an ad hoc, informal basis.
5.114 The Commission
is of the view that this process of interchange should
be given a more formal basis. The Commission proposes
that a body such as the Australian Police Ministers
Council could develop a national scheme for investigations
including accreditation
for investigators with appropriate
skills and integrity checks and arrangements for the
interchange or sharing of information,
facilities,
equipment and other resources. Leasing agreements as
to equipment and facilities might be one option. The
as a major federal investigator should have a
consultative role in the development and implementation
of this process.
Draft recommendation
Possible extensions to the NIIC's work and jurisdiction
Draft recommendation 5.21.
A body such as the Australian
Police Ministers Council could develop a national scheme
for investigations including accreditation
for investigators
with appropriate skills and integrity checks and arrangements
for the interchange or sharing of information,
facilities,
equipment and other resources. The NIIC should have
a consultative role in the development and implementation
this process.
for further federal jurisdiction
5.115 A number
of federal agencies other than the NCA and the AFP
are involved in aspects of law enforcement and may
similar investigative and enforcement functions.
For example, the Australian Securities Commission has
responsibility for investigating
offences under the
corporations legislation. It conducts investigations,
including surveillance and public hearings. Other agencies
with investigative powers and a role in dealing with
criminal offences include the Australian Customs Service,
the Australian
Taxation Office, the Department of Social
Security, the Health Insurance Commission and the Australian
Competition and Consumer
Commission.
5.116 The Commission's
terms of reference extend only to a consideration of
systems for the AFP and the NCA. However, it may be
that consideration should be given to the NIIC having
all of these other
agencies when they exercise any
law enforcement powers. This would be on the basis
that the same general issues are raised in
considering individual civil liberties and the need
for practical and effective law enforcement. Each agency
contribute to the NIIC's funding in proportion
to its likely involvement with the NIIC's functions.
This extended federal role
for the NIIC might assist
to achieving an appropriate and consistent federal
approach to complaints and corruption and do so
the most cost efficient way.
The Commission welcomes comments on possible extensions
of the NIIC's jurisdiction.
uniform Australian law enforcement complaints and disciplinary
5.117 Each Australian
with complaints against its law enforcement agencies.
It is argued in chapter 7 in the context of preventing
the causes of complaints and corruption that a national
standard and
system for many aspects of law enforcement
might be desirable. A system that provided one uniform
complaints and disciplinary
system might well have
advantages in providing a cost effective and integrated
response to problems that often transcend State
Territory borders. This system would be more feasible
if there was in place a national standard for recruitment
and training
and for core professional competencies
as discussed in chapter 7. The introduction of one
uniform complaints and disciplinary
system would require
considerable consultation and agreement between the
federal, State and Territory Governments and their
law enforcement agencies together with the need for
uniform legislation. The Commission in the context
of this inquiry is simply
highlighting it as a potential
option for the future.
The Commission invites comments on the possibility
of a uniform Australian law enforcement complaints
and disciplinary system.

Introduction
6.1 This chapter
discusses the current disciplinary processes of the
AFP and the NCA and other options available to management
to deal with poor or inadequate performance or misconduct.
It proposes a significantly different approach to disciplinary
proceedings.
This would involve a change from the current
quasi-criminal, para-military model to a new approach
based on managerial responsibility
and administrative
review. As a consequence the Federal Police Disciplinary
Tribunal would be abolished and merits review would
be provided by the Administrative Appeals Tribunal
(AAT). The chapter also examines the use of ADR, disciplinary
sanctions and providing information to complainants.
non-disciplinary responses to misconduct or poor performance
6.2 Currently there
are a number of non-disciplinary options available
to the AFP Commissioner to respond to employee unsuitability,
poor or inadequate performance or misconduct. The failure
to re-appoint and the early ending of appointments
can be relevant
to cases where an AFP appointee is
subject to complaints whether substantiated or unsubstantiated.
term appointments
6.3 Since 1990 the
AFP has had a legislative system of fixed term appointments
of generally five years. This provides a means
the AFP can assess an employee's suitability as the
date for consideration of re-appointment approaches.
AFP Regulation
5 prescribes the minimum standards for
appointment or re-appointment. For re-appointment to
occur the Commissioner must be positively
that the person is suitable for re-appointment. The
person must meet the requirements of competence and
qualifications
to hold a position including that the
person is of 'good character and reputation' and meets
other relevant criteria for suitability
for the position.
Performance
6.4 The AFP also
has a performance appraisal system for all staff. The
Commission's consultations indicate that the current
performance appraisal system is not being used as effectively
as it might be so that it does provide a genuine and
appraisal of performance. It is suggested
that some supervisors have tended not to formally rate
a person's performance as inadequate
in a particular
area when objectively that was the appropriate conclusion.
The Commission understands that the performance appraisal
system is now under review by the AFP.
Commission's
views on fixed term appointments and performance appraisal
6.5 Fixed term appointments
and performance appraisal are each valuable tools for
AFP management. They can be used to ensure that
suitable persons are employed and that performance
is effectively monitored and appropriate management
responses are made.
6.6 The AFP should
be able to use fixed term appointments and an effective
performance appraisal system to provide effective incentives
and disincentives to ensure high standards of performance
and employee suitability. For example, re-appointments
in cases where
there are specific problems may be made
for a lesser period and include a probationary period.
The Commission acknowledges that
fixed term appointments
and s 26E have improved work practices and attitudes
in the AFP.
6.7 However, the
Commission is not convinced that the older AFP cultural
attitude that appointment to the AFP is a 'job for
life' has changed sufficiently. Nor is the Commission
convinced that the AFP's older management style that
made decisions on
the assumption of the permanency
of staff has been completely replaced by one that a
member must annually justify his or her
employment.
6.8 The Commissioner
has the power under s 26E of the AFP Act to end an
appointment before its due expiry. The Commissioner
must not make such a determination merely because of
the existence of acts or omissions in respect of which
disciplinary charges
have or could be laid.
6.9 Section 26E
has been used as an alternative to disciplinary proceedings.
Currently s 26E can be used only where non-disciplinary
factors are also evident. Some of the advantages of
using s 26E when compared to the current disciplinary
process are that
it is quicker, more reliable and less
expensive. The Commission's draft recommendations about
the disciplinary process in this
chapter are designed
to improve the speed and effectiveness of that process
and reduce its costs. However, the Commission considers
that the AFP Commissioner should have a wide range
of responses available to deal with poor or inadequate
performance and misconduct.
Management must decide
the appropriate response to a particular case but any
action taken must be according to the relevant provisions
of the legislation and subject to any administrative
review available.
changes to s 26E
6.10 At the time
of writing of this paper the federal Government has
introduced the
Australian Federal Police Amendment
This Bill would amend s 26E with the purpose
of allowing the Commissioner to respond quickly and
effectively to matters involving
serious misconduct.
The Bill defines 'serious misconduct' to mean
corruption, a serious abuse of power, or a serious
dereliction of duty; or
any other seriously reprehensible act or behaviour
by a person, whether acting, or purporting to act,
in the course of his or
her duties as a member or staff
member or not.
The Bill enables the Commissioner to declare that a
person is retired under s 26E because of serious misconduct
that is having,
or is likely to have, a damaging effect
on the morale or self-respect of AFP personnel or the
reputation of the AFP. The Commissioner
must have reasonable
grounds to make such a declaration. The declaration
excludes such a person from the operation of the procedures
for termination of employment provided by the
Relations Act 1988
It is the Government's intention that all other retirees
under s 26E will have access to merits review for unfair
dismissal before
the Industrial Relations Commission
and the Industrial Relations Court (IRC).
6.11 The Commission
supports the proposed amendments to s 26E so that the
Commissioner can provide a quick response to corruption
and other gross misconduct without having to wait for
the outcome of disciplinary or criminal proceedings.
The provision as
amended is part of the 'necessary
armour to combat corruption within the AFP'.
6.12 The Commission
considers that the effectiveness of s 26E might be
increased by two additional changes.
6.13 The first
change is in relation to cases of 'serious misconduct'
under s 26E which
involve a serious abuse by the person of his or her
are committed for a purpose that involved corruption
are committed for the purpose of perverting or attempting
to pervert the course of justice.
6.14 The Commission
is of the view that in those cases the Commissioner
should have the discretion, when satisfied upon reasonable
grounds that the case is appropriate, to seek a reduction
of the employer's contribution to superannuation and
interest on
that amount. A retirement under s 26E for
cases involving serious misconduct that satisfy the
above criteria should not result
automatically, as
at present in the absence of a conviction for a corruption
offence or for certain prescribed disciplinary offences,
in the retiree receiving a full redundancy package.
The view of the Commission is that in such a case the
retiree has forfeited
the right to the total employer
contribution and that the benefit should be reduced
either wholly or partly. It is this employer
contribution
that can be clearly identified as a benefit that the
retiree has derived from the office rather than the
own contributions. The obvious example where
this change would be desirable is where the serious
misconduct has resulted in an
unjust enrichment such
as taking bribes.
This clearly constitutes a serious abuse of the person's
office for illegal gain. The discretion to deduct a
part and not merely
the whole of the employer's contribution
and interest thereon allows the order to be specifically
tailored to the gravity and
circumstances of the particular
6.15 The second
change to s 26E that the Commission favours is for
the merits review for decisions under that section
provided by the AAT rather than as currently
proposed in the Bill before Parliament by the Industrial
Relations Commission and
the Industrial Relations Court
(IRC). This change proposed by the Commission would
include the availability of AAT merits review
decision by the Commissioner to seek a reduction of
the employer contribution to superannuation as discussed
above. Under
the current proposed amendment the Industrial
Relations Commission and the IRC would provide merits
review of all retirements
under s 26E except for those
involving serious misconduct where there would be no
merits review available.
6.16 In the deliberations
did not have the benefit of the Commission's discussion
of AAT review of disciplinary decisions contained in
this paper. The Commission considers that administrative
review by the
AAT would be also be more appropriate
6.17 At paragraphs
6.50-65 of this chapter the Commission explains why
administrative review by the AAT is the most appropriate
mechanism generally for review of disciplinary decisions
by the Commissioner. Decisions by the Commissioner
under s 26E are
similar in process and result to the
Commissioner's disciplinary decisions. They are both
integral aspects of the AFP's managerial
responsibilities.
They both concern the Commissioner assessing facts
and circumstances and considering an officer's suitability
for employment.
6.18 The AFP is
significantly different to many public sector organisations.
It is constituted by a workforce which is engaged
fixed contracts. In the case of the AFP disciplinary
breaches and a failure to perform satisfactorily should
be regarded
fundamentally as breaches of these contracts
of appointment. The industrial relations legal process
with its emphasis on arbitration
and decision-making
across the broad spectrum of employees does not sit
well with the specific statutory appointments for AFP
staff. The adoption of an industrial relations approach
might tend to obscure the basic objectives of the disciplinary
managerial process and perhaps maintain a quasi-criminal
approach with, for the serious cases, the application
of something akin
to the criminal standard of proof.
The Commission is of the view that the attitude that
disciplinary and employment suitability
decisions are
quasi-criminal is more likely to affect an industrial
relations approach than the application of administrative
6.19 The Commission
also considers that the use of the AAT in relation
to s 26E may be more consistent with the Government's
concerns about the law and procedures for unfair dismissal.
The Commission understands that the Government is considering
to these laws and procedures.
6.20 The Commission
is also concerned about duplication of decision making
that might occur with the IRC having jurisdiction.
Under its jurisdiction unlawful terminations are heard
by judicial registrars. A party to the decision may
ask the Court to
review the decision by the judicial
registrar and that review is conducted by a judge.
The rate of applications for review of
unlawful termination
decisions was 27% nationally although in many cases
applications for review are abandoned before being
heard by a judge. The IRC comments that it is too early
to assess accurately the true level or reviews.
The Commission is concerned that this system may add
on another layer of decision making and may encourage
dubious applications
for review.
6.21 The Commission
is also concerned that an industrial relations process
could increase delays and costs. The Commission notes
that the IRC has been unable to achieve a stable workload
level or certainty about the number of judicial officers
The number of claims for unlawful termination is increasing.
6.22 Moreover,
the IRC in response to the large number of unrepresented
claimants that seek an order for unfair dismissal has
opted for a preference for oral evidence rather than
written evidence in the form of affidavits or statements
of evidence.
This is on the basis that the preparation of written
evidence is a skilled process not easily undertaken
by non-lawyers. The
Commission does not anticipate
that personnel of the AFP or the NCA are likely to
be unrepresented or will lack the skill to
written material.
A reliance on oral evidence is likely to prolong hearings
and increase the likelihood of matters running because
each party is
not fully aware of the other party's
case before the hearing has commenced.
Application
6.23 Fixed term
appointments for all staff, a similar power to s 26E
and effective performance appraisal would be valuable
for the NCA. In particular, fixed term appointments
are very important in the law enforcement area to ensure
commitment to the
objectives of the organisation, to
minimise bureaucratisation, to reduce the opportunity
for long term relationships that can
foster corruption
and to give management sufficient flexibility to allocate
staff and resources to meet changing needs. The record
of the AFP with fixed term appointments demonstrates
its value for law enforcement agencies. The introduction
of fixed term
appointments would mean that the NCA
would have an employment regime totally separate from
the Australian Public Service.
Draft recommendations
AFP non-disciplinary responses to misconduct or poor
performance
Draft recommendation 6.1.
The AFP management should
continue to have a wide range of options available
to deal with inadequate or poor performance or misconduct.
This should include fixed term appointments, an effective
performance appraisal system and the capacity to retire
as provided for under s 26E. The Commission
is particularly interested in comments on the AFP and
NCA performance appraisal system
and how, if necessary,
they can be made a more accurate and effective assessment
of performance.
Draft recommendation 6.2.
The Commission endorses the
proposed amendments to s 26E to allow the Commissioner
to declare that a person is retired because
misconduct that is having, or is likely to have, a
damaging effect on the morale or self-respect of AFP
or the reputation of the AFP.
Draft recommendation 6.3.
Section 26E should be amended
so that in cases of serious misconduct involving a
serious abuse by the person of his or her office,
committed for a purpose that involved corruption or
for the purpose of perverting or attempting to pervert
the course of
justice, the Commissioner should have
the discretion to seek a reduction, total or partial,
of the employer's contribution to
superannuation and
interest on that amount. The exercise of this discretion
should be reviewable on the merits by the AAT.
Draft recommendation 6.4.
Section 26E should also be
amended so that merits review for decisions under that
section would be provided by the AAT.
Draft recommendation 6.5.
Consideration should be given
to the NCA having its own employment regime including
fixed term appointments, a power similar
to s 26E and
effective performance appraisal.
disciplinary process
6.24 The following
section describes the AFP's current disciplinary process.
after investigation
6.25 The AFP makes
a decision about what action to take after considering
investigation reports concerning complaints and allegations.
The AFP has recently established a legal branch which
currently provides in-house legal advice in relation
to discipline. If
there is evidence to support a criminal
action it is common practice to refer the matter to
the DPP. If the evidence supports
a disciplinary charge
the AFP may proceed to take disciplinary action or
refer the matter to the Australian Government Solicitor
(AGS) for a formal legal advising. There may be an
exception to this common practice where the potential
criminal matter is
bound up with disciplinary matters
and it would be preferable for them to be heard as
a package of disciplinary charges. This
is a matter
for consultation between the Ombudsman, AFP, DPP and
AGS. The DPP prosecutes criminal charges and the AGS
a disciplinary matter before the Disciplinary
Tribunal. Where the DPP considers there is insufficient
evidence for a criminal
charge, then a disciplinary
charge should be considered.
Action may also be taken as noted above by the AFP
to terminate or not renew a member's fixed term appointment
or to retire a
person under s 26E.
of the Ombudsman after investigation
6.26 The Ombudsman
has certain powers to make recommendations and reports
to the Commissioner after an investigation of a complaint
whether the investigation has been conducted by the
IID, her office or by a special investigator. Where
the Ombudsman has conducted
a special investigation
herself, because it concerns a member of the IID or
for some other reason it is inappropriate for the
to investigate, the Ombudsman can report and can recommend
that an AFP appointee be charged with an offence or
of discipline or be cautioned or admonished.
The same applies to cases where the Ombudsman proposes
to conduct a further investigation after the IID has
conducted an investigation.
6.27 Following
an investigation by IID or the Ombudsman, the Commissioner
may propose that the AFP member be charged, cautioned
or admonished under the Complaints Act.
Where the Ombudsman disagrees with the Commissioner's
proposed action, the Commissioner must refer the matter
to the Attorney-General
to determine what action is
appropriate in the circumstances.
Disciplinary
6.28 The AFP disciplinary
offences are provided for in the AFP (Discipline) Regulations.
AFP appointees are guilty of a disciplinary
if they contravene duties and responsibilities as provided
for in the AFP (Discipline) Regulations. Specific offences
failing to behave with courtesy to the public and failure
to comply with the law and relevant General Orders
and Instructions
disobeying or failing to carry out orders
making deliberately false entries or statements
failing to report loss or damage to property or to
account for property
unauthorised use of firearms
drinking or taking drugs while performing duties
intentionally prejudicing police services by failing
to give prompt attention to one's duties or being absent
from duty without
improperly disclosing information about complaints
or allegations
unauthorised communication of information including
computer programs or information stored in computer
seeking influence to obtain a benefit
soliciting or accepting gifts or gratuities
performing work outside the AFP
failing to give notice of bankruptcy.
6.29 Many of the
charges that are proceeded with are of a general nature,
alleging improper conduct. There are the following
general disciplinary offences
being guilty of disgraceful or improper conduct, either
in an official capacity or otherwise
acting in a disorderly manner or in a manner unbecoming
an AFP appointee
acting in a manner prejudicial to the good order and
discipline of the AFP
acting in a manner that brings, or is likely to bring,
discredit to the reputation of the AFP
being charged with a criminal offence and the court
is satisfied that the charge is proved.
6.30 AFP appointees
who aid or abet the commission of any disciplinary
offence are also deemed guilty of that offence.
role of the Federal Police Disciplinary Tribunal
6.31 The Federal
Police Disciplinary Tribunal (FPDT) was established
Complaints (Australian Federal Police) Act
on 1 May 1982. It consists of a President and
a number of deputy presidents and other members as
appointed by the Governor-General.
The President and
Deputy President must be holders of a judicial office
while other members must either be a magistrate or
a legal practitioner of at least five years standing.
The positions may be part-time.
The Disciplinary Tribunal when sitting is constituted
by one member.
6.32 The Tribunal's
role is to determine promptly and effectively proceedings
instituted in respect of breaches of discipline
members of the AFP and appeals arising out of findings
made or penalties imposed by the Commissioner of Police.
6.33 The Tribunal
may also inquire into a matter relating to the AFP
that is referred to it by the Attorney-General. In
circumstances, the Tribunal may be assisted by
counsel, a member of IID or the AFP. The Tribunal then
reports to the Attorney-General
on its findings. This
broad power of review could be very useful in reviewing
controversial aspects of the AFP's operations or
that raise significant public interest. However, the
Commission understands that no such reference has ever
6.34 The Tribunal
may determine its own procedure subject to the Act
and Regulations.
The Tribunal is required to conduct its proceedings
with as little formality and technicality as possible
as a proper consideration
of the matter permits.
with the current AFP disciplinary process
Difficulties
with the underlying philosophy
6.35 The Commission
considers that the current philosophy underpinning
the disciplinary process is inappropriate and outdated
for law enforcement agencies such as the NCA and the
AFP. Complaints and resulting disciplinary proceedings
have been dealt
with by the application of a largely
inflexible, formal process that tends to be adversarial
in character. Modern police management
has changed
substantially but the disciplinary process has not
kept pace with those changes.
6.36 Historically
the police have tended to regard themselves as a para-military
force with an emphasis on hierarchy and the imposition
of discipline through formal channels such as laying
disciplinary charges for infractions, whether minor
or serious. To a large
extent this approach removes
responsibility for discipline from line managers of
Its main defect is that it does not focus on what
the Commission considers to be the fundamental issue
which is that of employment
suitability in the light
of the findings in the disciplinary proceeding. It
is the Commission's view that disciplinary proceedings
should not be regarded or dealt with as criminal matters
and that they do not require the criminal process and
trial. The Federal
Court has distinguished between
the objectives of disciplinary matters and those of
the criminal law:
[t]he object of disciplinary proceedings under the
Discipline Regulations is to protect the public, to
maintain proper standards
of conduct by members of
the Australian Federal Police and to protect the reputation
of that body. The object of disciplinary
proceedings
is not to punish.
6.37 The current
inflexible disciplinary process encourages some supervisors
to use it in cases where other management responses
would be more appropriate and useful. This is especially
so for minor infractions of rules and procedures where
there is no
suggestion of wilful misconduct and no
serious consequences flow from the breach. The same
inflexible, adversarial process may
lead to some officers
who have made a mistake or who have breached a disciplinary
rule to try to 'tough it out' and hope that
the complexity
or delays in the system may assist their cause.
difficulties with the operation of the disciplinary
6.38 Some of the
Commission's consultations and submissions suggest
that an overly adversarial, criminal style process
and lenient
penalties are significant problems under
the current AFP disciplinary process. The most obvious
and serious examples discussed
are where officers are
dismissed from the agency for significant misconduct
such as serious assault but are then reinstated by
the appeals mechanism. This concern is not unique to
the AFP disciplinary process. Other Australian police
services are concerned
with the same issue.
According to the NSW Royal Commission into the Police,
courts and tribunals generally have shown a reluctance
to find police
officers guilty of offences or to impose
significant penalties when they do so.
6.39 To impose
lenient penalties on an officer who is guilty of significant
misconduct may lead to a demoralising cynicism and
a loss of faith within the organisation. Good, professional
officers concerned with the health and standing of
their agency
may become frustrated and apathetic about
improving police culture. Officers who are 'let off
the hook' may become a liability
within the agency
and there is often an understandable reluctance to
trust them in the performance of ordinary law enforcement
tasks or for them to have access to any confidential
information. Individual complainants and members of
the general public
who become aware of lenient penalties
may also become sceptical about the commitment of the
police to maintain appropriate standards
of conduct.
6.40 Part of these
doubts about the effectiveness of disciplinary proceedings
may stem from concerns about the standard of proof
and its application to particular cases. There is a
concern that courts and tribunals in disciplinary matters
tend to require
a satisfaction of proof that is too
onerous given the nature of the proceedings and the
facts of the particular case. The current
of proof for AFP disciplinary proceedings is the balance
of probabilities.
Prior to 1993 it was the criminal one of 'beyond reasonable
The current standard is based on the principle that
disciplinary decisions should not be punitive but instead
protect the community
and the reputation of the AFP.
As a result of the common law standard laid down in
Briginshaw v Briginshaw
the balance of probabilities test works on a sliding
scale depending on a number of factors including the
severity of the offence
and the gravity of the consequences.
The standard approaches the criminal test as the issue
becomes more serious in nature or
consequences, such
as assault or use of excessive force.
6.41 Mr Justice
Foster, President of the FPDT and a consultant to this
Inquiry, has advised the Commission that since the
'inappropriate'
criminal standard of proof was removed
in 1993 different results are observable by the Tribunal.
According to his Honour, while
the criminal standard
in the past may have led to decisions that were regarded
as 'lenient', when assessed by the ordinary civil
of proof, the situation is now different. The Commission
is interested in views on the effects of the change
standard of proof since 1993.
6.42 Other possible
reasons for the suggested leniency by courts and tribunals
may be that there has been too great a focus on
decisions that may affect an individual's livelihood
and a belief that to mete out significant penalties
where they would nevertheless be appropriate
will tarnish the reputation of the police force. Justice
Foster has advised the
Commission that it is quite
incorrect to suggest that these considerations would
enter into the actual decision making process
FPDT. The Commission invites comment on the influence,
if any, of such beliefs in the context of the AFP disciplinary
6.43 Some of the
Commission's consultations and submissions have expressed
concern with the role of the FPDT. These criticisms
have been levelled at the decision-making process of
the FPDT and have not impugned the integrity, skill
or capacity of any
of its members past or present.
These criticisms suggest that the Tribunal's decision
making process results in some inappropriate
and lenient penalties. According to the AFP and the
Ombudsman the process is too formal, legalistic and
The AFP has submitted that the average period from
notice to the Tribunal to a hearing by the Tribunal
has been six months.
According to the AFP the Tribunal has taken a quasi-criminal
approach, in that its procedures and formality approach
a criminal trial. While the Tribunal has a
discretion to dispense with formality and technicality,
it may feel constrained in
its capacity to use this
discretion widely. Technical, adversarial proceedings
may also involve greater costs not only to the
involved but also to the Tribunal's resources and the
administration of justice.
by the President of the FPDT
6.44 Mr Justice
Foster has advised the Commission that the Complaints
Act 'envisages' that hearings should at least approximate
to curial hearings in the ordinary sense. Matters before
the Tribunal are hotly contested and it has necessarily
adopted procedures
that are appropriate to essentially
adversarial litigation. In response to the suggestion
that the Tribunal has been too 'legalistic'
Foster advised that the Tribunal's proceedings have
the formality necessary to attach public credibility
to the proceedings,
such as is accorded to a hearing
of a charge before a Magistrate. The Tribunal also
rejected criticisms of delay. Justice Foster
that once the Tribunal is notified that the parties
are ready to proceed the Tribunal is able to arrange
suitable hearing
dates at the first reasonable time.
The Tribunal has never received any complaints of delay
in the conduct of its proceedings.
It has succeeded
in assigning early hearing dates, notwithstanding an
18 month delay in filling a vacancy after the retirement
of a member.
6.45 Justice Foster
has also emphasised the following matters.
6.46 The Tribunal
is independent. It is not part of the internal investigation
machinery of the AFP. It does not exist to enforce
any particular disciplinary policy. It has no inquisitorial
role. It has no conciliatory role. Its only role is
to adjudicate
upon the charges brought before it, ie
to hear and determine them on the evidence placed before
it, giving a reasoned decision
in accordance with law.
Its hearings are conducted in public (except where
considerations of security demand otherwise). Its
determinations,
both on the question of guilt and appropriate penalty,
depend upon evidence placed before it. It is, of course,
necessary that such evidence be tested by cross-examination.
Common justice requires that it only hear evidence
in the presence
of both parties. If the Commissioner
is of the opinion that the re-instatement of a dismissed
officer produces problems in relation
to employability
and general morale, then the Tribunal needs to hear
evidence of these matters in the presence of the officer
charged. Evidence of good character and conduct is
frequently given on behalf of officers charged with
disciplinary offences,
frequently from responsible
senior officers. In accordance with accepted legal
principle, such evidence is received and considered
both in relation to guilt and penalty. If no countervailing
evidence is given, the Tribunal may well give a decision,
by some to be 'lenient'.
6.47 The Tribunal
was intended to be and is composed of experienced lawyers.
The President and Deputy-President are Federal Court
Judges, with previous experience in the common law
and criminal jurisdictions of Supreme Courts. Other
members have been and
are practising Queen's Counsel
and retired Magistrates. The Deputy-President is also
a member of the AAT. They have had extensive
in the evaluation of evidence in contested cases. Most
cases before the Tribunal are contested, frequently
numerous witnesses giving conflicting accounts.
Apart from the expertise which the members past and
present have brought with
them, they have, of course,
gained further experience in the course of hearing
and determining cases before the Tribunal.
6.48 Obviously,
the interests of the public and the police force and
its officers require that careful consideration be
to the question of whether an independent Tribunal
should be retained and, if so, whether it should be
retained in its present
form. It should not be lightly
abandoned in favour of some untried system.
Commission's
6.49 The lack of
confidence expressed by the AFP and the Ombudsman in
the current disciplinary process does provide additional
support to the need for a significant change in approach
that in the Commission's view is supported by other
fundamental policy
considerations identified in this
The Commission welcomes further comments on the role
of the Federal Police Disciplinary Tribunal including
whether it ought to
be abolished or retained subject
to any changes to improve its efficiency. Specific
comments are also welcomed on the effect
of the standard
of proof, the Tribunal's procedures, case management
and speed of dealing with matters.
approach: managerial responsibility and administrative
6.50 The Commission
considers that a new and significantly different approach
to the disciplinary process will be better able
achieve its fundamental objectives of ensuring high
standards of performance and conduct, with consistent
and fair decision-making
provided in a cost-effective
and timely manner.
responsibility
6.51 The Commission
proposes that the current AFP disciplinary system be
replaced with a managerial decision-making model taking
the following format.
6.52 After a matter
has been appropriately investigated the AFP Commissioner
would be required to consider any relevant information
include the investigator's report containing responses
from the officer
and a transcript of interview with
the investigator. If the Commissioner or delegate is
satisfied that an allegation of misconduct
substantiated the Commissioner or delegate may commence
misconduct proceedings against the appointee.
6.53 The action
for misconduct must be in writing, contain particulars
of the alleged misconduct and contain the Commissioner's
proposed course of action in response to the misconduct.
The action for misconduct must also advise that she
or he may respond
in writing to the misconduct action
and the Commissioner's proposed course of action. The
legislation should specify the date
by which the appointee
must respond. Twenty eight days might be an appropriate
period. The Commissioner or delegate must consider
any response by the appointee in making a decision
by the Commissioner would
be reviewable by the AAT.
The whole misconduct process would be open to judicial
review under the
Administrative Decisions Judicial
Review Act 1977
(Cth) on the basis of an error of law.
Administrative
6.54 The Issues
Paper asked whether administrative review would be
preferable to the current quasi-criminal disciplinary
that in serious matters approaches a criminal
trial. The Commission's view is that administrative
review is clearly the most
appropriate decision-making
process when the fundamental objectives and circumstances
of the disciplinary process for law enforcement
are considered. The key objectives are employee suitability
and the achievement of appropriate standards of conduct.
The law enforcement agency must have primary responsibility
for the imposition of a penalty. This principle is
generally agreed
upon. However, there must be external
review of this internal decision-making process to
ensure effective accountability and
transparency and
to protect the rights of officers to natural justice
and a fair hearing. Administrative review meets each
of these requirements.
6.55 It also might
be argued that the AFP with administrative review would
be subject to a disciplinary process to which no other
public sector organisation was subject. The Commission
considers that there is a strong case to argue that
all public sector
agencies should have disciplinary
processes that are based upon managerial responsibility
and administrative review. However
that broad issue
is beyond the Commission's current terms of reference.
Nevertheless, when the specific case of law enforcement
discipline is considered the Commission's view is that
there are compelling reasons for administrative review.
6.56 Law enforcement
is one of the most crucial areas of the interface of
the citizen and the State. The actions of the police
have great potential to affect the rights and liberties
of citizens.
Moreover, it is the police and law enforcement agencies
generally who have the ability to use force. They have
the right to use
force in certain circumstances to
carry out their duties. They are almost unique in this
regard. Law enforcement officers may
also have greater
potential for significant corruption and other forms
of misconduct than most other members of groups in
community. The nature of their work and the people
they deal with, for example, criminal suspects who
may have access to large
amounts of money or drugs,
increases the risk of significant corruption and often
in circumstances where it is difficult to detect
where police cultures have tended to condone or at
least failed to bring to notice misconduct.
6.57 The NSW Royal
Commission into Police's Interim Report identified
special difficulties in investigating police misconduct:
police know the system and are likely to have early
warning of any interest in their activities
they are skilled in investigation techniques and counter
surveillance
they are likely to have corrupt associates willing
to cover for them
they are experienced in being interviewed, in being
cross examined and in giving evidence
their good credibility and character are readily assumed
by jurors, courts and tribunals
they can exert considerable personal influence over
internal informants and internal investigators particularly
if they hold senior
6.58 In summary,
law enforcement officers are in a special position
of trust and responsibility with the community because
their capacity to use force, the wide scope for
the exercise of individual discretion from officers
even low in rank, the significant
potential for misconduct
or corruption and the difficulties in proving misconduct.
They are in this special position of trust
when compared
to the vast majority of other occupations within the
community. There is a vital public interest in ensuring
that only people of high repute and performance remain
in law enforcement agencies. In all these circumstances
it is imperative
that the basic objectives of the disciplinary
process are effectively met. Administrative review
offers the greatest opportunity
for that to occur.
to provide administrative review
6.59 The Commission
considers that if administrative review is the appropriate
form of review then the AAT is the obvious body
provide it. The AAT is the principal federal agency
for the provision of administrative review on the merits.
It is an independent
body which reviews on the merits
a wide range of administrative decisions made by Commonwealth
departments, Ministers and other
tribunals. It can
only review decisions where it is given specific power
to do so under the relevant legislation. It currently
reviews decisions relating, for example, to taxation,
social security, veterans affairs, civil aviation,
customs and FOI.
6.60 It already
has jurisdiction in reviewing matters that involve
similar considerations as would arise under the AFP
disciplinary
process. Much of the AAT's work concerns
review of the giving, suspension or revocation of licences
or authorisations to carry
out activities in the commercial
area and in community activities. For example, the
AAT has review of the grant of export licences
civil aviation licences. In other words the AAT frequently
reviews decisions relating to the fitness of individuals
organisations to carry out complex or significant
tasks that involve broad community interests. These
matters can involve safety
and revenue issues and the
right of the community to have access to information
6.61 The AAT also
has some current jurisdiction concerning criminal or
disciplinary matters. This includes hearing appeals
Migration Act 1958
(Cth) relating to deportations
on the basis of criminal record and the review of decisions
involving the Health Insurance Commission.
6.62 The Commission
considers that the AAT has the personnel and procedures
necessary to deal effectively with review of disciplinary
decisions by the AFP. It is true that disciplinary
matters will often be hotly contested but the AAT often
hears matters that
are rigorously argued. There are
also other advantages in having the AAT provide the
6.63 The AAT is
a permanent, established full-time body that could
reduce delays in having AFP and NCA matters heard.
also provide input from a membership with a
broader range of experience than the legal expertise
of the Police Disciplinary Tribunal
and it already
has an established process of informality and administrative
review rather than the focus and tradition of a criminal
law tribunal. The AAT has developed flexible procedures,
including a discretion to admit evidence, to take an
inquisitorial
position in investigating matters, to
mediate and conciliate matters pre-trial, to employ
alternative dispute resolution and
to control pre-trial
preparation of cases.
6.64 If it were
given jurisdiction in this area the AAT may require
some new appointments of specialist and/or part time
and targeted professional development for some
current members with the potential to build up experience
in the area. A specialist
division within the AAT may
be appropriate given that it would be a new area of
review and that it involves consideration of
law enforcement
law and policy.
6.65 The Commission
does not consider that review of the AFP Commissioner's
disciplinary decisions and those of the Chairperson
of the NCA would require any significant increase in
resources for the AAT. The Commission expects that
under its draft recommendations
relatively few cases
would proceed to the AAT. This is especially so given
the intended limitations on the capacity to seek review
for minor sanctions.
Moreover, many disciplinary decisions are unlikely
to involve complex legal issues that might be raised,
for example, in the
administrative review of decisions
made by the Commissioner of Taxation. The real core
of the issue in many misconduct matters
determine the adequacy of the performance of the officer.
That involves as much as anything a consideration of
expectations about acceptable standards of conduct
by law enforcement officers. Much of the current technical
argument arises
because disciplinary matters tend to
be treated as criminal trials. The role of the AAT
should be to use its accepted administrative
law approach
and not to try to deal with these matters as quasi-criminal
or criminal.
Disciplinary
Appeal Committees not favoured
6.66 The Issues
Paper discussed the option of giving Public Service
Disciplinary Appeal Committees (DACs) jurisdiction
of the AAT. Under the
Public Service Act 1922
(Cth) there is provision made for disciplinary action
against officers of the Australian public service.
There is also provision to appeal to a Disciplinary
Appeal Committee in cases where dismissal has not resulted.
Review of cases
where dismissal has been based upon
misconduct are now vested in the Industrial Relations
Commission (IRC) under the unfair dismissal
Industrial Relations Act
(Cth). These changes
came into effect on 22 September 1995. In the Commission's
view this is not the preferable approach. Discipline,
in the context under consideration, should not be seen
as an industrial matter.
6.67 The Commission
considers that for the reasons expressed above the
AAT is the most suitable body to exercise review. However,
there are also some specific difficulties with DACs
having review powers. To use the DACs would mean having
two different processes,
with the IRC hearing dismissal
cases and the DACs other matters. This is likely to
produce a fragmented and overly complex system
context of AFP and NCA complaints. The Commission prefers
that one body be vested with the responsibility of
administrative review according to the merits
of the case. Moreover the record of the Disciplinary
Appeals Committees is the
subject of some concern.
Their decisions apparently are often challenged in
other forums by unions and management perhaps reflecting
also a lack of confidence in the Committees.
Draft recommendation
Disciplinary Appeal Committees not favoured
Draft recommendation 6.6.
The AAT should provide review
on the merits of disciplinary decisions by the AFP.
It may be necessary to establish a specialist
within the AAT to provide this review.
of a uniform NCA disciplinary process
6.68 The NCA does
not have its own disciplinary code. Staff who are employed
Public Service Act 1922
(Cth) are subject
to Australian Public Service disciplinary proceedings
and rights of review. They exercise their rights by
lodging an application for review with the Public Service
and Merit Protection Commission
. However, no other categories of staff of the NCA
come within the Public Service Act disciplinary code
and review process. The
current options for disciplining
other NCA staff members are essentially informal. Thus
for example, the major sanction open
to the Chairperson
in relation to seconded officers is to terminate the
secondment and return the officer to the home agency.
In addition an adverse report may be made on the officer.
There is no particular sanction available in respect
of consultants
except for terminating their contracts.
6.69 The Commission
considers that it is fundamental to the effective management
of the NCA that it should have a formal misconduct
code that applies to staff members in their work for
the NCA. The NCA should have its own code of misconduct
with procedures
for making findings as to liability
and determining penalties. The Commission considers
that the misconduct code of the AFP as
amended according
to the draft recommendations in this paper would be
a good starting point for determining the content of
the NCA's misconduct process.
6.70 Where an NCA
staff member is a State or Territory seconded officer
or a member of a joint task then agreements could be
entered into with the relevant home agency for the
NCA to determine appropriate disciplinary action where
the Chairperson is
satisfied that a breach has occurred.
The NCA would report the result of the action to the
be part of the officer's
continuous service record. The home agency could decide
that further action was necessary as a consequence
of the NCA's determination but would have to take into
account any penalty already imposed by the NCA. These
suggestions might
require some amendment to home agency
legislation about discipline.
6.71 The Commission
also proposes that the any disciplinary decisions made
by the NCA should be subject to administrative review
by the AAT in similar fashion to the AFP process. The
Commission can see no reason why this should not be
the case. Both the
AFP and the NCA are federal law
enforcement bodies with similar agendas and objectives.
6.72 An alternative
approach that the Commission regards less favourably
is for the NCA complaints system to apply to all NCA
staff but not the NCA disciplinary system. The NCA
or the NIIC would be limited to making reports to the
officers or joint task force
members with recommendations about whether criminal
or disciplinary action should be taken. It would
then a matter for the home agency to determine the
course of action including any disciplinary action.
The home agency would
then report the result back to
the NCA and the NIIC.
Draft recommendations
The lack of a formal NCA disciplinary process
Draft recommendation 6.7.
The NCA should have its own
misconduct code that applies to all NCA members and
staff. The preferable method for achieving this
for the NCA to enter into agreements with the relevant
enable those officers to be subject to the
disciplinary system of the NCA in relation to their
actions or purported actions under
the NCA Act or the
exercise of their powers and functions under the NCA
