Consequences
of AAT review
6.73 If the AAT
provides administrative review the following consequences
and changes would follow.
standard of proof
6.74 As noted in
paragraph 6.40 the current standard of proof for AFP
disciplinary proceedings is the balance of probabilities.
However, as a result of the common law standard laid
Briginshaw v Briginshaw
the balance of probabilities test works on a sliding
6.75 The standard
of proof for decisions reviewed by the AAT where the
conduct of a person is the basis for a decision involving
that person is the balance of probabilities.
The Commission considers that this test is appropriate
for the AAT reviewing AFP and NCA disciplinary decisions.
principle is applicable to AAT review.
Draft recommendation
The standard of proof
Draft recommendation 6.9.
The standard of proof should
be the usual standard adopted by the AAT, that is,
on the balance of probabilities.The
would be applicable under AAT review.
right to silence
6.76 There are
two different situations to consider: the right to
silence during investigation; and the right during
investigation.
The IID and ISA currently have power
to compel answers from a member of the AFP.
There is no general right to refuse to answer on the
ground of self-incrimination, but there is a proviso
that the information
so obtained will not be used against
the person in any criminal proceedings arising out
of the investigation. In the course of
investigations,
any evidence so obtained is only admissible in disciplinary
proceedings and not in any subsequent proceedings
other than a prosecution for failing to provide information
or giving false information
or in an application by the Ombudsman to the Federal
Court for an order compelling production.
The Ombudsman has the same power to compel answers
when investigating an AFP matter, although this provision
applies generally
to any relevant witness.
However AFP appointees are cautioned and have the
right to silence when questioned about a criminal offence.
6.78 The Commission
does not support there being an unmodified right to
silence during investigations concerning possible disciplinary
matters. The right to silence in the course of investigations
is a traditional aspect of criminal investigation and
proceedings.
However, as already argued above, disciplinary
proceedings, particularly in relation to law enforcement
officers, are different
in purpose and result from
criminal proceedings. Moreover, the absence of a right
to silence in misconduct investigations helps
an environment where AFP appointees believe they have
a duty to report misconduct and to tell the truth when
are investigated.
During a hearing.
A person appearing as a witness before the Police Disciplinary
Tribunal must answer questions that are required to
be answered
by the Tribunal and produce any document
required to be produced. The penalty for non compliance
is six months imprisonment.
However there is a defence
of reasonable excuse for non-compliance which includes
the case where the answer or the production
document may tend to prove that the person has committed
an offence against a law or in the case of an AFP appointee,
that the appointee has been guilty of a breach of discipline.
6.80 The Commission
considers that the NIIC when conducting a hearing should
have the power to compel answers and the production
of documents. Such information should not be admissible
in evidence against the person in criminal or civil
proceedings but
should be admissible in disciplinary
proceedings.
6.81 Under the
AAT Act a refusal or failure to answer questions that
a person is required to answer is an offence.
However, the privilege to refuse to incriminate oneself
in respect of a criminal matter is accepted by the
AAT to be applicable
in its jurisdiction.
Each of these principles should apply to review of
AFP and NCA disciplinary decisions.
Draft recommendations
Modified right to silence
Draft recommendation 6.10.
There would be no right
to silence during investigations by the NIIC. However,
as is currently the case for investigations by
IID no information obtained should be able to be used
in any criminal proceedings arising out of the investigation.
IID's power to compel answers from a member of
the AFP during an investigation should continue. During
investigations the normal
requirement for a caution
if there is a belief that a criminal offence may have
been committed should apply.
Draft recommendation 6.11.
The NIIC when conducting
a hearing should have the power to compel answers and
the production of documents. Such information
not be admissible in evidence against the person in
criminal or civil proceedings but should be admissible
in disciplinary
proceedings.
Draft recommendation 6.12.
The current provision of
the AAT Act in respect to this matter should apply
to review by the AAT.
Proceedings
normally to be public
6.82 Currently
hearings are to be held in public except in special
circumstances.
The Disciplinary Tribunal may direct that the hearing
be private or restrict the publication or disclosure
of evidence or its
decision or findings if it is desirable
in the public interest or because of the confidential
nature of the evidence or matter.
The Tribunal may
also restrict or prohibit the publication or disclosure
of any matters relating to the hearing including evidence
decisions or findings.
6.83 The AAT Act
has a very similar provision. Hearings before the AAT
are to be in public. However the Tribunal is able to
direct that a hearing or part of a hearing take place
in private where, for example, the evidence is of a
confidential nature.
6.84 The Commission
considers that the AAT provision provides an appropriate
balance between ensuring that the AFP and the public
has information about the working of the complaints
system and protecting the privacy and confidentiality
of parties.
Draft recommendation
Proceedings normally to be public
Draft recommendation 6.13.
Review of disciplinary decisions
by the AAT will be in public unless the AAT determines
otherwise as currently provided for under
the AAT Act
to a decision
6.85 Currently,
there is no automatic right of appearance by complainants
before the Tribunal. The Tribunal may grant leave to
the complainant to appear or be represented if it is
satisfied that there are special circumstances to justify
6.86 The AAT Act
provides that an application may be made by, or on
behalf of, any person or persons (including the Commonwealth
or an authority of the Commonwealth) whose interests
are affected by the decision.
The AAT has defined 'are affected by the decision'
broadly to mean that it is not necessary to show that
it is a pecuniary interest
or even specific legal rights.
In many cases it is conceivable that a complainant
this test. The Commission is concerned that if a significant
number of complainants were to seek review that this
would detract from the main purpose of misconduct proceedings
which is to maintain effective standards of conduct
and employee
suitability in law enforcement agencies.
That should be achieved by the proper conduct of the
matter by the AFP or the NCA and
by their legal representatives.
6.87 To allow complainants
to regularly intervene as parties would also increase
costs and delays in the conduct of these proceedings.
It is also likely to increase the prospect of the proceedings
becoming more adversarial and more technical. If a
complainant
wishes compensation or restitution, the
NIIC may make those recommendations or the complainant
may institute civil actions. Currently
if the Attorney-General
considers that a penalty imposed upon a member by the
Commissioner is inadequate, the Attorney may appeal
the decision to the Tribunal.
6.88 After considering
these circumstances the Commission's view is that it
is necessary to include in the AFP and NCA legislation
a provision to the effect that for the purposes of
the jurisdiction of the AAT 'persons affected by a
decision' is to be limited
to the person who is the
subject of the proceeding or to the Chairperson of
the NIIC. The latter should be allowed to seek review
of a decision, where he or she considers that the AFP
Commissioner or the NCA Chairperson has imposed a manifestly
penalty. This is necessary to ensure that
internal decisions are fair and adequate. It should
be a rare occurrence for the NIIC
to seek review. The
Commission considered whether the power of the NIIC
to report to Parliament about an inadequate penalty
might be sufficient but is of the view that there must
be an express provision for the NIIC to seek review
so that any possible
injustice in the particular case
is reviewed directly and quickly. It would still be
open to the NIIC to report on the penalties
generally or in relation to individual cases.
Draft recommendation
Parties to a decision
Draft recommendation 6.14.
The right to seek review
by the AAT of a disciplinary decision should be expressly
limited to the officer the subject of the
to the Chairperson of the NIIC where he or she is satisfied
that the penalty imposed is manifestly inadequate.
on the availability of AAT review
6.89 The Commission
proposes that there should be a limit on the availability
of review by the AAT. The imposition of minor sanctions
should be the sole province of the AFP or NCA. Those
sanctions are an integral part of management responsibility
and to allow
review reduces the capacity for management
to provide leadership and responsibility for the health
of the organisation. Moreover,
to allow review by the
AAT of minor sanctions will needlessly increase costs
and delays in the process. Reprimands, counselling,
training or retraining and any penalty that can be
reduced to a monetary figure that is below a stipulated
amount of penalty
units should not be reviewable by
the AAT. The Commission considers that currently a
figure of $500 would be appropriate.
Draft recommendation
Limit on the availability of AAT review
Draft recommendation 6.15.
There should be a limit
on the availability of review by the AAT for certain
sanctions. Reprimands, counselling, training or
and any penalty that can be reduced to a monetary figure
that is below a stipulated amount of penalty units
not be reviewable by the AAT. The Commission
considers that currently a figure of $500 would be
appropriate.

may order that costs incurred by the member be paid,

However, the Commissioner cannot obtain a costs order
against a member.

liability for costs
6.93 Another issue
is the liability of individual AFP appointees to pay
legal costs when their misconduct was directly responsible
for charges against an individual being dismissed or
for a person being wrongly charged in the first place.
Until now the AFP
has met costs awarded against the
informant. Where misconduct is involved it may be more
appropriate for the AFP to require the
responsible
appointee to do so.
Draft recommendation
Individual liability for costs
Draft recommendation 6.17.
There should be a statutory
discretion for the AFP Commissioner or the Chairperson
of the NCA to be able to require an individual
to pay all or part of the agency's legal costs where
the Commissioner or Chairperson is satisfied that the
misconduct was intentional, reckless or negligent,
and led to charges against an individual being dismissed
or for a person being
wrongly charged in the first
place. A decision to exercise this discretion should
be subject to the approval of the NIIC and
any decision
to impose payment should be reviewable by the AAT if
it is above a minimum level of penalty units (equivalent
at the moment to $500).
6.94 One argument
is that the proper determination of complaints is in
the public interest and consequently accused officers
should receive public funding in order to meet the
costs of the Tribunal, particularly where reputation
and career are at stake.
While making no recommendation,

funding by Police Associations. This was
referred to
in recent consultations with the AFPA.
ALRC 9 stated that where the complainant is granted
leave to be represented and costs are borne by the
Commonwealth, then justice
would require that similar
assistance should be made available to the police officer.

to disciplinary proceedings

for negligence proceeding
6.99 The proceeding
that an appointee was negligent or careless in the
discharge of his or her duties, was removed when s
of the AFP Act was introduced. The rationale for
this removal was that s 26E was to be the procedure
to deal with negligent conduct.
However, the Commission
considers that s 26E cannot be regarded as a complete
substitute for negligence proceedings. Section
provides for retirement. However, there will be cases
of negligence where retirement would be an excessive
outcome. The
Commission is of the view that negligence
proceedings should be available to deter behaviour
that results in significant consequences
such as the
loss of public revenue.

Dispute resolution
scope for Alternative Dispute Resolution
6.102 Currently
ADR tends to be used to resolve minor complaints only.
Moreover, it will not always be suggested or tried
all such complaints. The Commission considers that
informal resolution or
mediation. They can be categorised
as minor complaints. The process for those complaints
is discussed in chapter 5. However,
one option is to
use ADR for a much broader range of complaints including
moderate and perhaps in some circumstances even serious
complaints.
6.103 There are
advantages in the use of ADR for a wider range of complaints.
From the perspective of the public interest, ADR
offer the most effective way of actually changing individual
police officers' attitudes and behaviour. It might
to the needs of individual complainants
who, according to survey evidence, often do not want
formal disciplinary action taken
but instead want the
police officer to understand the complainant's feelings
and point of view and to modify his or her own behaviour.
Some complainants, but certainly not all, also want
a face to face meeting with the police officer to discuss
the incident.
Moreover, many complainants see delay
as a major problem and it is the formal investigation
and determination of complaints that
is likely to take
much longer than ADR. Police officers might generally
prefer an ADR approach because there is no intensive
investigation nor risk of a conviction for a disciplinary
6.104 Some disadvantages
may be that police may feel pressured to 'accept' ADR
when they believe they are not guilty of any misconduct.
Complainants may also be pressured by the police to
accept ADR because it will let the individual officer
'off the hook' on
disciplinary charges. The systemic
problems that a complaint may identify may be less
likely to be addressed if ADR is used because
to be a less public process than a formal investigation
and hearing.
6.105 The Commission's
view is that the legislation should not prescribe in
any detail what types of complaints other than those
classified as minor are suitable for ADR. The NIIC
should be able to determine any guidelines for its
use taking into account
for example, the seriousness
of the complaint, the public interest, the wishes of
the complainant, whether the alleged misconduct
deliberate or inadvertent and whether investigation
will be more effective in resolving the complaint.
However, any ADR
undertaken by the AFP or the NCA should
be subject to the approval of the NIIC and monitored
by the NIIC. The NIIC should be
able to develop guidelines
with these agencies about the use of ADR in cases that
are not categorised as minor.
6.106 One particular
form of ADR that the Commission is interested in obtaining
views about in the context of complaints against
AFP and the NCA is the adaptation of conferencing or
re-integrative shaming to complaints. These conferences
used primarily in juvenile justice cases
instead of the traditional court process. It is suggested
that these conferences best
satisfy the victim and
are more likely to prevent further misconduct. There
is a conference with the major players, to allow
the participants the opportunity to explain how the
incident affected them, to get the offender, with the
assistance of
others, to draw up a final plan that
is agreeable to everyone and to monitor the implementation
of the plan.
6.107 The Commission
sought comments on this model in the issues paper but
the only response it has received is from the AFP which
suggests that diversionary conferencing is not appropriate
for matters involving corruption. The Commission does
not know whether
conferencing has been used in relation
to complaints against the police. Some issues that
arise are whether conferencing is appropriate
for complaints,
who would be present at the conference, who would convene
it and whether it would be suitable for all types of
complaints, including internal complaints such as sexual
harassment and corruption. In cases of corruption who
would be the
'victim' or represent the 'victim'?
ADR be compulsory in any case?
6.108 Complainants
should not be required or pressured into agreeing to
any form of ADR. The officer's consent for informal
resolution of minor matters should not be required.
In all other cases the officer's consent for participating
in ADR should
be necessary.
Draft recommendation
Should ADR be compulsory in any case?
Draft recommendation 6.23.
Complainants should not
be required or pressured into agreeing to any form
of ADR. The officer's consent for informal resolution
of minor matters should not be required. In all other
cases the officer's consent for participating in ADR
should be necessary.

of statements
6.109 Currently
evidence of a statement made and answer given by an

not admissible against the appointee in any proceedings
including proceedings
in relation to a breach of discipline.
6.110 The Commission's
view is that this provision should continue as it encourages
officers to use ADR. However, statements made
by officers
should be able to be used for purely employment related
Draft recommendation

Draft recommendation 6.24.
Statements or answers made
by subject officers in the course of ADR should not

also apply to the complainant. However, statements
made by officers should be able to be used for purely
employment related
and official records
6.111 In Queensland
for both Informal Resolution and mediation there are
no details recorded on the police officer's file. This
is also the case in England. The argument for such
an approach is that it is necessary to encourage police
to participate in
the process and to admit responsibility
where appropriate. One possible problem is that there
may be officers who are subject
to many complaints
resolved informally but there is no disciplinary action
available and there is no recording on their file.
It might be argued that it would be desirable to have
a system of monitoring ADR in relation to individual
officers, not on
their personal files, but for management
purposes. It could be difficult, however, to maintain
this distinction.
6.112 The Commission's
view is that the AFP and the NCA should have a full
profile of their staff in order to assess staff needs
and development and their performance. This must include
details about any ADR process. It may be best to record
ADR on performance review documents rather
than personnel files but there must be a co-ordinated
system that readily provides
a full employment history
and profile.
Draft recommendation
ADR and official records
Draft recommendation 6.25.
The AFP and the NCA should
have a full profile of their staff in order to assess

This must include details about any ADR process. It
may be best to record details of ADR on performance
review documents rather
than personnel files but there
must be a co-ordinated system that readily provides

6.113 Sanctions
or penalties are imposed in order to protect the public
rather than to punish the wrongdoer and to ensure that
the wrongful action does not recur.
6.114 The sanctions
for a breach of an AFP disciplinary offence are provided
for in the AFP (Discipline) Regulations. Where the
Commissioner determines that an appointee is guilty
of a disciplinary offence the Commissioner may
admonish, caution or reprimand the appointee
impose a fine of a maximum not exceeding the amount
equalling the salary for five days
reduce the salary to a rate that is not lower than
the lowest salary for that rank
reduce the appointee to a lower rank
dismiss the appointee.
with minor misconduct without a misconduct proceeding
6.115 The Commission
considers that in response to minor cases of misconduct
supervisors and managers should be able to take positive,
direct and immediate responses including requiring
attendance at counselling or training programs or issuing
Caution Notices.
These Notices should be able to be
issued on the spot. The Victoria Police has introduced
similar measures. A failure to respond
to these lower
level measures may result in a misconduct proceeding
being commenced.
Draft recommendation
Dealing with minor misconduct without a misconduct
Draft recommendation 6.26.
For minor cases of misconduct,
supervisors and managers should be able to take positive,
direct and immediate responses including
attendance at counselling or training programs or issuing
Caution Notices. These should be able to be issued
spot. A failure to respond to these lower level
measures may result in a misconduct proceeding being
after a misconduct proceeding
6.116 Where the
Commissioner finds a misconduct proceeding proved the
Commission proposes that the following outcomes should
be available:
Counselling, training or retraining
A fine linked to salary seems an inappropriate
measure of penalty given that salaries vary considerably.
For example, two officers
could be liable for the same
misconduct but could receive significantly different
penalties on the basis of their different salaries.
While in some cases this could be appropriate to reflect
their different responsibilities and positions of trust,
cases it may not. The Commission considers
that the most effective means of ensuring that monetary
penalties continue to be consistent
and able to reflect
changes in inflation is for them to be provided for
by penalty units as is now common in legislation dealing
with fines. Without being prescriptive a maximum at
the moment of say $5 000 might be appropriate.
Reduction in rank or seniority.
The AFP has advised
that it has removed its traditional rank structure
and now uses the term 'Federal Agent' for all personnel
working in federal areas. However, the existing ranks
continue to be used in the ACT region, Jervis Bay and
the external territories.
The Commission has included
the sanction to cover the present situation but will
consider its necessity in its final report.
The sanction
may need to be broadened to allow a reduction in a
work classification or position. The Commission considers
if there is a sanction to reduce rank, seniority,
a work classification or position, it should include
a power to reduce that
status for a set period of time
and then allow a return to the previous rank, seniority,
work classification or position. The
AFP should also
be able to suspend these reductions if the officer
complies with specified conditions. A failure to comply
would allow the AFP to enforce the reduction.
Reduction in remuneration.
This sanction should be
available particularly if there is no system of ranks.
Transfer to other duties
superannuation orders be available?
6.117 The part
on s 26E earlier in this chapter dealt with a proposed
change to that section so that for cases of retirement
for serious misconduct a reduction in the employer
contribution to superannuation would be available.
This part of the issues paper deals with withholding
superannuation benefits where there has been an adverse
finding in a disciplinary
proceeding. Under the AFP
Act, persons may be subject to an order against their
superannuation as a penalty if they are found
a corruption offence and sentenced to more than 12
months imprisonment or
a 'relevant disciplinary offence' and dismissed from
the AFP as a result.
6.118 This second
circumstance is unique to the AFP as no other Commonwealth
employee is liable to such a sanction for a disciplinary
matter. Under the AFP Regulations a 'relevant disciplinary
offence' means any improper use by an AFP appointee
of the fact
that he or she is an AFP appointee in the
course of the commission of a particular disciplinary
offence and, in particular, acts
constitute disgraceful or improper conduct either in
an official capacity or otherwise
are prejudicial to the good order and discipline of
are likely to discredit the reputation of the AFP
or which lead to the AFP member being charged before
the court with an offence against the Commonwealth,
State or Territory or
another country where that charge
is proved to the satisfaction of the court whether
or not the court proceeds to conviction,
any act by an AFP member to aid, abet, counsel, procure
or be knowingly concerned in or party to the commission
of any disciplinary
6.119 One argument
for the availability of superannuation orders for disciplinary
matters in the case of the AFP is that AFP personnel
are in a special position of trust and responsibility
and therefore should be open to heavier sanctions than
other public officers
for a gross breach of that trust.
6.120 The alternative
view is that the taking of superannuation should be
regarded as a sanction suitable only in criminal matters
and restricted to serious criminal matters. Disciplinary
proceedings are not intended to be punitive. Their
purpose is to ensure
appropriate standards of behaviour
and employee suitability.
Commission's
6.121 The Commission
supports the availability of deducting the whole or
a portion of the employer's contribution for certain
disciplinary proceedings.
6.122 While the
Commission considers that AFP personnel are in a special
position of trust and responsibility it is difficult
to argue that those responsibilities make them unique
when compared to the whole range of public officers.
However, the Commission
does not consider that it is
necessary to make that argument to support the availability
of reducing superannuation in certain
disciplinary
cases. The Commission is of the view that the availability
of the sanction should be extended to all public sector
personnel. This extends beyond the Commission's current
terms of reference but the principle is clear. It is
possible for an
officer in any public agency to be
in a position of significant trust and responsibility
and to abuse his or her office. Many
staff regularly
deal with members of the public or revenue or public
monies. There is likely to be some scope for corruption
and serious misconduct in every agency. An example
of the inequity under the current situation is where
an AFP officer and
officers from a broad range of other
public agencies are involved in the one wide scale
system of bribery. Each may have acted
in the same
way with the same degree of misconduct. It seems inconsistent
and unfair to have one additional sanction available
in respect of the AFP officer but unavailable in respect
of the rest.
6.123 The Commission
considers that the taking of some amount of the employers'
contribution is not punitive in cases of serious
that involve corruption or a significant abuse of the
person's office. Those who have abused their office
enrichment should not be permitted to gain
the full benefits of superannuation derived from that
office. This is not a punitive
sanction but restorative
6.124 However
the current definition of 'relevant disciplinary offence'
is very broad. One approach would be to provide that
any misconduct proceeding that results in dismissal
is serious enough for a superannuation order to be
available. However, that
would be moving away from
some requirement that the misconduct has an element
of corruption or significant abuse of one's office.
The Commission prefers that the option mirror the provision
relating to the corruption offence. That is, the option
be available for misconduct proceedings
involved an abuse by the person of his or her office
was committed for a purpose that involved corruption
was committed for the purpose of perverting or attempting
to pervert the course of justice.
6.125 As discussed
in relation to s 26E at paragraph 6.14 if superannuation
orders are to be an option then they should be restricted
to the employer's contribution and interest on that
amount. It is this portion that can be clearly identified
as a benefit
that the person has derived from the office
rather than the employee's own contributions. There
should also be a discretion to
deduct a part and not
merely the whole of the employer's contribution and
interest thereon. This would allow the order to be
specifically tailored to the gravity and circumstances
of the particular case.
Draft recommendations
Should superannuation orders be available?
Draft recommendation 6.27.
Deductions from an officer's
superannuation should be available as a sanction for
a misconduct proceeding. The option would only
be available
for misconduct proceedings that
involved an abuse by the person of his or her office
was committed for a purpose that involved corruption
was committed for the purpose of perverting or attempting
to pervert the course of justice.
Draft recommendation 6.28.
Superannuation orders should
be restricted to the employer's contribution and interest
on that amount. There should also be a
discretion to
deduct a part and not merely the whole of the employer's
contribution and interest thereon.
there be a power of expungement?
6.126 There is
an issue as to whether misconduct proceedings should
be expunged from an officer's records and files after
period of time or where the officer has acted
in a way that calls for expungement. Currently there
is no expungement provision.
6.127 The Commission
does not support the introduction of an expungement
provision. Misconduct proceeding are about employee
standards and suitability. They are clearly relevant
to an employment history and profile and should be
recorded for the purpose
of promotion, security clearances
and reappointment.
Draft recommendation
Should there be a power of expungement?
Draft recommendation 6.29.
There should be no statutory
provision for the expungement of misconduct proceedings.
the complainant
6.128 The Commission
considers that regular and informative reporting to
the complainant is a vital part of an effective and
credible complaints system. There are two main areas
to consider - reporting about investigations and reporting
of misconduct proceedings.
requirements in relation to investigations
6.129 A responsive
and comprehensive system for reporting about the progress
and outcomes of investigations of complaints is likely
to increase public confidence in the integrity of the
complaints system. It may also require additional resources
agencies providing the service.
6.130 Complainants
are entitled to routine communication about the status
of their complaint subject to the exceptional demands
of operational security that may be relevant, particularly
in cases alleging corruption.
6.131 The current
reporting requirements under the Complaints Act are
not elaborate. The Ombudsman upon receipt of the investigation
report is to provide a complainant with particulars
of the investigation
and the Ombudsman may at any time also give a complainant
a progress report about the complaint.
6.132 Reports
should contain sufficient information for a complainant
to understand and follow the course of the investigation
and decision-making process. Where a complaint is not
investigated or substantiated the reasons for this
should be provided
to the complainant. That might involve
for example, an explanation of the standard of proof
that applies.
6.133 The Ombudsman's
current practice is to have one officer who takes responsibility
for keeping a complainant advised of developments
the case. The NIIC, the AFP and the NCA should designate
a contact officer for a complainant in respect of each
complaint. That person would be responsible
for periodically reporting to the complainant about
the progress of the investigation,
the reasons for
any significant delay and for answering any direct
inquiries from the complainant. One possibility is
an obligation
of reporting at least monthly. This could
be done where appropriate by telephone.
on misconduct proceedings
6.134 Under the
Commission's draft recommendations it is the AFP or
the NCA that would have the primary role in determining
misconduct actions. As part of that responsibility
they should inform the complainant of the misconduct
process and the outcome.
This should include information
any penalty imposed and the reasons for it.
6.135 The AFP
and the NCA should be required under their legislation
to report to the complainant the outcome of misconduct
actions including the outcome of any review by the
AAT or appeal to the Federal Court. They should also
be required to do so
within a time to be specified
in the regulations or under guidelines. Twenty eight
days from the finding on a misconduct action
a decision by the AAT or the Federal Court might be
an appropriate time frame.
Draft recommendations
Reporting on misconduct proceedings
Draft recommendation 6.30.
Reports should contain sufficient
information for a complainant to understand and follow
the course of the investigation and
decision-making
process. Where a complaint is not investigated or substantiated
the reasons for this should be provided to the
complainant.
That might involve, for example, an explanation of
the standard of proof that applies.
Draft recommendation 6.31.
The NIIC, the AFP and the
NCA should designate a contact officer for a complainant
in respect of each individual complaint. That
would be responsible for periodically reporting to
the complainant about the progress of the investigation,
the reasons
for any significant delay and for answering
any direct inquiries from the complainant. One possibility
is an obligation of reporting
at least monthly. This
could be done where appropriate by telephone.
Information
6.136 The Commission
is of the view that the AFP and the NCA should give
consideration to greater dissemination of information
misconduct throughout their respective organisations,
subject to appropriate
protection of the identity of
complainants. This could be done in gazettes, in-house
information bulletins and notice boards.
6.137 If a proceeding
has been held in camera then dissemination of specific
decisions even in an anonymous format should be avoided
and instead information should be communicated through
guidelines.
However, the Commission considers that as a general
rule, those officers found guilty of significant misconduct
have forfeited
their right to any anonymity. For less
serious cases anonymity and the use of guidelines may
be appropriate.
Draft recommendation
Information about disciplinary results
Draft recommendation 6.32.
The AFP and the NCA should
disseminate widely information about the results of
proceedings throughout their respective organisations
subject to appropriate protection of the identity of
complainants. For example, this could be done by using
gazettes, in-house
information bulletins and notice
Legislative
structure of the disciplinary systems
6.138 The AFP
disciplinary proceedings are provided for by the Australian
Federal Police (Discipline) Regulations made under
the AFP Act. The Commission has considered whether
these regulations should be made pursuant to the NIIC
Act but has reached
the view that because discipline
is not always linked to complaints the discipline regulations
should remain under the AFP Act.
This also acknowledges
the role of discipline as a managerial tool for the
law enforcement agency. For the same reasons the disciplinary
provisions for the NCA should be included under the
Draft recommendations
Legislative structure of the disciplinary systems
Draft recommendation 6.33.
The AFP discipline regulations
should remain under the AFP Act.
Draft recommendation 6.34.
The disciplinary provisions
to be drafted for the NCA should be provided for under
the NCA Act.
use of evidence and information from disciplinary proceedings
6.139 The Issues
Paper sought comment on the derivative use of evidence
and information from disciplinary proceedings. There
is a concern that material and evidence from disciplinary
proceedings is being used by parties in criminal proceedings
proceedings.
This is known as derivative use. One contentious issue
is when a brief of the prosecution or the defence becomes
available to
a party in a different proceeding. It
was thought that this was not a problem in practice
at least in the jurisdiction of complaints
Material could only be obtained by subpoena and that
would be subject to the normal protections of relevance
and privilege.
6.140 The AFP
has submitted that the prohibition against derivative
use under the Complaints Act only operates where an
appointee is a defendant in civil proceedings or
an accused in criminal proceedings.
If an appointee was a witness in criminal or civil
proceedings, information supplied by that person in
the course of an IID investigation
may be admissible.
The AFP has also submitted that IID files have been
subpoenaed for use in criminal proceedings, mainly
question the credibility of AFP appointees. This
has sometimes been used where the wide information
sought had little relevance
to the criminal proceedings.
The AFP has submitted that the legislation should be
amended to afford greater protection to appointees
when they supply information under direction.
6.141 The Attorney-General's
Department has advised that under the
Evidence Act
(Cth) use immunity and derivative use immunity
is given to testimony compelled by a court. The Department
has commented that
it does not appear that under the
Complaints Act such additional immunity is necessary
to encourage co-operation with an investigation.
according to the Department, the inclusion of greater
protection against derivative use might be considered
a more complete reflection of the right
to silence.
6.142 The Commission's
view is that as a matter of principle derivative use
should not be permitted or encouraged. Generally disciplinary
matters should be regarded as limited to the purpose
of imposing proper employment standards. The use of
information derived
from them should not be used for
criminal or civil proceedings. However, there may be
cases where it would be fair or reasonable
some limited use of such information in other proceedings.
The Commission is interested in views on how this principle
of discouraging derivative use should work in practice
and what legislative change, if any, is necessary.
recommendation
Derivative use of evidence and information from disciplinary
proceedings
Draft recommendation 6.35.
As a matter of principle
derivative use should not be permitted or encouraged.
Comment sought on the application of this principle
There may be cases where it would be fair or reasonable
to permit some limited derivative use from disciplinary
proceedings.
The Commission is interested in views
on how this principle of discouraging derivative use
should work in practice and what legislative
if any, is necessary.

7. Anti-fraud and anti-corruption plans and measures
Introduction
for planning
developing an anti-corruption plan
current NCA anti-corruption plan
anti-corruption measures
anti-corruption measures
of the NIIC
and education
of conduct and mission statements
Possibilities
of a national or regional anti-corruption strategy
Introduction
7.1 Effective complaints
and disciplinary systems are essential to the accountability
and credibility of the AFP and the NCA.
However, they
do not constitute a complete response to those requirements
of accountability and credibility. They must be complemented
by effective anti-fraud plans and anti-corruption plans
that are properly implemented and monitored. The NSW
ICAC has submitted
that corruption prevention can be
achieved through pro-active work, formulating a strategic
intelligence plan to combat corruption
and targeting
specific areas. Since corruption is frequently a product
of the culture of an organisation, a change in culture
is necessary and can be achieved through education
and training.
7.2 This chapter
examines anti-fraud plans and anti-corruption plans
for the AFP and the NCA and the proposed role of the
in these strategies. The chapter then deals with
specific anti-corruption measures. The chapter concludes
by discussing the possibility
of a national or regional
strategy and approach to fraud and corruption in law
enforcement agencies.
Need for planning
7.3 The Commission
considers that it is essential that the AFP and the
NCA each has its own comprehensive anti-fraud plans
anti-corruption plans and that these plans are
co-ordinated where necessary. The AFP and the NCA as
major law enforcement agencies
should provide leadership
in the Commonwealth in the development and implementation
of these plans. The Commission considers
plans must accurately analyse risk in all aspects of
the respective organisations. They must be comprehensive
their scope. Furthermore, the anti-corruption plans
should provide for a thorough system of integrity testing
for all recruits
and regular integrity testing for
all staff. The view of the Commission is that there
should be testing at least every three
years for all
7.4 The AFP and
NCA are currently in the process of devising new fraud
control plans The Commission expects that the new plans
will contain effective risk assessment of fraud. The
Commission may comment on the new plans if they are
finalised by the time
of the Commission's final report
due on 30 September 1996.
7.5 The federal
Government in 1987 released a report that reviewed
systems for dealing with fraud on the Commonwealth.
report highlighted the need for each Commonwealth
agency to have a plan for fraud control.
What is required is recognition that fraud can and
does occur, and an explicit, systematic approach to
its prevention and detection
appropriate to the requirements
of the agency in question.
7.6 This approach
at a minimum requires
identification of risk areas
analysis of options for reducing the risks
formulating and introducing means of prevention, detection
and deterrence
staff training
exchange of information with other agencies
statistical records sufficient to ensure some measurement
of the effectiveness of action against fraud.
7.7 In December
1994 a revised Commonwealth policy called
Best Practice
Fraud Control
was introduced which set down standards and guidelines
for Commonwealth agencies and made the Commonwealth
Law Enforcement Board
(CLEB) responsible for the co-ordination
of fraud control policy.
7.8 The Commission
has sought advice from the AFP and the NCA about their
anti-fraud plans. It has also contacted CLEB which
advised that both the AFP and the NCA submitted fraud
control plans that complied with the requirements in
place before December
1994. Both agencies, like many
other Commonwealth agencies, are in the process of
revising their risk assessment and fraud control
to meet the additional requirements. The AFP has advised
that it intends that its new fraud control plan will
be submitted
to CLEB in December 1996 or January 1997.
The NCA expects that its updated fraud control plan
will be approved in early July
developing an anti-corruption plan
7.9 The AFP has
advised that it is developing a Security Plan that
is to be a comprehensive anti-corruption plan. Some
elements of that Plan are
a professional reporting policy that will encourage
reporting of unacceptable practices and protect and
mentor whistleblowers
an integrity audit regime that involves all personnel
being required to declare private interests and to
submit to testing for
illicit drugs in the workplace
senior management to provide statements of integrity
current NCA anti-corruption plan
7.10 The NCA has
advised the Commission that it currently has no formalised
anti-corruption plan but it has a number of measures
in place to mitigate against corruption in the NCA.
Those measures are discussed below. The Commission
considers that the NCA
should develop an anti-corruption
plan that includes a risk assessment for corruption
and regular integrity testing of all staff.
anti-corruption measures
7.11 The AFP advises
that it has other extensive anti-corruption measures
in place including the following:
the establishment of the Internal Investigations Division
(IID) and Internal Security and Audit (ISA) with the
latter having a
pro-active focus against corruption
fixed term appointments
a unified workforce and a performance management program
the inclusion of security, ethics, corruption and fraud
awareness in all training programs
ending of appointments under s 26E and proposed amendments
to the section to make it more effective in removing
officers who
have been found to be guilty of serious
loss of superannuation for conviction of corruption
offences and prescribed disciplinary offences
lowering of the burden of proof in disciplinary matters
from beyond reasonable doubt to the balance of probability
a recent restructuring to adopt a multi-skilled team
approach with the intention that the team concept will
act as a self regulator
by the rotation of personnel
between teams and throughout the organisation
establishment of the AFP Security and Audit Committee
in 1990 to reduce the risk of fraud and corruption
by ensuring in-depth
audit scrutiny of programs and
the AFP Protective Security Manual and the AFP Information
Technology Security Policy Statement which establishes
security clearance
a network of Regional Security Officers for each AFP
The AFP is also introducing as part of its accountability
and ethics reforms
a new remuneration scheme to reward those who increase
their capabilities and value to the AFP
greater use of electronic audio and video recording
equipment, particularly for detecting people committing
crimes and for recording
interactions between officers
and the public at police stations and offices.
The AFP is conducting research into the utility of
forensic lie detection (polygraphs) in assessing integrity.
anti-corruption measures
7.12 The NCA has
advised that it has the following anti-corruption measures
the use of small multi-disciplinary teams
rigorous external reporting requirements to the PJC,
the IGC, the Standing Committee on Organised Crime
and Criminal Intelligence
(SCOCCI) and the National
Co-ordination Committee
internal reporting through weekly reports to the General
Manager, Operations and the Authority from all its
secondment of investigators for a limited period, mainly
rotation of staff within regional offices
special care taken by the NCA in its recording and
control of information because of the scrutiny of
the NCA's coercive powers
a strict 'need to know' requirement for information
passing beyond investigation teams
management is closer to investigation teams than in
other law enforcement agencies because of the need
to ensure NCA standards
meet the expectations of the
NCA's partner agencies
a high level of internal control of exhibits and documents
strict supervision and control of assets
auditing of financial procedures and systems
annual audit by the Australian National Audit Office.
7.13 The NCA has
a Security Branch whose main function is to develop,
implement and monitor protective security within the
so as to ensure the integrity of its staff and
assets. It carries out security checks on staff and
potential members of staff.
There is also an internal
auditor who examines whether procedures are being followed.
The NCA has an Audit Committee which meets
year to review internal audit matters.
Role of the NIIC
7.14 The NIIC would
be a key agency in providing external review and assistance
to the AFP and the NCA in relation to anti-corruption
strategies and measures. The Commission proposes that
the NIIC should regularly consult with the AFP and
the NCA about their
anti-fraud plans and anti-corruption
plans. The NIIC would be empowered to conduct random
audits of all aspects of the process
and to make recommendations
annual reports to Parliament information about
AFP and NCA strategies and would report on the results
of its auditing. The AFP and the NCA in developing
and reviewing
these measures and strategies would need
to develop appropriate information links with the NIIC.
Draft recommendations
Need for planning
Draft recommendation 7.1.
The AFP and the NCA should
each develop comprehensive and co-ordinated anti-fraud
plans and anti-corruption plans. These plans
include effective risk assessment. The anti-corruption
plans should include thorough and regular integrity
Role of the NIIC
Draft recommendation 7.2.
The NIIC should regularly
consult with the AFP and the NCA about their anti-fraud
plans and anti-corruption plans. The NIIC would
empowered to conduct random audits of all aspects of
the process and to make recommendations about these
plans. The NIIC
would include in their annual reports
to Parliament information about the AFP and NCA strategies
and would report on the results
of its auditing. The
AFP and the NCA in developing and reviewing these measures
and strategies would need to develop appropriate
information
links with the NIIC.
Comments sought on anti-corruption measures
The Commission welcomes comments on the AFP and NCA
anti-corruption measures (eg relating to effectiveness
and fairness) discussed
above, including comments on
any additional measures.
and education
7.15 The Commission
considers that training and education of AFP and NCA
staff is significant in helping to reduce the causes
of complaints and corruption. Given that the complaints
and disciplinary systems proposed in this paper are
significantly different
to the current situation, AFP
and NCA staff would need to be trained for the operation
of these new systems. Information and
training should
also be included in induction packages and courses.
The NIIC should provide assistance in the training
requirements.
Draft recommendation
Training and education
Draft recommendation 7.3.
The AFP and the NCA in conjunction
with the NIIC should be responsible for providing training
to all AFP members about their
new systems and for
providing training to recruits and refresher training.
conduct and mission statements
7.16 There is a
growing interest in developing ethics and codes of
conduct within organisations including law enforcement
These measures can increase integrity and
accountability. The Canberra Police Association has
submitted that the AFP design and
implement an ethics
course for members and staff members to train them
fully on all issues regarding the complaints process.
7.17 The AFP has
advised that it is developing a Code of Conduct that
will be supplementary to other relevant policies such
as training. The Commission's view is that it would
be desirable for both the AFP and the NCA to have a
document that is accessible
to both their staff and
to the general public that sets out the rights and
obligations of staff and the public in relation to
making and resolving complaints. This could be part
of a broader code of conduct or a mission statement.
The Commission considers
that the document would be
a valuable educative measure for both the agencies
and the community. The Commission would not envisage
that a breach of the code or statement would confer
any legal right. The purpose would be to improve understanding
and awareness
and set out general standards of conduct.
Draft recommendation
Code of conduct and mission statements
Draft recommendation 7.4.
The AFP and the NCA should
have a document that is accessible to both their staff
and to the general public that sets out the
and obligations of staff and the public in relation
to making and resolving complaints. This could be part
of a broader
code of conduct or a mission statement.
A breach of the code or statement would not confer
any legal right.
Possibilities
of a national or regional anti-corruption strategy
7.18 There are
two possibilities to consider. The first is a national
strategy for Australia and the second is a strategy
incorporates not only Australia but also other
countries in the region.
7.19 The Commission
considers that a national strategy to ensure the integrity
and competency of Australian law enforcement officers
has many attractions. It recognises and takes account
of the fact that criminal activity and general law
enforcement issues
cross jurisdictional boundaries.
This is exemplified in the role of the NCA itself where
co-operation and joint operations with
national, State
and Territory law enforcement agencies is a primary
function. It also encourages the adoption of national
and allows greater professional support to
those seeking to apply these standards in any particular
7.20 This national
approach could include standards for recruitment, training,
policy and procedures and data systems. There have
been some developments in these areas.
7.21 The Australasian
Police Ministers Council (now the Ministerial Council
on the Administration of Justice) has endorsed the
development of an Australasian Policing Strategy.
One of the major goals is to enhance professionalism
and accountability including developing a common code
of ethics, a strategy
for police education and training
and developing strategies for dealing with serious
misconduct. One desirable innovation would
be the introduction
of a uniform complaint management data system which
would be very useful in assisting in comparisons of
different approaches to complaints including the resources
involved. The NSW Government has requested the Ministerial
on the Administration of Justice to consider
the introduction of such a system.
7.22 A further
development that suggests that a regional strategy
beyond Australasia is also a possibility is a result
AFP proposal at the Conference of Commissioners
of Police of Australia and the South West Pacific region
in March 1996. The Conference
resolved to
produce a clear, understandable and viable definition
of corruption
support the proposal to define a set of anti-corruption
measures for consideration by all agencies in the region
require the Police Commissioners' Policy Advisory Group
(PCPAG) to advance this proposal out of session and
to report to the next
direct the PCPAG to pay particular attention to the
recommendations of the NSW Royal Commission into the
Police and the Fifth
Australasian Internal Investigations
request the PCPAG to examine and report to the Conference
on the resource and operation efficiency implications
of each of the
measures that it recommends.
The Commission is interested in comment on the feasibility
and content of any national strategy and whether an
Australasian or
extensive regional approach is possible
and desirable.

8. Scope of complaints systems
