Introduction
8.1 This chapter
considers the scope of complaints systems for the AFP
and the NCA.
of complaint
8.2 The Commission
in its issues paper asked whether 'complaint' should
be defined in the Act. Most submissions suggested that
there was no need to define complaint on the basis
that it was better to leave complaint undefined rather
than risk too narrow
a definition.
The AFP has submitted that complaint should be defined.
The AFP wanted the definition to make it clear that
persons who in the
course of their employment with
a government instrumentality communicate information
to the AFP which is capable of being a complaint
the Act are not to be regarded as complainants unless
they specifically request this. The NCA in its submission
advocate a definition of complaint but submitted
that complaints mechanisms should not deal with policy
or resource issues.
8.3 The Commission
has decided that in light of its recommendation to
remove the distinction between complaints and allegations
a new definition of complaint is required. The Commission
proposes the following definition. Complaint should
be defined to
include all situations where a person
expressly or by implication seeks review of, or challenges,
the validity or appropriateness
of the conduct of an
AFP appointee or of the AFP as an organisation. The
proposed definition would remove the distinction between
complaints and allegations and would be sufficiently
broad so as to encourage people to come forward with
their grievances.
The broad definition would ensure
that the NIIC and others do not unnecessarily restrict
the ambit of what can be raised.
of the distinction between 'allegations' and 'complaints'
8.4 Currently there
is a distinction between 'complaints' and 'allegations'.
'Allegations' are determined according to the procedures
set out in AFP General Order 6 and are defined to mean
'a report' made by an AFP member or by the public 'concerning
the behaviour
of an appointee not being a matter to
which the Complaints Act applies'.
Matters to which the Complaints Act apparently does
not apply are:
internal complaints made to an AFP appointee
'off duty' conduct
disputes relating specifically to the employment of
an AFP member.
8.5 'Off duty' conduct
is behaviour that is unconnected to the exercise, or
purported exercise, of police powers or the performance,
or purported performance, of police functions. The
distinction between off duty conduct and conduct where
a member purports
to act as a police officer is often
complex. Some submissions suggested that because police
officers enjoy a privileged position
of trust and exercise
extensive powers as well as having a public duty to
uphold and enforce the law, police must aspire to exemplary
standards in order to maintain community confidence.
Even off duty behaviour can reflect on the police force.
For example,
an officer who constantly gets drunk
while off duty in hotels, might be seen as bringing
discredit on the reputation of the AFP.
Off duty conduct
may indicate a potential for corruption. For example,
a police officer who uses drugs recreationally may
supplied by criminals raising the possibility of
blackmail and the request for favours in response to
police protection of illegal
activities.
8.6 The Australian
Federal Police Amendment Bill 1996 which deals with
the power of Commissioner to terminate appointments
the basis of serious misconduct adopts the following
definition of 'serious misconduct' that appears to
cover off duty conduct:
corruption, a serious abuse of power or a serious dereliction
any other seriously reprehensible act or behaviour
by a person whether acting, or purporting to act, in
the course of his or her
duties as a member or staff
. [italics added].
The Commission is of the view that off duty conduct
should remain predominantly a matter for the AFP to
deal with internally if
it raises issues about a member's
suitability for the profession of policing. The Commission
recognises, however, that there
may be some situations
where the NIIC should investigate off duty conduct,
particularly where there is a suggestion of serious
misconduct. The Commission proposes that off duty conduct
should be able to come within the definition of a complaint.
the NIIC would have a discretion to send matters
involving off duty conduct to the AFP. The NIIC would
be able to require a report
with a particular matter involving off duty conduct.
of internal complaints
8.7 A number of
submissions including those from the AFP and the Attorney-General's
Department made the point that there was merit
oversight of internal complaints by an external body
and suggested that the determination of all allegations
AFP should be regularly audited and reported
on by the external agency. The Commission supports
Unnecessary
distinction
8.8 The Commission
is of the view that the current distinction between
complaints under the Complaints Act and allegations
General Order 6 is both confusing and unnecessary.
The introduction of one system of complaints that
covers the current category
of allegations would allow
the NIIC to have a complete picture of the types of
complaints that are being made about the AFP and
assist in providing an overview of the AFP's performance.
Distinction
between misconduct and employment performance
8.9 It is the Commission's
view that a relevant distinction is between complaints
that relate to misconduct and complaints that
merely to employment performance and suitability. The
Commission is therefore of the view that all matters
which would
previously have been regarded as falling
under General Order 6 would now fall within the broader
definition of a complaint and
would fall within the
review of the NIIC. However, the Commission proposes
that the NIIC would have a discretion and a system
of guidelines to refer matters back to the AFP and
the NCA that relate solely to internal management.
As stated earlier the
Commission envisages that these
guidelines would deal with matters such as off duty
Interagency
communications
8.10 In the issues
paper the question of interagency communications was
raised with the example given of a communication between
the DPP and the AFP about an AFP member's failure to
appear at a court hearing. Because it is a statement
of an AFP officer it would be regarded
as falling within the current definition of complaint
despite the unsuitability of dealing
with the matter
in this way. The NIIC should therefore have a power
under the guidelines it develops to deem that interagency
communications are not complaints unless the agency
requests the communication to be dealt with in that
Draft recommendations
Removal of the distinction between 'allegations' and
'complaints'
Draft recommendation 8.1.
The distinction between allegations
and complaints should be abolished with matters currently
defined as allegations under General
Order 6 to be
regarded as complaints.
Draft recommendation 8.2.
The NIIC should have a discretion
to refer matters that relate solely to internal management
and resolution.
Draft recommendation 8.3.
In conjunction with the AFP
and NCA the NIIC would draft a set of guidelines which
deal with the matters that will be referred
the AFP and NCA.
Draft recommendation 8.4.
Complaint should be defined
to include all situations where a person expressly
or by implication seeks review of, or challenges,
validity or appropriateness of the conduct of an AFP
appointee or of the AFP as an organisation. 'Conduct'
should be defined
to include any action or inaction
(or alleged action or inaction):
whether or not it also involves non-AFP participants
whether or not it occurred while the AFP appointee
was officially on duty.
Draft recommendation 8.5.
A similar definition of complaint
should be adopted for the purposes of the NCA.
Draft recommendation 8.6.
The NIIC should have a power
to deem that interagency communications are not complaints
unless the agency requests the communication
dealt with under the Act.
not to investigate
8.11 Section 24
of the Complaints Act provides the Commonwealth Ombudsman
with a number of discretions to determine that a complaint
should not be investigated or should not be investigated
further. The Ombudsman can exercise the discretion
not to investigate
she is satisfied that the complainant became aware
of the action complained of more than 12 months before
the complaint was made
if in her opinion:
(i) the complaint is frivolous or vexatious or was
not made in good faith; or
(ii) the complainant does not have a sufficient interest
in the subject matter of the complaint
if a person has been charged with an offence or breach
of discipline or offered a caution or admonition for
a breach of discipline
in relation to the action complained
if in her opinion, the investigation or further investigation
of that action is not warranted having regard to all
the circumstances.
8.12 The Ombudsman
also has power to determine that a complaint shall
not be investigated where a complainant has sought
by a court or tribunal or has a right to have
the action complained of reviewed by a court or tribunal.
8.13 The Commission
is of the view that each of these discretions should
be available to the NIIC. The Ombudsman's submission
suggested that these discretions are broad enough.
In relation to the 12 month limitation the Department
of Immigration and
Ethnic Affairs has suggested that
the limitation period should be increased so that people
from non-English speaking backgrounds
who have cause
to complain have a sufficient opportunity to become
familiar with the complaints processes of the AFP before
the time period elapses. The Department in its submission
gives the example of refugees from a country where
the police are
part of a repressive regime. These refugees
distrust the police and the independence of any complaints
mechanism. A twelve month
limitation period therefore
would not provide an adequate opportunity for a refugee
to gain trust in the Australian complaints
and lodge a complaint. The Commission supports the
extension of the time limit to a period of two years.
It recognises
that the NIIC would still have the more
general discretions which it can apply if investigation
in all the circumstances is thought
not to be warranted.
Draft recommendation
Discretion not to investigate
Draft recommendation 8.7.
Each of the discretions contained
in s 24 of the Complaints Act should be available to
the NIIC although the time limit in s
24(1)(a) should
be extended to two years.
be subject to a complaint?
8.14 Complaints
may be made about 'action taken by an AFP appointee',
whether or not the AFP appointee is identified.
'AFP appointee' is defined as:
(a) a member of the Australian Federal Police; or
(b) a staff member of the Australian Federal Police;
(c) a special member of the Australian Federal Police.
The Commission considers that all AFP employees and
staff should come within the complaints system including
AFP temporary employees,
secondees and consultants
engaged by the AFP. Having all categories of staff
covered by the legislation reflects the reality
any staff member can be the subject of a complaint.
For example, complaints could cover unauthorised access
to information.
By ensuring that all staff are covered
by the complaints system the AFP will be encouraged
to take a wider view of the complaints
system than
merely regulating police powers.
For the purposes of the complaints legislation the
definition of an AFP appointee should be amended to
clearly cover consultants
and secondees.
'Collective
8.15 The current
approach focuses on individual police employees. Although
the Ombudsman can investigate a complaint which is
in substance 'about the practices and procedures of
the Australian Federal Police'
a complaint must still arise from the actions of an
individual AFP appointee. The Commission proposes that
the Act should clearly
indicate that collective action
by the AFP can also give rise to a complaint within
the Act. This would allow a greater focus
on organisational
deficiencies identified in complaints.
and scope of complaints legislation
8.16 The range
of NCA employees covered by the proposed NIIC Act is
dealt with in chapter 4.
Draft recommendations
Who may be subject to a complaint?
Draft recommendation 8.8.
All employees, temporary
employees, secondees and consultants of the AFP should
be covered by the complaints legislation.
Draft recommendation 8.9.
The Act should provide that
a complaint can be about the AFP as an organisation
or about the collective action of the AFP.
be able to complain?
8.17 The Complaints
Act does not clearly define who can make a complaint.
The Act provides that the Ombudsman may determine that
a complaint should not be investigated if the 'complainant
does not have a sufficient interest in the subject
matter of the
complaint'.
The practice of the Ombudsman is to accept complaints
made by individuals or organisations on behalf of the
person who is directly
affected by, or a witness to,
the conduct that gives rise to the complaint provided
there is some indication that the individual
has authorised the agent to make the complaint.
'Sufficient
8.18 The Act does
not define 'sufficient interest'. The term could be
applied broadly to include a member of the public with
personal knowledge of, or a special interest in, the
circumstances giving rise to a complaint, or narrowly
so as to be limited
to those directly affected by the
events giving rise to the complaint.
8.19 A broad test
of standing has the potential to increase the number
of complaints and it may increase access by Aborigines
and Torres Strait Islanders, youth and persons from
a non-English speaking background to the complaints
system if they can
complain through bodies such as
community legal centres. To ensure broad access to
the complaints system the legislation should
allow for complaints by agents and different bodies
on behalf of complainants.
There is no need, however, to define who these organisations
or individuals could be as to do so would curtail the
complainant to ask any person or organisation
to make the complaint on his or her behalf. The legislation
should provide that
groups or corporations may lodge
complaints as entities.
of authority
8.20 The Commission
is of the view that there is no need to specify that
an agent must have evidence of authority to act as
an agent. At present there is no requirement for proof
of authority and the Ombudsman is able to deal with
the matter in a common
sense way. If there is uncertainty
as to whether the complainant has authorised the person
or organisation lodging the complaint
to do so the
Ombudsman contacts the individual, or asks the person
lodging the complaint to provide written authorisation
requests the complainant to contact the Ombudsman.
Formalising the system of authorisation is likely to
lead to a more rigid
and complex system which may deter
complainants with low literacy skills and result in
less access to the system.
Draft recommendations
Who should be able to complain?
Draft recommendation 8.10.
The requirement of standing
should be open to any person or body with sufficient
interest. There is no need to define 'sufficient
more specifically. The legislation should specify that
organisations or corporate bodies may lodge a complaint
the same way as an individual.
Draft recommendation 8.11.
The legislation should expressly
allow complaints by organisations on behalf of individuals
but there is no need to specify the
organisations.
Draft recommendation 8.12.
The legislation should not
specify a requirement of proof for an agent to lodge
and proceed with a complaint.
anonymous complaints be accepted?
8.21 A complaint
to an AFP appointee can be made whether or not the
identity of the complainant is known to the member
who receives
the complaint.
The Act is silent concerning anonymous complaints
to the Ombudsman, but it appears they are accepted
subject to the Ombudsman's
discretion to determine
that a complaint should not be investigated if 'the
investigation, or further investigation, of that
is not warranted having regard to all the circumstances'.
There are no express provisions concerning anonymous
'allegations'. The Ombudsman's records show that the
following numbers of
anonymous complaints have been
In 1993/94, 7 anonymous complaints were received of
which 4 were investigated
In 1994/95, 8 anonymous complaints were received of
which 1 was investigated
From June 1995 to February 1996, 11 anonymous complaints
were received of which three have, or are in the process,
of being investigated.
The proportion of anonymous complaints for the years
1993/94 and 1994/95 is therefore less than 1.3% with
a much smaller proportion
of those anonymous complaints
being investigated.
8.22 Many complainants,
especially police officers, fear victimisation when
they make a complaint.
The opportunity to make anonymous complaints encourages
complaints to be made because complainants need not
fear retribution.
This promotes the legislative aim of ensuring the
disclosure of instances of police deviance, particularly
internal complaints
which are most likely to reveal
serious police misconduct.
The Ombudsman has stated that several serious complaints
have been lodged anonymously by AFP appointees which
upon investigation
have been found to have had substance.
8.23 During the
Commission's consultations some members of the AFP
objected to the fact that complaints could be made
anonymously.
They argued that this gave people the
opportunity to make malicious or false complaints without
fear of punishment. The Ombudsman's
Office has said
that in the case of anonymous complaints the Office
will often carry out some preliminary inquiries to
whether there is any likely substance in the complaint
and whether there is sufficient information to enable
the complaint to
be investigated. A full investigation
is usually only conducted when the answer to both of
these questions is in the affirmative.
The Ombudsman
has a discretion to decide that a complaint which lacks
proper substance ought not be investigated and the
supplied by the Ombudsman suggest this discretion
8.24 The Commission
considers that the very small number of anonymous complaints
which are actually investigated by the Ombudsman
year suggests that anonymous complaints are not being
used widely to victimise police. The Commission is
of the view that
anonymous complaints should continue
to be able to be accepted given that they have in the
past revealed serious misconduct and
that they provide
an avenue for complaint for police officers in particular
who may fear victimisation.
8.25 The NSW Police
Legislation Amendment Bill 1996 provides that the Ombudsman
should not investigate an anonymous complaint
of the opinion:
that the complaint appears to contain sufficient information
to enable an investigation to be carried out and
that the conduct which is the subject of the complaint
- if the conduct had occurred, provide reasonable grounds
to believe that a criminal offence had been committed
by a police officer,
- if a departmental charge were proved against a police
officer in relation to the conduct, warrant the imposition
of a substantial
punishment on the police officer.
The Commission considers that a similar provision should
be included in the AFP and NCA complaints legislation.
8.26 No particular
problems with anonymous complaints in relation to the
NCA have been brought to the Commission's attention.
The Commission's tentative view is that the same approach
should be adopted for the NCA as proposed for the AFP.
Draft recommendations
Should anonymous complaints be accepted?
Draft recommendation 8.13.
Anonymous complaints should
continue to be accepted. However, an anonymous complaint
should only be investigated where:
the complaint appears to contain sufficient information
to enable an investigation to be carried out and
the conduct which is the subject of the complaint if
it had occurred would constitute a criminal offence
or a disciplinary offence.
There is no need to specify that anonymous complaints
must be made to the NIIC.
Draft recommendation 8.14.
The NIIC does not require
a specific discretion not to investigate anonymous
complaints as the discretion to determine that investigation
is not warranted in all the circumstances is sufficient.
offences to achieve proper scope of complaints system
8.27 There are
a number of offences that can be used to help achieve
an appropriate balance between encouraging legitimate
but deterring or punishing false ones.
8.28 It is an offence
to make a false complaint under the Complaints Act.
However, proceedings for the offence cannot be brought
without the consent in writing of the Ombudsman or,
if the Ombudsman refuses
to consent, the Attorney-General.
The offence was introduced following a recommendation
of a Working Party consisting of the
AFP, the Commonwealth
Ombudsman and the federal Attorney-General's Department.
The Senate Standing Committee on Legal and Constitutional
Affairs also agreed with the introduction of the offence.
of the offence
8.29 There are
different views about the necessary width of the section
and the appropriate penalty. For example, the Northern
Territory Act
provides for an extremely broad offence of intentionally
creating a 'false impression' which would lead to the
investigation
of a complaint. However, the consent
of the Northern Territory DPP is required before the
charge may be laid. In Queensland,
it is also an offence
to make a frivolous or vexatious complaint to the Criminal
Justice Commission,
and if the offence is proven the offender may be ordered
to pay the costs of any investigation which is instituted.
In South Australia the false complaint offence includes
an offence of preventing a person from making a complaint.
Any prosecution is subject to the consent of the Police
Complaints Authority.
8.30 One view is
that the process for dealing with false or vexatious
complaints is necessary to ensure that the complaints
mechanism retains internal credibility.
The Ombudsman's view is that on balance there is probably
a place for such an offence but only with at least
the existing safeguards
in place. The Ombudsman still
has some concerns about the offence and whether it
acts as a deterrent.
8.31 The Commission
considers that the offence of a false complaint should
be retained in order to ensure a proper balance in
the system. The Commission proposes that the offence
should be amended to ensure that a court or tribunal
can award the costs
of any investigation into the false
complaint against any person found guilty of the offence.
The NIIC should monitor the way
in which complainants
and potential complainants are informed of the existence
of the offence by police so as to ensure that
of the offence is not used as a way of improperly
deterring people from making complaints.
8.32 The Commission
has received no specific submissions on the application
of this offence to the NCA. It is of the view however
that the offence should apply to false complaints concerning
Draft recommendation
False complaints
Draft recommendation 8.15.
The false complaints offence
should be retained but should be amended to allow a
court on conviction of a person to award the
of any investigation into the false complaint against
that person.
Victimisation
of complainants
8.33 The AFP Complaints
Working Party
recommended that there be an offence of victimisation.
The Senate Standing Committee on Legal and Constitutional
Affairs also
recommended that an offence of victimisation
be created to balance the proposed offence of false
In 1994 an offence of victimisation was inserted in
the Complaints Act as follows
s 88A. A person must not cause, or threaten to cause,
detriment to another person (the victim) on the ground
that the victim,
or any other person:
(a) has made or might make a complaint under this Act;
(b) has made or might give a document or other information
to a person under this Act.
Penalty: Imprisonment for 6 months.
8.34 The rationale
for the offence is clear. Complainants need to be encouraged
to come forward with grievances and to be made
that they are protected from possible victimisation
including threats of prosecution for making a false
complaint or
from civil action by a member against
whom the complaint is made.
The Commission is of the view that the offence of
victimisation should be retained. No submissions have
been received in relation
to the NCA and the availability
of this offence. The Commission considers that this
offence should also apply to matters arising
in relation
to the NCA.
Draft recommendation
Victimisation of complainants
Draft recommendation 8.16.
The offence of victimisation
should be retained under the NIIC Act and should apply
to complaints concerning the NCA.
substance of a complaint to another AFP member
8.35 It is an offence
for an AFP officer, staff member or person investigating
a complaint to divulge information supplied to
or acquired
by the AFP during the investigation of a complaint
except in the performance of his or her duties or with
the consent
in writing of the appropriate person. The
section does not prevent a person from disclosing information
in a production of a
report made under the Act or in
evidence before a court or disciplinary tribunal.
It is also an offence for the Ombudsman or her staff
to divulge or communicate information obtained in the
course of investigation
of a complaint.
8.36 These offences
are intended to protect the confidentiality of information
relating to complaints and their investigation.
there is an exception that allows the Commissioner
a discretion to disclose information to any person
or to the public
if in the opinion of the Commissioner
it is in the interests of the AFP or the public.
The AFP uses this discretion to disclose information
to supervisors and managers when patterns of complaints
appointees are detected by IID. This
information is now also taken into account when considering
dismissal or reappointment of
appointees.
8.37 The Ombudsman
has said that difficulties remain over how much information
should be disclosed for such purposes.
The Ombudsman is concerned that protection of the
complainant's right to complain may be curtailed if
complainants believe that
their personal details and
complaints could become widely known within the AFP.
There have also been cases where information
was disclosed
to operational areas of the AFP in order to investigate
the individual complainant. The Ombudsman argues that
this should only occur where there is overwhelming
public interest in that investigation as balanced against
the public interest
in protecting the integrity of
the complaints system.
8.38 According
to the Ombudsman the information required by the AFP
to supervise and manage appointees does not need to
information identifying the complainant. The
Ombudsman suggests that the secrecy provisions should
be redrafted to provide that
information relating to
appointees, including complaint investigation details,
should be available for prescribed purposes within
the AFP, but that personal information identifying
the complainant should not be disclosed without the
consent of the Ombudsman.
To protect the internal confidentiality
of the complaints process, secrecy provisions need
also apply to information obtained
by AFP appointees
as a result of a complaint investigation. This will
ensure that the integrity of the complaints system
protected and appointees are accountable.
and secrecy provisions
8.39 The Commission
is of the view that similar provisions should apply
to the NCA. The current secrecy provisions applying
to the NCA are discussed in chapter 4.
Draft recommendation
AFP and NCA secrecy provisions
Draft recommendation 8.17.
The secrecy provisions in
s 87 should be redrafted to ensure that information
relating to appointees does not need to include
information
identifying the complainant. The legislation should
provide that information relating to appointees, including

but that personal
information identifying the complainant
should not be disclosed without the consent of the
and encouragement of whistleblowers
8.40 Whistleblowers
are people in organisations who report corrupt, illegal,
fraudulent or harmful activity by the organisation
or members of it.
8.41 The first
whistleblowers legislation was passed in Queensland
in the form of
Whistleblowers (Interim Protection)
and Miscellaneous Amendments Act 1990
(Qld). This legislation
was primarily aimed at providing interim protection
for whistleblowers until fully fledged legislation
was passed. The next State to introduce whistleblower
legislation was South Australia with the passage of
Whistleblowers Protection Act 1993
(SA). Other
legislation passed includes the
Whistleblowers Protection
Protected Disclosures Act 1994
(NSW) and the
Public Interest Disclosure Act 1994
Western Australia has made some amendments to the
Corruption Commission Act 1988
(WA) and has thus provided
a degree of protection to whistleblowers in that State.
8.42 A Senate Select
Committee on Public Interest Whistleblowing issued
a report in August 1994 and recommended that Commonwealth
legislation should be passed establishing an independent
agency to be known as the Public Interest Disclosures
Agency (the
Agency). The role of the Agency would be
to receive public interest disclosures and arrange
for an appropriate authority to investigate
them. Other
functions would be to ensure the protection of people
making such disclosures, to provide a national education
program and to develop and oversee recommendations
relating to the Agency's role.
8.43 The Committee
recommended that the AFP be covered by the whistleblower
protection legislation. It also commented that there
appeared to be a lack of successful police force reform
to date and that additional education initiatives and
strategies should
be directed at whistleblower protection
in State and federal police services. Other recommendations
were directed at changing
community attitudes towards
whistleblowers and informing the public of the public
benefits flowing from whistleblowing.
8.44 On 1 December
1994 a further Senate Select Committee was established
to inquire into unresolved whistleblower cases. The
report of this Committee was tabled in October 1995.
A response by the previous government to the earlier
report was tabled
in Parliament on 26 October 1995.
The Government stated that it would introduce whistleblowing
legislation as part of its response
to the Gibbs committee's
recommendations
and as part of its response to the recommendations
of the Samuels' Commission of Inquiry into ASIS.
However, no further action was taken on introducing
whistleblowers legislation before the recent change
of government.
8.45 The NSW Royal
Commission into Police has highlighted the ethical
dilemmas and fears that many would-be whistleblowers
Whistleblowers must be encouraged and protected
and this is especially so in relation to organisations
like the AFP and the NCA
where there may be cultures
that victimise or intimidate whistleblowers.
Draft recommendation
Protection and encouragement of whistleblowers
Draft recommendation 8.18.
Legislation to protect and
encourage whistleblowers is necessary. Although legislation
covering all Commonwealth agencies would
be desirable
there is a pressing need for legislation that covers
the AFP and the NCA.

9. Processing complaints
Introduction
Information
and advertising
Advertising
duty on the AFP to provide information
importance of recording complaints
should complaints be recorded?
to record a complaint
Introduction
9.1 This chapter
examines receiving and recording complaints and providing
information about the complaints systems.
9.2 The Complaints
Act does not specifically prescribe who may receive
a complaint. It is only when a complaint is lodged
the Ombudsman or an AFP appointee that the complaints
procedure under the Act is invoked.
9.3 The Commission's
view is that although complainants should be able to
ask any individual or group to lodge a complaint on
their behalf, it is not desirable to widen significantly
the range of places at which complaints can be lodged
formally. The
Commission proposes that complaints will
be regarded as formally lodged when they are lodged
with an AFP appointee, an NCA staff
member, the Commonwealth
Ombudsman or with the NIIC. The Ombudsman should be
retained as a lodgement point because of the fact
she has offices in all States and Territories apart
from Tasmania and because she has already built up
a public profile
in the community in relation to police
complaints. On receiving complaints the Ombudsman would
be obliged to forward them directly
to the NIIC. The
Commission also believes that the State and Territory
anti-discrimination bodies, ICAC, the CJC and the NSW
PIC as well other State and Territory external agencies
for handling police complaints might be possible reception
complaints against the AFP and the NCA.
The Commission invites comments on this suggestion.
Draft recommendations
Receiving complaints
Draft recommendation 9.1.
The legislation should specify
that complaints can be lodged with the NIIC, the Commonwealth
Ombudsman, the AFP, the NCA and
the NIIC. The complainant
would be able to nominate an agent to act on his or
her behalf.
Draft recommendation 9.2.
The legislation should specify
that complaints lodged with a Commonwealth Ombudsman's
office should be forwarded to the NIIC
within 48 hours
of receipt. Confidentiality obligations should also
Information
and advertising
Advertising
9.4 There is a need
for greater public accessibility to the AFP and NCA
complaints systems. Advertising and information strategies
should target those most likely to be unaware of the
system or who lack the confidence to use it. Campaigns
should be directed
towards youth, ethnic groups, women,
gays and lesbians, Aborigines and Torres Strait Islanders
and the socially disadvantaged.
These campaigns should
be conducted in innovative and accessible ways. For
example, in targeting youth, the NIIC might consider
making itself available for interviews or providing
information that could be played on television or radio
programs that have
a large youth audience. Advertising
and information could be directed towards youth magazines
and newspapers as well as schools,
drop in centres,
sporting organisations, welfare organisations and other
venues that cater for a youth audience. The NIIC should
consult with youth workers and others working in the
field to ensure that its advertising campaigns are
run in ways that are
accessible to, and effective for,
targeted groups such as youth.
9.5 Information
for groups from non-English speaking backgrounds should
be directed towards the ethnic press and radio and
services which are used by ethnic groups. Information
for indigenous Australians could be disseminated through
ATSIC, the Aboriginal
Legal Service and through media
programs and information outlets delivering services
to indigenous Australians.
9.6 Information
could also be disseminated in appropriate places including
police stations, community legal centres and migrant
centres. There could be a complaints against police
'hotline' under the supervision of the Commissioner
for Complaints. The
Commissioner for Complaints should
employ an Aboriginal and Torres Straits Islander liaison
officer and should put in place outreach
programs to
with special needs. Each of these measures would involve
some additional resources but it should be recognised
that simply translating information into different
languages or providing
pamphlets will not ensure broad
access to the complaints system. Innovative campaigns
should also be conducted to target other
specific groups
such as women and the gay and lesbian community. Both
mainstream and alternative avenues should be explored
and the focus should be on both written and oral presentations
to ensure that those with literacy problems are not
from the information campaign.
Draft recommendations
Information and advertising
Draft recommendation 9.3.
The NIIC in consultation
with the Commonwealth Ombudsman should conduct advertising
and information campaigns in innovative and
ways targeting youth, women, gays and lesbians, Aboriginal
and Torres Strait Islanders, people from non-English
speaking backgrounds and the socially disadvantaged.
The NIIC should ensure that its campaigns are conducted
both in written
and oral forms so that those with literacy
problems are not excluded.
Draft recommendation 9.4.
The Commissioner for Complaints
operate a hotline for complaints against the AFP and
have an Aboriginal and Torres Straits Islander liaison
officer and
should conduct outreach programs directed at specific
ethnic groups and other groups with special needs.
duty on the AFP to provide information
9.7 The Commission
asked in the issues paper whether there should be a
duty on police to inform a person about the complaints
system after any significant interaction with the police
or whether there should be a legislative requirement
that those interrogated
in police custody be advised
that they have a right to make a complaint.
9.8 The majority
of submissions which addressed these issues contended
that there should be not be a legislative requirement
on the police. Some submissions indicated that this
would be tantamount to inviting complaints while others
suggested that imposing
this requirement on police
would lead to a situation where police would provide
the information in such a way as to actively
potential complainants from taking up their complaints.
The Ombudsman, on the other hand, thought that there
some merit in this proposal.
9.9 The Commission
considers that it is not practical to place a duty
on police to inform a person about the complaints system
after any significant interaction with the police.
This would be lead to complex debate about what constituted
a significant
interaction. However, the Commission
does support an obligation on police to inform a person
under arrest of the existence of
the complaints process.
Crimes Act 1914
(Cth) which deals with
the obligations of police when investigating a Commonwealth
crime should be amended to provide this duty.
The Commission
is of the view that it is essential for people to know
as crucial as other rights during the investigative
process. There is a significant problem with people
being unaware of their
right to complain or being directly
or indirectly intimidated or deterred from making a
legitimate complaint. This proposal of
a legislative
duty would ensure that people under arrest would be
properly informed of their rights. It would be a positive
reinforcement of the independence and effectiveness
of the complaints system. Taping or video-recording
of interviews would
allow the method and manner of
the provision of the information by the police to be
monitored so as to avoid intimidation of
complainants.
importance of recording complaints
9.10 There is considerable
evidence that police may under-record complaints.
This is often due to an antagonistic or defensive
attitude towards the complaints system. Under-recording
of complaints may be
reduced by changing the police's
perception and culture.
should complaints be recorded?
9.11 Currently
the Act requires various particulars of complaints
which are referred to the IID to be recorded.
The particulars that must be recorded are set out
in the Complaints Regulations and include:
the names of the complainant, the AFP appointee who
is the subject of the complaint, the AFP appointee
to whom the complaint was
made (if applicable) and
the investigator
the dates of the making of the complaint or the referral
from (or to) the Ombudsman (as applicable) and of any
referral from the
Disciplinary Tribunal to the Commissioner
for further investigation
a description of the substance of the complaint
the details and outcome of any charge.
9.12 More limited
recording requirements are imposed on the Ombudsman.
There are also annual reporting requirements placed
and the Ombudsman
concerning police complaints. The specific requirements
for annual statistics include details of the total
number and different
of complaints which were substantiated, unable to be
substantiated, not substantiated or withdrawn,
number of complaints in which the complainant and AFP
appointee were reconciled and the number that were
determined should
not be investigated.
Other useful information could be obtained for assessing
the operations of the complaints system or of the police.
9.13 The issues
paper asked whether there should be a requirement to
record more comprehensive details about the complainant
the complainant's sex
whether the complainant is a juvenile
the language spoken at home
the ethnicity of a complainant
whether the complainant is Aboriginal
whether the complainant is disabled.
9.14 A number of
submissions and those who were consulted suggested
that such information if it is to be accurate must
be collected
from the complainant. Requests for this
information might cause complainants to feel discriminated
against or threatened. The
Ombudsman submitted that
her office was currently exploring how to obtain useful
data about ethnicity and other demographic data
discouraging complainants by asking intrusive questions.
The Ombudsman believed that other methods such as surveys
were preferable ways of obtaining this information.
The Commission considers that at the very least the
collection of such information
should be voluntary
and this should be explained to the complainant along
with an explanation of the reason why the information
is collected and any persons or agencies to whom the
information might be disclosed.
9.15 The Commission
also suggested in the issues paper that it might be
useful to record more comprehensive details about the
AFP appointee who is the subject of the complaint.
At present, only the name, rank and number of the member
must be recorded.
The Ombudsman has questioned whether it is useful
to prescribe legislatively any further details than
those above as she believes
the categories under which
it is important to record details will change over
time. The Federal Privacy Commissioner has submitted
that deriving further details about AFP appointees
from existing personnel records would be likely to
fall under Information
so long as the use of such information was confined
to internal management purposes within the agency concerned.
However, as
the use of the information for this purpose
would not have been envisaged at the time when the
information was collected the
Commissioner thought
it would be wise to consult with staff first as not
to do so could discourage staff from providing such
data in future. At the same time any use of such data
would need to be carefully monitored to ensure that
officers or staff
could not be identified despite not
being named.
9.16 The Commission
proposes that no further details for complainants,
appointees or the complaint itself should be specified
in the legislation as it would be making the process
too rigid when what is necessary is targeted research.
A legislative requirement
of more detailed information
in every case would create delays and additional costs
and would also run the risk of intimidating
complainants.
If the NIIC as part of its research function wanted
further information about complainants and AFP or NCA
it should conduct such research on a voluntary
basis and by other means such as surveys.
to record a complaint
9.17 The issues
paper asked whether there should be a specific criminal
or disciplinary offence of failing to record a complaint.
Failure to record a complaint can already constitute
a disciplinary offence under the broad provisions of
AFP General Order
5. The Commission considers that
specific offences are desirable. The recording of complaints
constitutes a fundamental part
of an effective and
fair complaints system. A failure to record obstructs
the whole process. It may frustrate potential complainants,
allow police who have misconducted themselves to escape
detection and appropriate sanction and it may contribute
to a misleading
picture of the performance of the law
enforcement agency. Under-recording of complaints is
a significant problem that must be
addressed. The Commission
proposes that a deliberate failure to record a complaint
should be a criminal offence while a negligent
or careless
failure to record a complaint should be a specific
disciplinary offence.

Recording complaints
Draft recommendation 9.5.
There should be a legislative
duty on police to inform persons under arrest of the
existence of the complaints process. Part
Crimes Act 1914
(Cth) should be amended to include
Draft recommendation 9.6.
The recording of complaints
by the appropriate officer should be a legislative
requirement. A deliberate failure to record a
should be a criminal offence, while a negligent or
careless failure to record a complaint should be a
specific disciplinary

Information Privacy Principle 10 deals with limits
on the use of personal information and provides that
a record keeper who has
records containing personal
information obtained for a particular purpose shall
not use the information obtained for other purposes
unless the individuals involved have consented, it
is authorised by law or it is necessary for enforcement
of a criminal law
or other law. Other situations where
the information could be used are where the record
keeper believes on reasonable grounds
that the information
is necessary to prevent or lessen a serious or imminent
threat to the life or health of an individual or
purpose for which the information is used is directly
related to the purpose for which the information was
10. The territories

external territories

and interpreting services
Introduction
10.1 This chapter
discusses special issues that arise in relation to
complaints systems for the ACT, Jervis Bay and the
territories. The AFP has a community policing
role in each of these territories.
10.2 Under the
Administrative Arrangements Order the federal Attorney-General
has responsibility for the AFP Act and the Complaints
and the Minister for Justice has portfolio responsibility
for the AFP. The ACT Attorney-General has pointed out
that despite
these arrangements the ACT community views
the ACT Attorney-General as the responsible minister
in regard to community policing
and the complaints
process. The ACT Attorney-General would like to see
the AFP complaints legislation amended so that complaints
that raise issues about policy and practices are notified
to him so that those broader issues can be dealt with
as separate
matters. The ACT Standing Committee on
Legal Affairs has recommended that legislation or procedures
be developed to allow the
ACT Attorney-General to have
an appropriate level of input into the handling of
complaints against police and that the legislation
be amended to enable, where appropriate, the Commonwealth
Ombudsman to make recommendations to the ACT Police
Commissioner
and the Chief Minister of the ACT.
10.3 The Ombudsman
in her submission recognised the need for information
to be provided to the ACT Government but argued that
the provision of information should not disclose personal
details of the complainant. The Ombudsman also considered
that rather
than providing the ACT Government with
information on all complaints that relate to ACT policing
it would be more useful to provide
information about
complaints trends and special briefings on particular
matters of significant concern to the ACT Attorney-General
or the ACT public. The AFP agreed that the Complaints
Act should be amended to allow for information concerning
relevant complaints
to be passed to the ACT Attorney-General.
10.4 The Commission
considers that it is important for the ACT Government
to have clear and timely access to information about
policing matters and complaints that arise in the ACT
or that affect the ACT. This is appropriate because
the ACT provides
funding for the service and because
the ACT community looks to the ACT government to take
responsibility for policing issues.
Any information
provided should be subject to privacy considerations
particularly in relation to complainants and operational
Draft recommendations
Draft recommendation 10.1.
The NIIC Act should allow
the AFP or the NIIC to make recommendations or to report
to the ACT Chief Minister and the Legislative
where the complaint or matter relates to ACT policing
(subject to privacy considerations).
Draft recommendation 10.2.
A protocol should be established
for allowing the ACT Chief Minister or ACT Attorney-General
to be advised as soon as practicable
by the AFP or
the NIIC of any complaint about ACT policing that raises
significant public interest for the ACT ( subject to
Draft recommendation 10.3.
Information and analysis
provided annually by the AFP and the NIIC to the
Chief Minister, ACT Attorney-General and the Legislative
10.5 The main issues
in relation to Jervis Bay are lack of community awareness
alternative to lodging complaints with the AFP. Both
the AFP and the Jervis Bay Administration have already
to increase public awareness of the complaints
system by use of pamphlets. The AFP also intends to
give occasional presentations
on the complaints system
to community meetings. The Commission's consultations
in Jervis Bay and the submission from the Department
of the Environment, Sport and Territories (DEST) suggest
that there may be some people who would be reluctant
to lodge a complaint
with the local AFP given the closeness
of the small community. The Commission was told that
people were likely to take the view
that because the
complaint would be investigated by other AFP police
it was not worthwhile jeopardising personal and sometimes
professional relationships by making a complaint. The
physical isolation of the territory from Canberra also
makes it difficult
to lodge a complaint with the Ombudsman
or AFP officers other than those in Jervis Bay.
10.6 The Commission
considers that the NIIC should arrange with DEST for
the Jervis Bay Territory Administration to be a receiving
point for complaints about the AFP. It should be able
to provide information to complainants about the complaints
process and
how to contact the NIIC. It should have
available information leaflets about the complaints
10.7 DEST pointed
out that unlike the AFP arrangements with the external
territories there is no formal agreement or memorandum
of understanding with the AFP in relation to Jervis
Bay. The Commission considers that a formal agreement
would be useful.
10.8 The relationship
between the AFP and the local Aboriginal community
is discussed in chapter 11.
Draft recommendations

Draft recommendation 10.5.

external territories

and consultations have identified the following as

10.10 The Commission's
consultations on Norfolk Island indicate that there
is very little understanding of the existing complaints
system and the role that the Ombudsman plays in the
10.11 Lack of
accessibility is also likely to a significant problem
on the Cocos (Keeling) Islands and Christmas Island.
fact it may be a greater problem on those islands
because of their diverse cultural backgrounds. The
population of Christmas
Island is drawn from Singapore,
Malaysia and the Cocos (Keeling) Islands. There are
three main languages spoken, English, Malay
and Chinese,
with a number of different dialects.
Two of the main islands of the Cocos (Keeling) Islands
are inhabited; West by about 200 Commonwealth and private
sector employees
and their families and Home by about
400 members of the Cocos Malay community.
10.12 Each of
the three external territories is geographically isolated.
None has any direct link with the Commonwealth Ombudsman's
offices. People on these islands may be reluctant to
make a complaint directly to the local AFP because
of the smallness of
the community and the few AFP appointees
on the islands.
They could be concerned that their complaints would
not be fairly dealt with or kept confidential.
10.13 The AFP
has advised that its members in the external territories
have now been requested to initiate education programs
to bring the complaints process to the attention of

for AFP personnel

advised that there is no specific cultural awareness

and it is investigating the cultural awareness and

Translating
and interpreting services
10.15 Cocos (Keeling)
and Christmas Islands have significant numbers of their
populations who are from non-English speaking backgrounds.
Translating and interpreting services are therefore
very important. One concern discussed in the issues
paper was the limited
use of the Translating and Interpreting
Service (TIS) on Christmas Island.
The AFP has submitted that because of legislative
requirements for the recording of interviews it had
not been equipped to use
TIS in the Christmas Island
police station for a period of time. Interviews where
TIS assistance was required were carried out
Administration's office to use its conference telephone
system. Suitable facilities have now been installed
in the police
10.16 The AFP
has advised that where it needs to interview a suspect
or victim and an interpreter is likely to be required
appear in court, the practice on Christmas Island
has been to enlist the services of local certified
interpreters if the interviewee
consents to this procedure.
Local certified interpreters are also engaged by the
Administration and the Shire Council.
10.17 The AFP
has indicated that there are a number of issues that
can arise in relation to translating and interpreting.
example is a situation where the services of a TIS
interpreter speaking a particular Chinese dialect and
the services of a Legal
Aid solicitor could not be
simultaneously obtained. Ultimately a local certified
interpreter was used but approximately eight
had elapsed from the time of the arrest until when
charges were laid.
10.18 The value
of TIS is limited in circumstances where a warrant
is to be executed and an interpreter is required to
the warrant to the person on whose premises
it is to be executed given the detail and complexity
of information contained in
considers that the preferable option is that interpreting
issues arising from search and seizure warrants
addressed by using local certified interpreters to
explain the nature and contents of the warrants and
the procedures involved.
10.19 The AFP
has stated that on Cocos (Keeling) Islands there are
a number of certified interpreters whose services are
by the police in addition to using TIS.
10.20 The Commission
has considered the issue of the use of interpreters
and translators more generally in chapter 11. It proposes
that the AFP and the NCA should try to ensure that
independent and appropriately trained and qualified
interpreters and translators
are used wherever there
is an issue of proper comprehension because of linguistic
reasons. This should especially be the case
interviews and statements.

11. Groups who may have special needs

Introduction
11.1 This chapter
discusses groups in the community who may have special
needs in relation to complaints against the NCA and
the AFP. This includes Aborigines and Torres Strait
Islanders, women, youth, gays and lesbians, people
from non-English speaking
backgrounds and people with
disabilities.
11.2 The first
part of this chapter deals with AFP and NCA personnel
who are from these groups. The second part is concerned
with possible difficulties for members of these groups
external to these organisations.
Part 1 - Internal complaints
EEO policy and grievance systems
11.3 The Office
of the Status of Women (OSW) has submitted that the
Niland report
indicates that there is a degree of hostility within
the AFP towards the AFP's EEO program. The Office suggested
that this hostility
may arise from a misunderstanding
of the differences between EEO, the operation of the
merit system and affirmative action and
also reflect
some conservative aspects of police culture.
11.4 The OSW suggested
that the AFP's response to the Niland report was superficial
in relation to the EEO program and would not
necessarily
result in long term attitudinal change. OSW considers
that education of officers on the productivity and
benefits of an EEO program was essential to effect
these long term changes. The Commission agrees with
11.5 The AFP is
developing a new EEO program for the 1995-1998 period
in conjunction with the consultancy firm Carmel Niland
and Associates. The AFP has appointed a Workforce Equity
and Diversity Specialist who would be responsible for
implementing effective equity and diversity strategies
within the broader AFP organisational framework, including
through the
AFP recruitment strategy
developing and managing implementation processes and
procedures including awareness training programs
monitoring and evaluating outcomes to ensure that best
practice outcomes are achieved and maintained.
11.6 The AFP has
submitted that it is developing new 'best practice'
conflict resolution guidelines to maximise managerial
to handling complaints of harassment and
discrimination.
11.7 The Commission
notes that the AFP is making a concerted effort to
improve its internal culture and to promote an effective
EEO program in response to the Niland report. The Commission
believes that the AFP as it continues to develop its
program should
further consult with bodies with expertise
and experience such as the Office of the Status of
Women, ATSIC, the Department of
Immigration and Ethnic
Affairs and others concerned with equal opportunity
employment.
Draft recommendation
AFP's EEO policy and grievance systems
Draft recommendation 11.1.
Education of officers on
the benefits of an EEO program is essential to effect
the desired long term changes in AFP culture and
promote EEO. The AFP should consider consulting with
bodies with relevant expertise and experience such
as the Office of
the Status of Women as it continues
to develop its EEO program and grievance procedures.
EEO policies and grievance procedures
11.8 The NCA's
EEO policy and program are discussed in its most recent
Annual Report.
The Commission has received no information on the
NCA's EEO or access and equity strategies in response
to the questions asked
in the issues paper. The Commission
again invites comments on the effectiveness of the
current policies and any suggested changes.
The Commission invites comments on the effectiveness
of the NCA's current EEO program and its access and
strategy policies and
any suggested changes.
Part 2 - External complaints
and Torres Strait Islanders
11.9 The Ombudsman
submitted that in the past Aboriginal and Torres Strait
Islander communities have had little trust in the AFP
complaints system but recently there has been some
increase in complaints being lodged by members of indigenous
communities.
The Ombudsman thought it essential that
her office play a much greater role in supervising
or directly investigating complaints
as members of
indigenous communities were more likely to have faith
in the system if they were confident that their complaint
would be independently supervised or independently
investigated.
11.10 In the AFP
's view the relationship between the Aboriginal community
in the ACT and the AFP is good with AFP management
regularly liaising with Aboriginal leaders. AFP police
members are undergoing a second round of cultural awareness
Aboriginal cross-cultural awareness training
is provided by consultants
who aim to explore cultural awareness and encourage
non-racist attitudes. Members discuss information about
Aboriginal and Torres
Strait Islander culture and are
asked to explore attitudes and behaviours towards those
cultures and to reflect on their own
attitudes and
the formation of those attitudes.
11.11 The AFP
advised that the Wreck Bay Aboriginal Community in
Jervis Bay and those AFP members stationed at Jervis
a good working relationship. Meetings of
the officer in charge and the Wreck Bay Aboriginal
Community Council (WBACC) take place
on an informal
basis. The WBACC has indicated that it does not wish
for a more formal liaison committee at this stage.
11.12 The Commission
held meetings in Jervis Bay and invited comments from
members of the community. The consultations indicate
that there are no special difficulties in the relationship
between the AFP and the Aboriginal community. The Commission
understands
that the AFP is considering appointing
an Aboriginal liaison officer for the area. This would
be a useful measure. The Wreck
Bay community should
be consulted about the position and the necessary background
and skills of the person. The AFP should continue
ensure that any AFP officer who is to work in Jervis
Bay has undergone the cross cultural awareness training
and can demonstrate
an understanding of its principles
and a capacity to act accordingly. The AFP should also
consider the appointment of Aboriginal
officers in
the area given the significant Aboriginal population.
That decision would also need to take into account
suitability
and operational requirements.
representation
11.13 The Royal
Commission into Aboriginal Deaths in Custody recommended
that in the adjudication of complaints made by or on
behalf of Aboriginal persons one member of the review
or adjudication panel should be an Aboriginal person
nominated by an
appropriate Aboriginal organisation(s)
in the State or Territory in which the complaint arose.
It also suggested that the panel
should also contain
a person nominated by the Police Union or similar body.
11.14 The Ombudsman
has suggested that this recommendation would create
a number of significant difficulties. There may be
considerable
problems in finding an Aboriginal person
acceptable to a wide cross section of the indigenous
communities. There would also be
a likelihood of polarisation
between the Aboriginal representative and the AFP Union
representative leading to the body being
unproductive.
Such an approach might also alienate other members
of the community who might question why one group apparently
receives special treatment.
11.15 The Commission
considers that it is preferable to develop a clearly
independent complaints body and to ensure that decision
makers on that body have effective cross cultural awareness
training. The NIIC as the proposed primary investigatory
body should
develop its own awareness programs and
strategies particularly for those who deal with complaints.
The NIIC should also employ
an Aboriginal and Torres
Strait Islander liaison and support officer to assist,
where appropriate, Aboriginal or Torres Strait
complainants. The officer should help to publicise
the complaints system amongst Aboriginal and Torres
Strait Islander
communities. In chapter 6 the Commission
proposed that the AAT should review disciplinary decisions
made by the AFP and the NCA.
The AAT has developed
cross-cultural awareness training and access and equity
strategies.
legal issues facilitators
11.16 There is
a community legal issues facilitator project running
in Nova Scotia, Canada which provides a service of
resources and support to the local Aboriginal
community and the criminal justice agencies in Nova
Scotia. The facilitators work
in conjunction with Aboriginal
clients and criminal justice agencies to create a good
working relationship and mutual understanding.
case of a complaint against the police a facilitator
usually makes contact with the Aboriginal client and
filing a complaint with the Complaints Division
of the Police. The facilitator may also accompany the
client to file the complaint
and be present when interviewing
occurs. The final police investigative report is usually
given to the facilitator to explain
to the complainant
the outcome and if necessary give advice and assistance
in relation to the appeal process. The Commission
comment on whether a similar scheme should be trialled
in Australia.

Complaints by Aboriginals and Torres Strait Islanders
Draft recommendation 11.2.
Decision-makers in the AFP
and NCA complaints and disciplinary systems should
have effective cross-cultural awareness training.
NIIC as the proposed primary external agency should
also develop its own cross-cultural programs and strategies,
particularly
for those who deal with complaints.

The NIIC should have an
Aboriginal or Torres Strait Islander liaison and support
officer in relation to assisting, where appropriate,
Aboriginal or Torres Strait Islander complainants.
The officer should also assist in helping to publicise
the programs amongst
Aboriginal and Torres Strait Islander
communities.
Draft recommendation 11.4.
The NIIC should establish
links with relevant bodies concerned with Aboriginal
and Torres Strait Islander interests.
from non-English speaking backgrounds
11.17 The National
Police Ethnic Advisory Bureau submitted that the existing
legislative provisions in relation to interpreters
are flawed in that they place an onus on investigating
officials to determine whether a person can communicate
fluently in
English. The Bureau argues that the issue
should not be whether the person is fluent in English
but should be the right of all
people to be understood
and to understand the nature of any interaction with
police. The Bureau believes that this can be achieved
if the AFP and the NCA strive to ensure that an independent,
professionally trained and if possible accredited interpreter/translator
will be engaged in circumstances involving police interaction
with people from ethnic communities requiring interpreting/translating
assistance.
11.18 The Bureau's
view is that the AFP and NCA should engage on site
interpreters or interpreters by means of the telephone
using the Translating and Interpreting Service (TIS)
in the following circumstances:
if the client makes a request for an interpreter/translator
if a police member deems it necessary to engage the
services of an interpreter/translator.
11.19 The Bureau
stated that while it supported the use of in-house
linguistic skills in general interaction with people
non-English speaking backgrounds it was inappropriate
and undesirable to use in-house linguistic skills in
criminal investigations
or other matters likely to
result in court proceedings. The Department of Immigration
and Ethnic Affairs also submitted that
friends or relatives
or witnesses to an incident should not be used as interpreters
for any official interview or provision of
statements.
11.20 The Bureau
also argued that it was vital to ensure that people
who came to Australia understood the role and services
of police in Australia. By decreasing their fear of
police, they would be more likely to participate in
police/community oriented
programs and to make complaints
delivery strategies should include ethnic
electronic media and talks at ethnic community venues
including Migrant Resource Centres and Adult Migrant
Centres. The Bureau also suggested that complainants
should regularly be kept informed of the progress of
investigations either
in writing or orally and that
preferably this should be done in the complainant's
Draft recommendations
People from non-English speaking backgrounds
Draft recommendation 11.5.
The AFP and the NCA should
try to ensure that independent and appropriately trained
and qualified interpreters and translators
wherever there is an issue of proper comprehension
because of linguistic reasons. This should be especially
for formal interviews and statements.
Draft recommendation 11.6.
Information delivery strategies
should include ethnic print and electronic media and
talks at ethnic community venues. Complainants
regularly be informed of the progress of investigations
and outcomes either in writing or orally. Preferably
this should
be done in the complainant's language.
prejudiced behaviour
11.21 Some submissions
addressed the question raised in the issues paper of
whether there should be a disciplinary offence of
prejudiced behaviour. The Attorney-General's Department
suggested that racially prejudiced behaviour was already
by regulation 18 of the existing discipline
regulations, but that the inclusion of such a specific
offence might have merit in
that it would have educative
value. Others who thought the offence might have educative
value were the Ombudsman, the Department
of Immigration
and Ethnic Affairs and the National Police Ethnic Advisory
11.22 The Commission
does not support the introduction of a specific new
proceeding against racially prejudiced behaviour. While
such a proceeding might have some educative value,
the key task is to improve internal culture and attitudes
so that improper
behaviour does not occur in the first
place. There is already a widely held view that the
disciplinary system is overly regulated
and complex
and that new proceedings will only add to that concern.
For those cases where disciplinary action is appropriate
then there is scope under the current proceedings to
take action.
Draft recommendation
Racially prejudiced behaviour
Draft recommendation 11.7.
There should be no specific
proceeding against racially prejudiced behaviour.
Gays and lesbians
11.23 The ACT
Gay & Lesbian Anti-Violence Project (the Project)
and the AFP are currently working together to reduce
amongst the community in accessing police services.
There are two gay and lesbian liaison officers and
community workshops have
been conducted by the AFP
addressing such issues as reporting processes, client
rights and delivery of service complaints about
AFP. There is also a public relations media campaign
and regular liaison with leaders in the gay and lesbian
11.24 The Project
believes that the introduction of the gay and lesbian
liaison officers will encourage lesbians and gays to
utilise police services including the complaints system.
11.25 The Ombudsman
has submitted that there have been several complaints
over the past few years which indicated antipathy towards
gays by AFP officers and at least one substantiated
case of abuse of a gay person by an AFP officer. The
Ombudsman argued that
greater supervision and independent
investigation in serious cases and a change in the
attitude of AFP appointees when dealing
with gays and
lesbians would encourage greater use of the system.
Training and other measures directed towards promoting
of others should be instituted within the
11.26 The Beat
Outreach Project Co-ordinator of the ACT Aids Action
Council (the Council) submitted that while the AFP
public duty to act on complaints concerning 'beats',
anecdotal reports had indicated that in the course
of these duties officers had sometimes used extreme
methods of investigation
such as spotlighting of individuals
in the vicinity of beats. The Council advised that
in September 1995 the NSW Police Commissioner
a circular to all officers in regard to policing beats,
stipulating police protocol and conduct in matters
covert operations, entrapment and patrol commanders'
responsibilities. The Council suggested that a formal
police policy regarding
the policing of beats in the
ACT could lead to an improvement for beat users in
their relations with police and a more co-operative
atmosphere for outreach activities. It suggested that
a beat policy should ensure that the first priority
of beat policing
should be preventing violence and
that plain-clothed police should only be deployed at
beats as a last resort.
Draft recommendations
Gays and lesbians
Draft recommendation 11.8.
The AFP should develop a
formal policy for the policing of beats and should
consider the policy developed by the NSW Police Service.
Draft recommendation 11.9.
The AFP should provide training
to all members and staff members to encourage attitudes
of acceptance of gays and lesbians within
the workplace.
Draft recommendation 11.10.
The AFP's policies and
performance on these issues should be monitored and
examined in consultation with relevant interest groups.
11.27 A major
issue for women in relation to complaints against the
police arises in relation to domestic violence cases.
AFP submitted that in the ACT region, police respond
to incidents of domestic violence as a matter of priority
as these disturbances
are considered to be potentially
life threatening situations. The primary role of police
upon attendance is to prevent violence
and to defuse
hostility. The AFP regularly liaise with the ACT Domestic
Violence Crisis Centre and is developing an AFP strategy
on domestic violence in the ACT. The Police Regional
Instructions in relation to handling domestic violence
cases are also
under review.
11.28 The Office
of the Status of Women has submitted that police need
to be clear about their law enforcement role and not
to confuse this with a conciliator role which might
minimise the seriousness of the violence and may jeopardise
the safety of
the victim. The Office believes that
training should be given to all new recruits and refresher
training to all officers at appropriate
The content of that training should be consistent with
the guidelines of the National Committee on Violence
Women and the National Strategy on Violence
Against Women. The Office also submitted that a specialist
domestic violence unit
is a way of ensuring a consistent
approach through policy and training and monitoring
police practice. Police should have clear
on charging and on their powers of arrest where a criminal
offence has occurred.
11.29 Similar
recommendations have been made in the ACT Community
Law Reform Committee's recent report into domestic
The Committee stated that many submissions
to it criticised police for failing to charge in domestic
violence cases where the
police had reasonable grounds
to believe that a criminal offence had occurred. In
response to these submissions the Committee
number of recommendations including
police regional instructions should direct police to
charge where police have reasonable grounds to believe
that a criminal offence
has occurred in the context
of domestic violence
the regional instructions should specify that the practice
of referring the victim to a service to obtain a domestic
protection order or a restraining order as
a substitute for charging is unsatisfactory and unprofessional.
The Committee also recommended that the decision about
whether to charge should be made by the police and
not by the victim. Both
victim and offender should
be told that the police will decide whether to charge.
11.30 The Committee
agreed with the AFP that it would be impractical to
create a domestic violence unit to deal with all domestic
violence cases because domestic violence constitutes
such a large proportion of police work in the ACT.
However, the Committee
recommended that there should
be a specialist domestic violence unit to:
drive and monitor the implementation by police of the
ACT Domestic Violence Strategy
provide support and advice to other police on domestic
violence matters
monitor the quality of domestic violence response by
liaise with external agencies and victims
undertake police domestic violence training
undertake law reform activity
co-ordinate police community education initiatives.
Other recommendations made by the Committee included
the need for a Domestic Violence Liaison Officer within
the Unit, the need
for new instructions on exercising
the power to arrest and to grant bail, and on the timing
and content of training for police
on domestic violence.

11.33 Complaints
arising from alleged sexual assault is another issue

is isolated from the rest of the police environment

11.34 The Coalition
Against Police Violence has submitted that unwarranted
strip searches conducted by the police, constitute
sexual assaults and that it is important for police
complaints systems to treat the use of unwarranted
strip searches as a
serious matter. The Third National
Conference on Women's Health held in Canberra and the
Taking Legal Action Against Sexual Assault
held in Melbourne
have endorsed the view that strip searches are a sexual
assault. Currently the legislation covering searches
at the federal level
is found in the
Crimes Act 1914
(Cth) as amended by the
Crimes (Search Warrants and
Powers of Arrest ) Amendment Act 1994
(Cth). The legislation
defines the conditions under which strip searches may
be conducted and the Commission seeks comment on
failure by police to abide by these conditions.
The Commission seeks comments on the AFP response in
relation to complaints arising from domestic violence
and sexual assault.
It also seeks comments on compliance
with the conditions for strip searches conducted by
the AFP under the
Crimes Act 1914
11.35 The issues
paper asked questions about the relationship between
the AFP and youth. Very few submissions addressed these
matters. The Children's Interests Bureau (SA) submitted
that children affected by disputed orders of the Family
Court deserved
special attention and sensitivity in
the way that volatile situations are managed. The Bureau

11.36 The Commission
in conjunction with the Human Rights and Equal Opportunity
Commission is currently conducting an inquiry
children and the legal process. As part of this inquiry
the Commissions have held consultations in Canberra
and conducted
youth focus groups. Comments made in
the ACT focus groups support the suggestion in the
issues paper that there are problems
in the relationship
between youth and the AFP in the ACT. Examples of the
complaints made at the focus groups were that:
Children, particularly teenagers, are regularly hassled
by the police. Police are often very aggressive towards
them and abuse
their power. Young people are stereotyped
by the police as trouble makers.
The appearance of youth is very important in young
people's interaction with the AFP - for example, skateboard
riders and those
who wear distinctive clothes such
as baseball caps turned backwards are common targets
of intimidation because of their appearance.
The attitude of young people is also important - for
example, they will get into more trouble if they are
seen as rude by the
police. There is the occasional
Police don't give kids a fair go in interviews. Kids
allege they have been bashed while in custody but cops
always stick together
so there is no point in complaining
Police don't caution young people enough.
The Commission considers that these comments and, in
particular, the frequency with which they are expressed,
are a matter for
concern. The Commission intends to
give further consideration to these issues in the context
of its inquiry into children and
the legal process.
However, for the purposes of this inquiry, areas of
concern are the perceptions of AFP officers about youth
and the distrust that youth have in the police and
the complaints system. The existence of these perceptions
means that the
NIIC should be particularly sensitive
to the requirements of youth in a complaints system.
The Commission seeks further comments
